Page  5
THE 
PEOPLE’S 
REPUBLIC 
OF 
BANGLADESH 
DEVELOPMENT 
SUPPORT 
CREDIT 
Credit 
and 
Program 
Summary 
Borrower: 
The 
People’s 
Republic 
of 
Bangladesh 
Amount: 
SDR 
217.6 
million 
(US$300 
million 
equivalent) 
Terms: 
Standard 
IDA 
terms; 
40-year 
maturity 
with 
10-year grace 
period 
Description: 
The 
proposed 
credit 
supports  the 
implementation 
of 
the 
Govemment’s 
poverty 
reduction 
strategy, 
which 
focuses on: 
. 
Acceleration 
of 
pro-poor 
growth. 
The 
I-PRSP aims 
to 
raise 
the 
economy’s 
growth 
rate 
from 
about 
5 
percent 
per 
annum 
to 
6-7 
percent, 
in 
order 
to 
attain 
the 
MDG 
poverty reduction 
target 
set 
for 
2015. 
This 
is 
to 
be 
accomplished 
by 
(a) 
ensuring a 
stable macroeconomic environment; 
(b) 
relying 
on 
the 
private 
sector 
as 
the 
engine 
of 
growth; 
(c) 
establishing a 
sound 
and 
well-functioning 
financial 
system 
and ensuring 
that  the 
poor 
have 
access 
to 
financial 
resources; 
(d) 
talung 
advantage 
of 
the 
opportunities 
provided 
by 
globalization, 
(e) 
developing 
rural 
areas 
and 
accelerating 
rural 
non-farm 
growth; 
(f) 
creating 
the 
conditions 
necessary 
for 
an 
expanding and 
dynamic 
manufacturing 
sector; 
and 
(g) 
substantially  augmenting 
the 
capacity  and 
quality 
of 
infrastructure 
services. 
. 
Promoting 
Good Governance. 
The 
Govemment’s 
strategy 
of 
improving 
governance 
focuses 
on 
addressing three 
major 
areas: 
(i) 
improving 
the 
investment 
climate 
by 
creating a 
competitive 
environment 
across 
all 
segments 
in 
society; 
(ii) 
establishing 
and 
enforcing 
clear 
rules 
and 
regulations 
for 
public 
sector 
administration;  and 
(iii) 
promoting 
voice and participation 
of 
the 
civil 
society, 
including 
women, 
the 
poor 
and 
disadvantaged 
groups, 
as 
a 
step 
toward 
a 
transparent 
and 
‘open’ government. 
This 
is 
to 
be 
done 
through 
targeted 
public 
expenditures 
in 
health,  education, 
and nutrition, 
and 
radically improved 
system 
of 
incentives 
for public 
service 
providers. 
Within 
the 
health 
sector, 
although 
some 
success 
has 
been 
achieved 
in 
preventive health 
care, 
a 
small 
proportion 
of 
poor 
people have 
access 
to 
public health 
care services. 
The 
strategy 
thus 
gives 
priority 
to 
completing 
the 
unfinished 
agenda 
and 
addressing 
the pro-poor 
concerns 
in 
health 
and 
nutrition. 
The 
introduction 
of 
a national 
nutrition 
program 
to 
address 
the 
malnutrition 
of 
chldren 
under 
two 
as 
well 
as 
pregnant and 
lactating 
mothers 
through 
the 
provision 
of 
food 
supplements, 
nutrition 
and 
health 
counseling 
is 
a positive 
step 
to 
address 
the 
problem. 
In 
the 
area 
of 
basic 
education, 
the 
plan 
is 
to 
improve 
quality, 
whle 
increasing 
access 
for 
the poorest 
segments 
of 
society. 
NGOs 
and 
private 
sector 
are 
envisaged 
as 
providers 
of 
high-quality 
education 
along 
with 
the 
government. 
. 
Promoting 
Women’s advancement. 
%le 
the 
gender-gap 
is 
closing 
in 
Bangladesh 
for most 
social indicators, 
the 
overall 
level 
of 
empowerment 
measured 
in 
terms 
of 
literacy, 
work 
force participation, 
property 
rights, and 
credit 
access, 
remains 
low. 
The 
strategy 
for 
furthering 
women’s 
advancement 
includes 
emphasis 
on 
policies 
and 
institutional 
actions 
to 
combat 
continuing 
negative 
gender 
ratios,  violence 
against 
women, 
hgh 
maternal 
mortality, 
and  restrictions 
on 
women’s 
employment  and 
economic opportunities; 
and 
policies 
to 
ensure 
formal 
equality; supporting 
affirmative 
actions at 
all 
levels 
and 
in 
all 
spheres; 
and creating 
a women-friendly  institutional 
environment overall. 
. 
Investing 
in 
Human 
Development. 
Page  6
Ensuring 
Social 
Protection. 
To 
help 
increase the 
poor’s 
crisis-coping capability 
and 
form 
important 
risk-insurance 
policies 
for 
them, 
four 
sets 
of 
policies 
would 
be 
emphasized 
for 
the 
poor 
to 
cope 
better 
with 
various 
income 
(consumption) 
shocks. 
The 
first 
set 
of 
policies 
will 
continue 
focus 
on 
social 
safety 
nets; 
the 
second 
on 
policies 
that 
will 
address 
the 
vulnerabilities 
of 
the 
‘new 
poor’; 
the 
third 
will 
emphasize 
the 
development 
of 
social 
solidarity; 
and 
the 
fourth 
set 
concerns 
risk 
insurance, 
including 
measures 
to mitigate 
risks 
faced 
by 
the 
poor. 
The 
actions supported 
by 
the 
credit 
will 
increase transparency 
and accountability 
in 
government, 
help 
restore 
public 
and 
private 
sector 
confidence,  and 
improve 
the 
investment climate. 
This, 
in 
turn, 
will 
contribute 
to 
accelerating the 
rate 
of 
economic 
growth,  employment creation and 
poverty 
reduction. 
Governance 
improvements 
in 
the 
social 
sectors, 
in 
particular, 
will 
help 
improve 
the 
quality 
of 
service 
delivery 
and 
make the 
services 
more 
affordable and 
accessible 
to 
the 
poor. 
The 
program 
entails 
significant 
risks. 
First, 
implementation 
may 
be 
slower  than 
planned 
because 
of 
limited 
experience, inadequate 
institutional 
and 
human 
capacity, 
and 
uneven 
support 
for reform 
within 
the 
Government. 
Second, 
given 
the 
powerful 
vested  interests 
who 
stand 
to 
loose 
from 
improvements 
in 
governance, 
existing 
political 
support 
for 
the 
medium-term 
reform 
program 
could 
erode 
if 
the 
distribution 
of 
gains 
from 
growth 
are 
not 
sufficiently 
equitable. 
Third, 
the 
reform 
costs 
to 
the 
budget 
could 
be 
higher 
than 
estimated 
if 
banlung 
and 
SOE 
reforms 
proceed 
at 
a 
slower 
pace. 
Fourth, 
fiduciary 
risks 
in 
Bangladesh 
are 
high 
as 
confirmed 
by 
various 
diagnostic 
studies 
done 
by 
the 
Bank 
and 
the 
Fund. 
Finally, 
there 
is 
a 
danger 
that 
the 
external  environment 
could  remain 
depressed 
for 
an 
extended 
period 
of 
time, 
preventing 
an  acceleration 
in 
the 
rate 
of 
export 
and 
economic 
growth. 
Several 
specific 
measures are 
being 
implemented 
to 
minimize 
these 
risks. 
The 
reform 
effort 
is 
being 
led 
by 
the 
Minister 
of 
Finance and 
Planning, 
who 
has 
an 
established 
track- 
record 
and 
was 
instrumental 
in 
ushering 
in 
significant reforms 
in 
the 
early 
1990s. 
He 
is 
being 
supported 
by 
a group 
of 
reform-minded 
leaders 
who 
are 
endeavoring 
to 
build 
constituencies 
for 
change 
within 
the 
political 
leadership. 
The 
Government 
has 
already 
implemented  significant 
up-front 
actions, 
laying 
the 
foundation 
for 
subsequent 
reforms. 
The 
Government 
has engaged 
in 
substantive 
dialogue 
with 
labor 
leaders 
and 
workers 
to 
ensure 
that 
their 
concerns 
are 
taken 
into 
account 
in 
the 
design 
of 
the 
retrenchment program. 
Bangladesh 
enjoys considerable 
goodwill 
among 
the 
donor  community, 
which 
has 
indicated 
that 
their 
support 
to 
the 
country 
would 
increase 
if 
the 
reform 
effort 
is 
sustained. 
To 
mitigate 
against 
fiduciary 
risk, 
the 
Government 
has 
agreed 
to 
implement 
a  number 
of 
reform 
measures 
under 
the 
Financial 
Management 
Reform 
Program 
funded 
by 
the 
DFID-UK 
and Government 
of 
Netherlands 
and also 
the 
Bank 
funded 
Procurement 
Reform 
TA 
Project. 
Additional 
support 
to 
improve 
the 
fiduciary 
environment 
will 
be 
provided 
by 
the 
IMF 
and 
by 
the 
Bank’s 
Central 
Bank 
Strengthening 
Project 
and 
IDF 
grant. 
The 
Bank 
will 
also 
reserve 
the 
right 
to 
audit 
the  deposit 
accounts. 
While 
the  above 
risk 
mitigation 
measures 
should 
provide 
adequate 
comfort 
that 
these 
risks 
are manageable, 
as 
an 
added 
risk-mitigation 
measure, 
the 
Bank 
will 
provide 
a 
credit 
acceleration 
clause that 
would  allow 
the 
Bank 
to 
demand 
immediate 
repayment 
of 
the 
proposed  credit 
amount 
by 
the 
Borrower 
in 
case 
if 
reforms 
are 
reversed 
or materially 
changed. 
Benefits: 
Risks 
and 
Mitigation 
Strategy: 
Estimated 
Disbursement: 
SDR 
217.6 
million 
disbursed 
in 
a single 
tranche 
upon 
credit 
effectiveness.

ID 
Number:

BOXES 
Box 
1 
: 
Governance 
Related Commitments 
made

IDA 
PROGRAM 
DOCUMENT 
FOR 
A 
PROPOSED 
DEVELOPMENT 
SUPPORT 
CREDIT 
TO 
THE 
PEOPLE’S 
REPUBLIC OF 
BANGLADESH 
I. 
Introduction 
1. 
Although 
Bangladesh’s 
longer-term 
achievements 
are 
noteworthy, 
the 
economy 
has 
continued 
to 
perform below 
its 
true 
potential. 
While 
the economy 
moved 
to 
a 
higher 
growth 
trajectory 
of 
5 
percent 
per 
annum 
during 
the 
1990s, 
compared 
to 
the 
4 
percent annual 
growth 
achieved 
in 
the 
1980s, 
this 
is 
still 
below 
the 
potential 
6-7 
percent 
growth 
rate 
needed 
to 
achieve 
the 
country’s 
2015 
poverty 
reduction 
goal 
of 
halving 
the 
incidence 
of 
poverty 
from 
current 
levels.’ 
Limited 
structural 
reforms 
have  been 
implemented 
since  the 
mid-1990s’ 
and 
most 
observers 
believe 
that 
economic 
growth 
and 
poverty 
reduction 
could 
accelerate 
if 
the 
country 
were 
to 
undertake 
significant  structural 
reforms 
in 
the 
areas 
of 
governance, 
state 
owned 
enterprises, 
financial 
sector 
and 
infrastructure. 
Against 
this 
general backdrop, 
a 
new 
Government 
with 
a  strong  parliamentary 
majority 
was 
elected 
in 
October 
2001, 
with 
an 
overwhelming 
mandate 
to 
improve 
governance 
and 
law 
and 
order 
and 
to 
strengthen  economic 
management. 
The 
Government 
has 
taken 
quick 
actions 
to 
stabilize 
the 
macroeconomic 
environment, 
is 
implementing 
structural 
reforms 
to 
improve 
the 
legal 
and 
regulatory 
framework 
in 
crucial 
sectors 
such 
as 
banlung, 
energy, 
and  telecommunications,  and 
is 
making 
encouraging  progress 
in 
implementing 
a 
medium-term 
program 
to 
improve 
governance. 
These 
actions 
are 
critical 
and  an  integral 
part 
of 
government’s strategy 
for 
accelerating 
growth 
and 
reducing poverty. 
2. 
Government’s 
reform 
efforts 
are 
being 
championed 
by 
the 
Minister 
of 
Finance 
and 
Planning, 
who 
established 
a 
record 
for 
implementing 
structural 
reforms 
as 
Finance 
Minister 
during 
the 
early 
1990s 
and 
is 
supported 
and 
encouraged 
by 
the 
Prime 
Minister. 
These 
reforms 
are 
also 
backed 
up 
by 
a team 
of 
reform- 
minded 
political 
leaders 
and 
bureaucrats. 
Immediately 
after 
tahng 
office 
the 
Govemment 
formed 
a 
committee 
of 
five 
Secretaries and embarked 
upon 
a 
100-day 
program 
to 
address 
the 
most 
urgent 
issues 
facing 
the 
country. 
On 
the 
basis 
of 
the 
report 
of 
the 
Secretaries’ 
committee, the 
Government 
developed 
a 
time-bound 
implementation 
plan 
for 
future reform. 
The 
reform 
actions 
were 
discussed 
and 
endorsed 
by 
the Cabinet 
Committee 
on 
Economic Affairs, 
the 
country’s 
main 
economic 
decision-making 
body,  and 
implementation 
began 
shortly 
thereafter. 
The 
previously moribund 
process 
of 
preparing 
the PRSP 
was 
simultaneously 
accelerated, 
and 
an 
I-PRSP 
has 
been 
prepared. 
The 
Minister 
of 
Finance and 
Planning 
and 
his 
economic team 
visited 
Washington 
in 
December 
2002, 
and 
discussed 
their 
adjustment 
efforts 
with 
senior 
Bank-Fund 
management. 
3. 
To 
support the 
implementation 
of 
the 
I-PRSP, 
the Government 
has 
asked 
the 
IMF 
and 
the 
World 
Bank for 
financial 
support. 
In 
response, 
the 
IMF 
is 
working 
on 
a PRGF. 
The 
World 
Bank 
is 
considering 
a 
series 
of 
single 
tranche adjustment operations 
within 
a 
medium-term 
policy 
framework 
to 
complement 
the 
IMF’s 
PRGF. 
This 
memorandum 
outlines 
the 
first 
of 
the 
envisaged  adjustment  operations, 
a 
Development 
Support 
Credit 
(DSC), 
to 
the 
Government 
of 
Bangladesh 
to 
help 
support  broad-based 
structural and 
governance 
reforms. 
The 
Credit 
is 
consistent 
with 
the 
objectives and 
strategy 
proposed 
by 
the 
Govemment 
in 
its 
I-PRSP 
and 
conforms 
to 
the 
Country 
Assistance Strategy 
(Report 
No: 
21326-BD, 
February 8,200 
l), 
which 
emphasizes 
strengthening 
govemance, 
sustaining 
human 
development 
gains 
and 
accelerating 
the 
pace 
of 
poverty 
reduction. 
It 
supports 
a 
subset 
of 
the 
Government’s 
reform 
agenda 
and 
will 
provide quick 
disbursing 
balance 
of 
payments 
assistance, 
complementing 
other 
initiatives 
supported 
by 
the 
Bank 
that 
are 
designed 
to 
contribute 
to 
the Government’s 
poverty reduction 
objectives. 
These 
include 
a social 
investment 
program, 
improvements 
in 
rural 
transport, 
reforms 
in 
the education and 
health 
sectors 
and 
assistance 
to 
strengthen 
the 
telecommunications 
sector 
and 
the 
central 
bank. 
Poveriy 
in 
Bangladesh: 
Building 
on 
Progress, 
joint 
poverty 
assessment 
by 
the 
World 
Bank 
and 
Asian 
Development 
Bank, 
December 
2002.

4. 
Section 
11 
discusses 
longer-term 
development 
outcomes, 
while 
Section 
I11 
provides a 
brief 
overview 
of 
recent 
economic 
developments. 
This 
is 
followed 
in 
Section 
IV 
by 
a 
summary 
of 
the 
medium-term poverty reduction 
strategy presented 
in 
the 
Government’s I-PRSP.* 
Section 
V 
discusses 
the 
first 
phase 
of 
reforms 
implemented 
by 
Government, 
including 
a 
summary 
of 
the 
Government’s 
revised 
macroeconomic 
framework. 
Section 
VI 
presents an 
overview 
of 
the 
Bank’s 
country 
assistance 
strategy 
for 
Bangladesh, 
while 
Section 
VI1 
concludes 
with 
an 
assessment 
of 
the 
benefits 
and 
risks 
of 
the 
operation, 
along 
with 
a 
discussion 
of 
fiduciary 
issues 
and 
poverty 
monitoring 
systems 
to 
assess 
social impact. 
The 
Government’s 
Letter 
of 
Development 
Policy 
is 
contained 
in 
Annex 
A, 
while 
the 
medium-term 
policy 
matrix 
is 
summarized 
in 
Annex 
B. 
Annex 
C 
outlines 
the 
medium-term 
macroeconomic 
framework 
underlying 
the 
proposed 
PRGF. 
The 
implementation 
of 
the 
medium-term 
macroeconomic framework 
is 
also 
a performance 
criterion 
for 
the 
Development Support 
Credit 
and 
future 
adjustment operations. 
Annex 
D 
presents 
the 
outcome 
indicators 
that 
the 
Govemment 
intends 
to 
monitor 
during 
the PRSP 
period. 
Annex 
E 
summarizes Bangladesh’s 
PRSP 
targets 
for 
2015. 
Annexes 
F 
and 
G 
presents 
key 
issues 
in 
Human 
and 
Rural 
Development. 
Statistical 
data 
is 
summarized 
in 
Annex 
H. 
The  remainder 
of 
this 
document 
is 
organized 
as 
follows. 
11. 
Development 
Performance 
Long-term 
Achievements 
5. 
As 
highlighted 
in 
the 
most 
recent 
Country 
Assistance 
Strategy, 
Bangladesh’s 
development 
is 
a 
“mosaic 
of 
achievements 
and 
disappointments”. 
The 
country’s 
achievements 
are 
many, 
but 
so 
are the 
signs 
of 
performance 
falling 
far 
short 
of 
potential. 
Thanks 
to 
prudent 
macroeconomic 
management 
and 
generally 
sound 
policies 
for 
much 
of 
the 
past 
decade, 
indicators 
of 
economic 
and 
social 
well-being 
have 
improved 
steadily. 
Rapid 
integration 
with 
the 
global 
economy 
saw 
Bangladesh’s 
ratio 
of 
trade 
to GDP 
more 
than 
doubled over 
the 
1990s, 
contributing 
to 
real 
per 
capita 
income 
growing 
by 
about 
3 
percent 
per 
annum, 
exceeding 
the 
average 
for 
low 
and 
middle 
income 
countries 
(Figure 
l).3 
Economic 
growth 
was 
most 
robust 
Figure 
1. 
GDP 
per 
capita growth,

6. 
Bangladesh implemented a 
successful 
family 
planning 
program 
using 
voluntary 
methods only, 
that 
resulted 
in 
the 
population 
growth 
rate 
falling 
from 
over 
3 
percent 
in 
the 
1970s 
to 
1.5 
percent 
in 
the 
late 
1990s. 
The 
total 
fertility 
rate declined 
from 
4.3 
in 
1990 
to 
3 
in 
1998. 
Impressive 
gains were 
made 
in 
increasing 
the 
child 
immunization 
rate 
from 
10 
to 
70 
percent 
during 
the 
1990s. 
In 
education, 
enrollments 
at 
the 
primary 
level 
doubled 
from 
8 
to 
17.7 
million 
between 
1980 
and 
2001,  and  coverage  increased 
from 
65 
percent 
to 
84 
percent 
of 
the 
age 
group. 
The 
primary 
school 
completion 
rate 
increased 
to 
67 
Dercent. 
and 
the 
rate 
of 
transition 
to

data 
are 
for 
1999. 
The poverty 
headcount 
rate 
refers 
to 
the 
upper 
poverty line. 
secoidary 
education also increased 
significantly. 
Enrollment 
in 
secondary 
education 
increased 
from 
3 
million 
in 
1990 
to 
over 
10 
million 
in 
1999. 
Female enrollment 
increased 
from 
35 
percent 
to 
54 percent at 
the 
lower 
secondary 
level 
and 
from 31 
percent 
to 
49 
percent 
in 
grades 
M 
and 
X. 
Similarly, 
life 
expectancy  and 
infant  mortality  improved 
appreciably 
(Table 
1). 
Bangladesh  has 
continuously 
strengthened 
its 
disaster 
management 
capabilities. 
This 
enhanced 
capability, 
as 
well 
as 
people’s 
resilience, 
was 
in 
ample evidence 
during 
the 
country’s effective 
response 
to 
one 
of 
the 
century’s 
worst 
floods 
in 
1998. 
7. 
Headcount 
rates, 
based 
on both 
the 
upper 
as 
well 
as 
lower poverty 
lines, 
indicate that 
poverty 
in 
Bangladesh declined 
considerably 
during 
the 
1990s. 
Analysis 
of 
three 
Household 
Expenditure 
Surveys 
(HES) 
conducted 
by 
the 
Bangladesh 
Bureau 
of 
Statistics 
(1991-92,  1995-96, 
and 
2000)  shows 
real 
per 
capita 
incomes 
in 
Bangladesh increased 
by 
about one-quarter 
during 
the 
1990s. 
While 
inequality 
rose 
somewhat over 
the 
course 
of 
the 
decade 
(the 
Gini 
coefficient 
rose 
from 
0.259 
in 
1991-92 
to 
0.306 
in 
2000), 
poverty 
levels declined considerably over 
this 
period. 
Fifty 
percent 
of 
the 
country’s 
population 
was 
poor 
(i.e. 
below 
the 
upper 
poverty 
line) 
in 
2000, compared 
to 
59 
percent 
in 
1991-92. 
Similarly, 
34 
percent 
of 
Bangladesh’s 
population 
was 
very 
poor 
in 
2000 
(as 
measured 
by 
the 
lower poverty 
line, 
using 
the cost 
of 
basic 
needs 
method), 
compared 
to 
43 
percent 
in 
1991-92. 
Throughout 
the 
decade, 
rural 
poverty 
remained 
higher 
than 
urban 
poverty, 
but 
declined 
by 
roughly 
the 
same 
percentages 
over 
the 
period 
(around 
8 
percentage points). 
In 
2000, 
a 
little 
over 
20 
percent 
of 
the 
country’s 
population 
resided 
in 
urban 
areas, 
where the 
incidence 
of 
poverty 
(around 37 
percent) was considerably 
lower 
than 
in 
rural 
areas 
(53 
per~ent).~ 
Notwithstanding 
this 
progress, data 
from 
the 
HES 
series 
indicate that 
the pace 
of 
poverty 
reduction 
in 
rural 
areas 
slowed 
down 
between 
1995-96 
and 
2000, 
with 
poverty 
in 
urban 
areas 
actually 
rising 
during 
this 
period. 
8. 
Much 
of 
the 
sustained 
economic 
progress made 
during 
the 
1990s 
can 
be 
attributed 
to 
the 
wide- 
ranging 
reforms introduced 
in 
the 
early 
1990s, 
by 
the 
present 
Minister 
of 
Finance and 
Planning, 
to 
remove 
the 
plethora 
of 
controls 
on 
economic 
activity 
and 
establish 
the 
private 
sector 
as 
the 
engine 
of 
growth. 
Import 
controls 
and 
many 
non-tariff 
barriers 
were  removed, 
while 
import 
procedures 
were 
simplified. 
The 
fixed 
exchange 
rate 
system 
was 
replaced 
by 
a 
more 
flexibly 
administered 
system; 
the 
investment 
regime 
was 
de-regulated 
to 
encourage 
private participation 
- 
both 
foreign 
and 
domestic; and 
market-oriented 
policies 
were 
introduced 
to 
create 
a supportive 
policy 
environment 
for 
the 
private 
sector. 
Power 
and telecommunications, 
sectors 
reserved 
for 
the 
state, 
were 
opened 
up 
for 
private 
investment. 
The 
investment 
regime, 
for 
both 
domestic 
and 
foreign 
investment, 
was 
liberalized 
to allow 
investment 
Poverty 
in 
Bangladesh: 
Building 
on 
Progress, 
joint 
poverty 
assessment 
by 
the 
World 
Bank 
and 
Asian 
Development 
Bank, 
December 
2002. 
3 
Page  12
without 
caps 
in 
all 
spheres 
except 
those 
restricted 
on 
national 
security 
grounds; 
all 
restrictions 
on 
repatriation 
of 
profit 
and income 
from 
foreign 
investment 
were also 
eliminated. 
Emerging 
Challenges 
9. 
Under 
growing political 
pressure, 
however, 
the 
pace 
of 
reforms 
began 
slowing toward 
the 
middle 
of 
the 
1990s. 
Governance 
continued 
to 
deteriorate 
and 
is 
exacting a huge 
toll 
on 
the 
economy. 
In 
addition, 
fiscal 
outcomes worsened 
and 
contingent 
liabilities 
rose, 
largely 
as 
a 
result 
of 
state-owned 
enterprises’ 
long-standing 
poor 
performance. 
Adverse 
developments 
in 
the 
external 
environment, 
compounded 
by 
the 
events 
following 
September 
1 
1,200 
1, 
led 
to 
a 
significant 
erosion 
in 
foreign 
exchange 
reserves, 
with 
economic 
growth slowing 
down to 
slightly 
over 
4 
percent 
in 
2002. 
Sustaining 
and 
accelerating 
the 
growth 
necessary 
to 
absorb Bangladesh’s 
burgeoning labor 
force 
and 
to 
reduce 
poverty 
will 
require  improvements 
in 
macroeconomic 
management, 
investment 
efficiency, 
competitiveness, 
accountability 
and transparency. 
III. 
Recent 
Economic 
Developments 
10. 
Bangladesh’s 
vulnerability, 
which 
increased 
significantly 
following 
the events 
of 
September 
1 
1, 
2001, 
is 
largely 
the 
result 
of 
structural 
weaknesses 
in 
the 
economy. 
Although 
the 
rate 
of 
GDP 
growth 
remains 
respectable, 
averaging 
5 
percent 
annually 
over  the 
1990s, 
it 
remains 
much 
below 
potential. 
Weak 
governance 
and deteriorating 
law 
and order 
are 
major 
concerns, 
and 
the 
country 
faced 
two 
sets 
of 
macroeconomic imbalances 
when 
the 
new 
Government 
took 
office 
- 
low 
foreign 
exchange reserves 
and 
rising 
fiscal 
deficits. 
1 
1. 
Reserve 
levels 
are 
low, 
despite 
recent improvements. 
Gross 
foreign 
exchange reserves 
with 
Bangladesh Bank, 
which 
peaked at-$3.3 
billion 
in 
the 
third

of 
FY95 
(Figure 
2), 
had 
declined 
to 
about 
$1.1 
billion 
by 
the 
end 
of 
2001, 
barely 
sufficient 
to 
finance  about 
one 
month 
of 
imports. 
The 
downward 
trend 
in 
reserves 
was the 
result 
of 
a 
number 
of 
factors 
including 
(i) 
the 
surge 
in 
imports 
in 
FYOl, 
particularly 
resulting 
from 
fiscal 
pressures 
of 
an 
over-sized 
Annual 
Development 
Program 
(ADP); 
(ii) 
the 
slow 
down 
in 
export 
growth, 
particularly 
since 
November 
2000; 
(iii) 
the 
rising 
foreign 
exchange 
payments 
on 
account 
of 
power 
purchase 
agreements, 
production 
sharing 
contracts 
for 
gas 
exploration 
and 
costly 
suppliers’ 
credits; 
and 
(iv) 
falling aid 
disbursements.

of

will

by

15. 
Key  thrust 
of 
the 
I-PRSP. 
Government’s 
overarching 
goal, 
as 
articulated 
in 
the I-PRSP, 
is 
to 
substantially 
reduce 
chronic 
poverty 
and 
invigorate 
social 
development 
in 
the 
shortest 
possible 
time. 
In 
addition 
to 
specifying 
social 
targets (Table 
2) 
in 
line 
with 
the country’s 
Millennium 
Development 
Goals, 
the 
I-PRSP 
commits 
the authorities 
to 
halve 
the 
proportion 
of 
population 
living 
below 
the 
poverty 
line 
by 
the year 2015. 
The 
strategy recognizes 
the 
need 
for 
acceleration 
of 
economic 
growth 
as 
the 
engine 
to 
drive 
down 
poverty. 
In 
parallel, 
the 
strategy 
lays 
emphasis 
on 
human 
development 
by 
paying 
attention 
to 
the 
need 
for 
greater 
access 
of 
the 
poor 
to 
education, 
health 
and 
nutrition, 
providing for 
greater 
social 
protection 
and 
empowering 
the 
poor 
through participatory 
processes 
that 
would 
give 
them 
voice 
in 
shaping 
their 
own 
destinies. 
16. 
Thus 
the 
Government’s 
poverty 
reduction 
strategy 
is 
a 
combination 
of 
key 
elements 
that 
are 
expected 
to propel 
growth, 
while 
making 
sure 
that the 
benefits 
of 
that 
growth 
are shared 
by 
the 
poor. 
The 
I-PRSP 
articulates 
this 
strategy 
as 
resting 
on 
five 
pillars: 
(i) 
acceleration 
of 
pro-poor 
growth; 
(ii) 
improvements 
in 
governance; 
(iii) 
investment 
in 
human 
development; 
(iv) 
women’s 
advancement; 
and 
(v) 
social protection. 
17. 
Acceleration 
of 
pro-poor 
growth. 
The I-PRSP aims 
to 
raise 
the 
economy’s 
growth 
rate 
from 
about 
5 
percent 
per 
annum 
to 
6-7 percent, 
in 
order 
to 
attain 
the 
MDG 
poverty 
reduction 
target 
set 
for 
2015. 
The 
key 
building 
blocks 
of 
this 
strategy 
are 
summarized 
below. 
A 
stable  macroeconomic 
framework 
is 
proposed 
as 
the 
foundation 
on 
which 
to 
build 
a 
sustainable 
path 
of 
accelerated 
growth. 
This 
would 
entail 
maintenance 
of 
low 
inflation 
and 
sustainable 
fiscal deficits 
and 
external 
debt. 
To 
ensure 
macroeconomic 
balance, 
the 
medium 
term 
focus 
will 
be 
on 
four 
critical 
areas: 
(a) 
increased domestic resource 
mobilization; 
(b) 
rationalized 
and 
improved quality 
of 
public 
expenditure; 
(c) 
prudent 
monetary 
policy; 
and 
(d) 
improved 
foreign 
sector 
management. 
The 
strategy 
emphasizes 
the 
pivotal 
role 
of 
the 
private 
sector 
as 
an 
engine 
of 
growth. 
To 
enable 
the 
private 
sector 
to 
play 
this 
role, 
the 
Government 
proposes 
to 
withdraw 
from 
manufacturing 
activity 
and 
create 
an 
investment-fi-iendly  environment, 
by 
pursuing 
policies 
to 
ensure 
macroeconomic 
stability, 
improve 
law 
and 
order, 
promote 
good 
governance, 
and 
maintain 
competitiveness. 
The 
Government 
recognizes 
that a 
sound 
and 
well-functioning 
financial 
system 
is 
essential 
to 
achieve the 
desired 
acceleration 
of 
growth 
and 
to 
ensure 
that 
the 
poor 
have 
access 
to 
financial 
resources. 
The 
Government 
plans 
to 
address 
three 
critical 
problems 
facing 
the 
financial 
sector: 
(i) 
Bangladesh 
Bank’s 
weak 
regulatory 
power; 
(ii) 
poor 
governance 
of 
the 
public 
financial 
institutions; and 
(iii) 
deficiencies 
of 
the 
legal 
framework. 
There 
is 
also 
continued 
recognition 
of 
the 
important 
role 
of 
micro 
credit 
as 
an 
instrument 
of 
poverty 
reduction. 
Recognizing 
that 
globalization 
presents 
challenges 
as 
well 
as 
opportunities 
for 
Bangladesh, the 
proposed  strategy 
seeks 
to 
seize 
the 
opportunities 
by 
strengthening 
institutional 
capabilities, 
addressing 
structural 
weaknesses, 
and 
improving 
the 
policy 
regime. 
Building 
on 
the past 
record 
of 
trade reforms, 
the 
Government plans 
to 
move forward 
on 
the 
trade 
reform 
agenda 
in 
order 
to 
achieve 
greater 
diversification 
of 
the 
export 
base 
and 
increase 
export 
competitiveness. 
Further, 
it 
intends 
to 
strengthen 
trade 
support capacity 
to 
cope 
with 
emerging 
changes 
in 
the 
global 
trading 
environment  and 
to 
ensure 
its 
effective 
participation 
in 
regional  blocks  and 
the 
WTO 
negotiations. 
6 
Page  15
. 
For 
rapid 
poverty 
reduction, the Government’s 
priority 
is 
to 
develop 
rural 
areas, 
where 
most 
of 
the 
poor 
live. 
This 
requires 
accelerated 
growth 
of 
agnculture and 
the 
rural 
non-farm 
sector. 
To 
encourage 
agricultural 
growth, 
the 
Government 
proposes 
to 
improve 
the 
policy 
environment 
by 
encouraging 
the 
adoption 
of 
new 
technologies, 
strengthening 
extension 
services, 
the 
provision 
of 
credit 
to 
small 
farmers, 
and 
providing 
an 
appropriate 
mix 
of 
incentives 
to 
pursue 
profitable 
operations. 
For 
accelerating 
rural 
non-farm 
growth, 
measures are 
planned 
to 
strengthen 
both 
backward 
links 
e.g. 
agncultural 
equipment 
and 
repair 
services, 
marketing 
of 
fertilizer, 
seed 
and 
other 
inputs, 
as 
well 
as 
forward 
links 
to 
agricultural activities 
like 
processing and 
marketing 
of 
agncultural 
products. 
Emphasis 
is 
to 
be 
given 
to 
improve 
infrastructure 
to 
improve 
competitiveness 
edge 
and 
exploit growth 
potential. 
The 
National 
Rural 
Development 
Policy 
200 
1, 
which 
has 
been 
formulated to 
guide 
the 
comprehensive development 
of 
rural 
areas, 
attaches 
importance 
to 
creating 
efficient 
and 
effective 
local 
government 
institutions 
as 
the 
decentralized 
decision-making 
fiamework 
within 
a 
participatory 
mode. 
While 
agnculture 
and 
rural 
development 
will 
remain 
critical 
to 
sustained 
growth 
of 
the 
Bangladesh economy, 
the 
Government 
recognizes that acceleration 
of 
growth 
will 
have 
to 
come 
fiom 
the 
expansion 
of 
a 
dynamic 
manufacturing 
sector, 
based 
on 
the 
principles 
of 
comparative 
advantage 
within 
an 
export-oriented 
policy 
framework. 
Thus 
the strategy 
is 
to 
pursue 
globally 
competitive 
industrialization 
dictated 
by 
“dynamic 
comparative 
advantage”, 
such 
that 
industrialization 
is 
employment-intensive 
with 
emphasis 
on 
small 
and 
medium 
enterprises 
(SMEs) 
and export-oriented 
industries. 
The 
strategy 
is 
to 
face 
the 
challenge 
of 
the 
impending 
phase 
out 
of 
the 
multi-fibre 
arrangement 
by 
taking 
concrete 
steps 
to 
sustain 
the 
economy’s 
export 
performance 
in 
the 
face 
of 
increased 
global 
competition. 
The 
Government 
recognizes that accelerated 
growth 
in 
all 
of 
the 
above components 
will 
not 
be 
feasible 
without 
substantial 
augmentation 
of 
the 
existing 
capacity 
and 
quality 
of 
infrastructure 
services, 
which 
serve 
as 
the 
backbone 
of 
a 
growing 
economy, 
while 
creating 
jobs 
for 
vast 
numbers 
of 
the 
poor. 
For 
instance, 
the 
impact 
of 
roads and bridges 
on 
poverty 
is 
well 
documented 
in 
Bangladesh. 
Thus 
there 
is 
strong 
emphasis 
in 
the 
proposed 
strategy 
on 
improving 
roads, 
electricity 
and telecommunications 
services. 
The policies 
will 
also 
support the development 
of 
information 
and 
communication 
technology at 
all 
levels 
of 
education, 
including 
development 
of 
institutions 
to 
facilitate 
the 
transfer 
of 
technology. 
. 
. 
18. 
Promoting 
Good 
Governance. 
The 
second 
pillar 
of 
the 
Government’s 
growth 
and poverty 
reduction 
strategy rests 
on 
the 
broad 
realization 
that 
poor 
governance 
is 
a 
severe 
impediment 
to 
current 
poverty reduction 
efforts. 
Therefore, 
the Government’s 
strategy 
of 
improving 
governance 
focuses 
on 
addressing three 
major 
areas: 
(1) 
creating a 
competitive 
environment 
across 
all 
segments 
in 
society, 
along 
with 
an 
unhindered 
flow 
of 
information; 
(ii) 
establishing and 
enforcing 
clear 
rules 
and regulations 
for 
public 
sector 
administration 
supported 
by 
separation 
of 
power 
among 
three branches 
of 
governance 
along 
with 
pragmatic 
oversight 
arrangements; 
and 
(iii) 
promoting 
voice 
and 
participation 
of 
the 
civil 
society, 
including 
women, 
the 
poor 
and 
disadvantaged 
groups, 
as 
a 
step 
toward 
a 
transparent 
and 
‘open’ 
government. 
To 
achieve 
these 
objectives, 
the 
Government 
plans 
to 
implement 
a 
judicial reform 
program 
to 
guarantee 
citizen’s 
constitutional 
rights, 
execute 
fundamental 
reforms 
of 
the 
police 
force 
to 
improve 
law 
and 
order, 
undertake 
measures 
to 
establish 
effective 
planning 
and budgeting 
systems 
supported 
by 
transparent 
accounting  and 
auditing, 
and 
improve 
public 
procurement 
systems 
with 
full 
financial 
accountability. 
The 
Government 
is 
committed 
to 
full 
independence 
of 
the 
Auditor 
General,  and 
the 
establishment 
of 
a 
fully 
functioning Public 
Accounts Committee. 
19. 
Investing 
in 
Human 
Development. 
Given 
the strong 
poverty reducing 
effects 
of 
human capital 
development 
in 
Bangladesh, 
a 
major 
emphasis 
of 
the 
Government’s 
strategy 
in 
the 
medium 
term 
is 
the 
development 
of 
basic 
capabilities and human 
resource 
development 
of 
the 
poor 
population. 
This 
is 
to 
be 
done 
through 
targeted 
public 
expenditures 
in 
education, 
health 
and 
nutrition 
and 
radically 
improved 
7 
Page  16
system 
of 
incentives 
for public 
service 
providers. 
Substantial resources have 
been allocated 
recently to 
the 
expansion 
of 
education, 
primary 
education 
in 
particular, 
but 
much 
remains 
to 
be 
done 
in 
the 
areas 
of 
health 
and 
nutrition. 
Within 
the 
health 
sector, 
although 
some 
success 
has 
been 
achieved 
in 
preventive 
health 
care, 
a 
small 
proportion 
of 
poor 
people have 
access 
to 
public 
health 
care 
services. 
The 
strategy 
thus 
gives 
priority 
to 
completing 
the 
unfinished 
agenda 
and addressing the 
pro-poor 
concerns 
in 
health 
and 
nutrition. 
Despite 
some 
improvement 
in 
the 
last 
decade, 
child 
malnutrition 
in 
Bangladesh remains 
high, 
with 
adverse 
implications 
for 
future 
poverty 
reduction. 
The 
introduction 
of 
a 
national 
nutrition 
program to 
address 
the 
malnutrition 
of 
chldren 
under 
two 
as 
well 
as 
pregnant 
and 
lactating 
mothers 
through 
the 
provision 
of 
food 
supplements, 
nutrition 
and 
health 
counseling 
is 
a 
positive 
step 
to 
address 
the 
problem. 
Existing 
food-targeting 
programs 
such 
as 
VGD 
and 
RMP 
(Rural Maintenance Program) 
will 
also 
be 
used 
to 
reach 
the 
poorest 
and 
the 
most vulnerable 
with 
nutrition-support 
package. 
In 
the 
area 
of 
basic education, 
the 
plan 
is 
to 
improve 
quality, 
while 
increasing 
access 
for 
the poorest 
segments 
of 
society. 
NGOs 
and 
private 
sector 
are 
envisaged 
as 
providers 
of 
hgh-quality 
education 
along 
with 
the 
Govemment. 
20. 
The 
Government 
is 
fully 
conscious  that 
the 
burden 
of 
poverty 
falls 
disproportionately 
on 
women. 
The 
underlying 
factors 
are 
low 
literacy 
rate, 
low 
nutrition, 
low 
income 
with 
discriminating 
wage 
differentials 
compared 
to 
men, 
low 
life 
expectancy, 
and 
high 
morbidity. 
While 
the 
gender-gap 
is 
closing 
in 
Bangladesh 
for 
most 
social indicators, the 
overall 
level 
of 
empowerment 
measured 
in 
terms 
of 
literacy, 
work 
force participation, 
property 
rights, 
and 
credit 
access, 
remains 
low. 
The 
strategy 
for 
furthering 
women’s  advancement 
includes 
emphasis 
on 
policies 
and 
institutional 
actions 
to 
combat 
continuing 
negative 
sex 
ratios, violence 
against women, 
high 
maternal 
mortality, 
restrictions 
on 
women’s employment and economic opportunities; 
policies 
to 
ensure 
formal 
equality; 
supporting 
affmtive 
actions 
at 
all 
levels 
and 
in 
all 
spheres; 
and 
creating a 
women-hendly 
institutional 
environment overall. 
Finally, 
the 
Government 
proposes 
to 
actively promote 
policies 
for 
increasing women’s 
participation 
in 
all 
spheres 
of 
development 
under 
the 
poverty reduction 
strategy. 
Promoting 
Women’s  advancement. 
21, 
Ensuring 
Social 
Protection. 
To 
help 
increase 
the 
poor’s  crisis-coping 
capability 
and 
form 
important 
risk-insurance 
policies 
for 
them, 
four 
sets 
of 
policies 
would 
be 
emphasized 
for 
the 
poor 
to 
cope 
better 
with 
various 
income 
(consumption) 
shocks. 
The 
first 
set 
will 
continue 
focus 
on 
social 
safety 
nets 
for 
the 
poor through 
various 
food-assisted 
and 
cash-assisted 
programs, old-age 
pension 
schemes 
in 
rural 
areas, 
support 
for 
the 
female destitute, and 
traditional 
relief 
programs. 
These 
programs 
will 
address 
the 
specific 
problems 
of 
chronic 
poverty 
of 
socially 
disadvantaged groups 
(street 
children, 
elderly 
poor, the 
disabled population, 
to 
name a 
few). 
The 
second 
set 
will 
address 
the 
vulnerabilities 
of 
the 
‘new 
poor’, 
including 
the 
retrenched 
and 
displaced workers 
from 
privatized 
SOEs, 
market reforms 
and globalization. 
The 
third 
set 
emphasize the 
development 
of 
social 
solidarity 
by 
encouraging 
bonding 
and 
bridging 
across 
self-help 
groups 
under 
GOs 
and 
NGOs 
at the 
local 
level. 
The 
new 
Social Development 
Foundation 
(SDF) 
will 
play 
an 
important 
role 
in 
fostering  social 
capital 
formation 
by 
promoting 
CBOs 
and 
local 
associations 
in 
building 
and  maintenance 
of 
small-scale 
community 
infrastructures. 
The 
fourth 
set 
concerns 
risk 
insurance, 
including 
measures 
to 
mitigate 
risks 
faced 
by 
the 
poor 
by 
(a) 
providing 
access 
to 
credit 
in 
times 
of 
emergency, 
(b) 
improving public 
health 
services 
to 
reduce 
health 
hazard 
related 
to 
income 
and 
consumption 
shocks,  (c) 
strengthening  disaster 
prevention 
and 
mitigation 
mechanisms 
to 
enhance 
the 
poor’s  coping 
capability 
in 
times 
of 
natural 
disasters, 
and 
(d) 
adopting countervailing 
measures, 
to 
enable 
the 
poor, 
particularly 
women, 
to 
cope 
with 
the 
costs 
incurred 
as 
a 
result 
of 
violence 
and personal insecurity. 
V. 
Reforms Supported 
by 
the 
Proposed 
Credit 
22. 
The 
proposed 
credit 
will 
‘support 
a 
subset 
of 
the 
Government’s 
medium 
term 
reform 
program, 
discussed 
above. 
The 
Government’s  approach 
has 
been 
to 
launch 
an 
initial 
set 
of 
reforms 
in 
areas 
where 
broad 
consensus 
exists, 
with 
the 
expectation that 
these 
will 
begin 
to pay 
off 
shortly, 
creating 
momentum 
8 
Page  17
and 
public 
support 
for 
subsequent 
measures. 
Consequently,  Government’s  emphasis 
during 
the 
first 
phase 
is 
on 
actions 
that 
will 
improve 
the 
investment climate and stimulate 
economic 
growth. 
Government 
is 
also engaging 
its 
development partners, 
including 
the 
World 
Bank, 
in 
a 
parallel 
dialogue 
to 
deepen 
reforms 
in 
several 
sectors. 
So, 
while 
the 
proposed 
DSC 
is 
focused 
on 
supporting the near-term 
priorities 
identified 
by 
the 
Government 
as 
critical 
to 
the 
implementation 
of 
the I-PRSP, other 
related 
investment 
operations 
are 
supporting 
the 
implementation 
of 
reforms 
in 
health,  education 
and 
rural 
development, 
including 
water 
management 
(see 
Section 
VI 
on 
the 
Bank‘s 
strategy). 
A. 
The 
Growth 
Agenda 
23. 
Notwithstanding 
several exogenous 
shocks, 
average 
GDP 
growth 
in 
Bangladesh 
has 
shown 
a 
steady 
increase 
from 
2.8 
percent 
in 
the 
1970s 
to 
3.7 
percent 
in 
the 
1980s, 
to 
4.8 
percent 
in 
the 
1990s. 
All 
three 
broad 
sectors, 
agriculture, 
industry 
and 
services, 
have 
contributed 
to 
growth. 
The 
acceleration 
in 
growth 
is 
attributable 
to 
increased 
private 
investment 
resulting 
from 
increased 
national 
savings 
and the 
opening 
up 
of 
the  economy 
to 
international 
trade. 
The 
initial 
stimulus 
to 
growth 
came 
in 
the 
late 
seventies and 
early 
eighties 
when 
Bangladesh launched 
an 
initial 
set 
of 
reforms, 
such 
as 
the 
withdrawal 
of 
food 
and 
apcultural 
subsidies, 
involving 
the 
private 
sector 
in 
the 
distribution 
of 
irrigation 
equipment 
and 
the phasing 
out 
of 
the 
monopoly 
of 
the Bangladesh 
Agriculture 
Development 
Corporation. 
Reforms 
in 
the 
early 
1990s 
opened 
the 
economy 
further 
by 
removing 
non-tariff 
restrictions 
on 
trade, 
improving 
export 
incentives, 
deregulating 
private 
investment and 
relaxing 
exchange controls. 
During 
this 
period 
regulatory 
barriers 
to 
entry, 
exit 
and 
competition 
were 
significantly 
reduced. 
24. 
However,  although 
the 
progress 
of 
the 
1990s 
is 
heartening, 
Bangladesh 
has 
fallen 
short 
of 
its 
growth 
potential 
when 
compared 
to 
countries 
like 
China 
and 
India. 
Recent 
surveys 
and 
analytical work, 
including 
a recent 
survey 
of 
1000 
manufacturing 
firms 
jointly 
conducted 
by 
the 
Bangladesh 
Enterprise 
Institute 
and 
the 
World 
Bank’, 
have 
identified poor 
governance  environment, 
wasteful 
state-owned 
enterprises 
which 
crowd 
out 
private 
sector 
activity, 
a 
poorly 
performing  financial 
system, 
and 
an 
inefficient 
and 
loss-making 
energy 
sector 
as 
the 
key 
constraints 
affecting 
the 
investment 
climate. 
While 
there 
may 
not 
be 
any 
regulatory 
barriers 
to 
entry, 
the 
fact  that 
it 
can 
take 
several 
months 
to 
get 
a 
telephone 
or 
electricity 
connection 
or 
a 
construction 
permit, 
as 
revealed 
by 
the 
investment 
climate 
survey, 
provides 
some 
indication 
of 
how 
difficult 
it 
can 
be 
to 
start 
a 
business 
in 
Bangladesh. 
Moreover, although 
6 
permits 
are 
needed 
to 
set 
up 
a 
business, 
as 
is 
the 
case 
in 
China 
and 
Indonesia, 
it 
takes 
much 
longer 
to 
get 
these 
permits 
in 
Bangladesh.  Businessmen 
therefore 
usually 
have 
no 
option 
but 
to 
resort 
to paying 
‘speed-money’ 
to 
overcome 
these 
bureaucratic 
hurdles. 
Macroeconomic 
Stabilization 
25. 
Early 
Actions. 
Creating a 
stable 
macroeconomic environment 
is 
a central 
pillar 
in 
Government’s 
strategy 
to 
accelerate 
economic 
growth. 
As 
a 
critical 
step 
in 
ensuring macroeconomic stability, 
and 
in 
light 
of 
the 
difficult 
foreign 
exchange  situation,  the 
Government 
took 
early 
actions 
in 
key 
areas 
of 
macroeconomic  management. 
When 
the 
Government 
was  formed, 
the 
Ministries 
of 
Finance  and 
Planning 
were 
put 
under 
one 
minister, 
to 
ensure 
better 
co-ordination 
between 
the  development 
and 
recurrent 
budgets. 
Revenue 
and Expenditure Commissions were 
established 
to 
make 
recommendations 
for 
improving 
fiscal 
management. 
Improving 
the 
Investment 
Climate 
in 
Bangladesh, 
Bangladesh Enterprise 
Institute 
and 
the 
World 
Bank, 
March 
2003. 
The survey 
was 
conducted 
during 
the 
second 
half 
of 
2002 
and 
included 
the 
following 
sectors: 
textiles, 
food 
and 
food 
processing, 
leather 
and 
leather 
products,  electronics, 
and 
chemicals 
and 
pharmaceuticals. 
The 
survey 
included 
firms 
in 
Dhaka 
(and 
surrounding 
areas) 
and 
Chittagong. 
9

26. 
Several 
actions were taken 
to 
contain 
the 
FY02

and 
its

financing. 
The

was 
cut 
by 
about 
17

2

Non-Aligned

@AM)

US$200

Interest

Certificates were 
initially

by 
between 
1

3

certificate

to 
reduce Government’s

servicing

The

that

proposals 
to 
be 
funded 
by 
external 
suppliers 
credits 
would 
be kept 
in 
abeyance, 
and  strict 
expenditure 
control 
processes 
were 
established 
to 
control 
the 
release 
of 
approved development 
funds.

of 
existing 
enterprise 
units, 
additional tax 
on 
undistributed 
profits 
of 
listed 
companies, 
inclusion 
of 
all 
sources 
of 
NGO 
profits 
into 
the 
tax 
net 
with 
the 
exception 
of 
income 
from 
micro-credit 
operations, 
abolition 
of 
zero 
duty 
rate 
on 
many 
imports, 
withdrawal 
of 
several 
VAT 
exemptions 
and 
extension 
of 
VAT 
to 
additional 
areas 
of 
activity, 
and 
increases 
in 
travel 
and 
motor 
vehicle 
taxes. 
These revenue 
increases 
are 
expected 
to 
substantially 
offset 
revenue 
losses 
that 
could 
result 
from 
the 
reduction 
in 
the 
maximum 
tariff 
rate and 
the 
reduction 
in 
corporate 
tax 
rates 
by 
5 
percent 
each. 
Actual 
results 
for 
the 
first 
three 
quarters 
of 
FY03 
are 
encouraging, 
with 
revenue 
collections 
growing 
by 
about 
20.3 
percent over 
the 
same 
period 
in 
FY02. 
Budget 
deficit 
in 
the 
first 
half 
of 
FY03 
declined 
to 
Tk36.6 
billion, 
compared 
with 
Tk70.9 
billion 
in 
the 
first 
half 
of 
FY02. 
29. 
Improving 
Fiscal Transparency 
and 
Budget Management. 
Several actions have 
been 
taken 
to 
improve 
fiscal 
transparency and 
ensure 
that day-to-day 
fiscal 
management 
is 
put 
on 
a 
sounder 
footing. 
Monthly 
fiscal 
performance 
reports 
are 
now 
generated 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
information 
gathered 
from 
a 
computerized 
budgeting 
and 
accounting 
database. 
Annual 
Development 
Program related 
fund 
releases 
are 
monitored 
on 
a 
daily 
basis, 
as 
is 
Government’s 
borrowing 
from 
the 
banking 
system. 
The 
performance 
of 
10 
major 
SOEs, 
which 
account 
for 
about  80 percent 
of 
total 
SOE 
contribution to 
GDP, 
is 
being 
monitored regularly 
as 
are 
inter-agency 
arrears 
owed 
to 
government. 
An 
inter-ministerial 
data 
monitoring 
and 
reconciliation 
task 
force 
has 
been 
formed 
to 
reconcile 
fiscal 
and 
monetary 
data. 
By 
streamlining 
budgetary 
procedures,  the 
carryover 
of 
expenditures 
into 
the 
next 
fiscal 
year 
has 
been 
dramatically 
reduced 
from 
Tk. 
17 
billion 
in 
FY02 
to 
Tk.5 
billion 
in 
FY03. 
30. 
Medium-Term 
Macroeconomic 
Framework. 
The 
Government’s 
medium-term 
macroeconomic 
framework, 
jointly 
agreed 
with 
the 
JMF 
and 
the 
World 
Bank,  provides 
for 
the 
maintenance 
of 
sustainable 
fiscal deficits 
and 
targets an acceleration 
in 
the 
rate 
of 
economic 
growth. 
Table 
3 
summarizes 
the 
program’s 
key 
elements, 
details 
of 
which 
are 
discussed 
in 
Annex 
C. 
Fiscal 
projections 
are 
consistent 
with 
Bangladesh’s 
reform 
needs, 
with 
increased revenue 
mobilization 
and 
better 
prioritized 
expenditures 
being 
central 
elements 
of 
the 
budget 
strategy 
over 
the 
medium 
term. 
3 
1. 
Prioritizing 
expenditures. 
On 
the expenditure 
side, 
the 
medium-term 
expenditure 
framework 
is 
expected 
to 
continue 
Bangladesh’s 
long 
standing 
tradition 
of 
emphasizing investment 
in 
the 
social 
sectors 
and 
in 
rural 
infrastructure. 
Health 
and education 
expenditures 
combined 
have averaged 23 percent 
of 
the 
total 
budgetary 
expenditures 
during 
the past 
10 
years 
and 
similar 
allocations 
are 
projected for 
the future. 
For 
FY03, 
the education 
sector gets 
top 
priority 
and 
accounts 
for 
15.4 
percent 
of 
total 
expenditures, 
while 
the 
health 
sector 
accounts 
for 
7 
percent. Defense 
expenditures 
remain 
low 
by 
developing country 
and 
South 
Asian 
standards, 
accounting 
for 
less 
than 
1.5 
percent 
of 
GDP or 
7.8 
percent 
of 
total 
expenditures. 
Nevertheless, 
further 
streamlining 
the 
ADP 
and 
strengthening 
MDG-related 
expenditures 
remains 
priority 
areas 
for 
action.6 
32. 
To 
provide 
an 
actionable 
plan 
for 
implementation 
of 
these 
priority 
measures, 
the 
Government 
has 
established 
a 
high-level 
Public 
Expenditure 
Review 
Commission 
which 
recently 
submitted 
its 
Interim 
report. 
The 
recommendations  focus 
on 
improving 
the 
project 
selection and 
project 
approval  criteria, 
stressing 
in 
particular 
the 
need 
for 
reduction 
in 
the 
number 
of 
unapproved 
projects 
(currently representing 
22 
percent 
of 
all 
ADP 
projects), 
and termination 
of 
low 
quality 
projects. 
The 
Commission 
deemed the 
following 
projects 
as 
low 
quality: 
projects that receive 
nominal 
allocations 
at such 
low 
rate that 
it 
would 
take 
many 
years 
to 
complete; 
projects 
that 
are 
better 
performed 
by 
the 
private 
sector 
(e.g., 
management 
of 
trucks 
and 
buses, 
ferries, 
motels, 
etc.); 
and projects prepared 
on 
unsound technical or 
financial 
grounds. 
Other 
recommendations 
pertain 
to 
improving 
the 
balance 
between 
capital and 
recurrent 
expenditures 
and 
unification 
of 
revenue 
and development 
budget, 
among 
others. 
It 
will 
be 
particularly 
beneficial 
to 
merge 
the 
Ministries 
of 
Finance 
and Planning. 
Following 
these 
recommendations,  above 
two 
hundred 
low 
Bangladesh 
Public 
Expenditure 
Review, 
joint 
report 
by 
the 
World

and

June 
26,2002.

priority 
projects have 
been 
dropped 
from

The

is

fi-eed

to

of

Priority

-

and

expenditures 
- 
need 
increased

together

in

(discussed

In

the

is 
on

in

and

In

wages 
and salaries 
still 
preempt 
large

of

squeezing non-wage

and

Non-wage

such

and

for 
the 
essential  service

therefore  expected 
to

Spending 
for

capital 
development 
is 
targeted 
to 
rise 
by 
at least 
1 
percentage 
point 
of

in

33. 
The 
projected 
higher 
fiscal deficits 
in 
FY04-06 
reflect 
increasing 
development expenditures 
and 
the  adjustment  cost 
of 
reforms 
that 
will 
be 
borne 
by

The

in

are 
largely 
due 
to 
expenditures 
on 
additional poverty 
reduction 
efforts 
in

with

and

in

The 
reform 
costs, 
largely 
on 
account 
of 
SOEs 
and 
NCBs, 
are 
estimated 
to 
amount 
to 
about 
Tk43 
billion 
(nearly  $750 
million) 
in 
FY04 
and 
FY05 
annually, 
both 
on 
account 
of 
cqrrent 
(severance 
payments, 
additional 
interest  cost) 
and 
capital 
(debt 
absorption, 
arrears 
settlement) 
costs.7 
The 
higher 
fiscal deficits 
are 
expected 
to 
be financed 
by 
increased 
external 
financing 
on 
concessional 
terms, 
with 
domestic 
financing 
remaining 
stable at 
around 
1.9-2

of

From 
FY06 
onward, the 
fiscal 
path 
is 
expected 
to 
show 
further 
consolidation, 
with 
a 
reduction 
in 
the 
overall 
government 
deficit. 
This 
would 
be facilitated 
by 
the 
closure/privatization 
of 
manufacturing 
SOEs 
and 
the 
restructuring 
of 
energy  sector 
SOEs 
by 
FY05. 
The 
size 
of 
the 
SOE 
investment 
program 
included 
in

had 
risen

since the 
early nineties 
to 
around 
Tk. 
5 
8 
billion, 
or 
almost 
one-third 
of 
the 
total 
ADP. 
The 
SOEs 
in 
manufacturing 
and 
energy together have 
been 
the 
highest 
claimant 
of 
budgetary financing. 
About 
95 
percent 
of 
their 
investment 
were 
financed 
under 
the 
ADP 
because 
these 
entities 
generated 
very

resources. 
With 
the 
completion 
of 
their 
privatization 
and 
restructuring 
program 
by

the 
central  Government  budget 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
substantially 
relieved 
of 
this 
wasteful 
expenditure burden. 
34. 
With 
regards 
to 
the balance 
of 
payments 
outlook, 
the 
projections 
assume 
that, 
with 
the 
revival 
in 
the 
global

a

exchange

and

the

of

Bangladesh 
will 
be

to 
improve 
its 
export 
growth

gradually 
from 
modest 
single 
digit 
to 
high 
single 
digit 
levels. 
The 
balance 
of 
payments 
current 
account 
deficit 
is 
projected to 
widen 
to 
1.3 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
FY04, 
increasing 
further 
to 
1.6 
percent 
in 
FY05, 
as 
imports 
grow 
responding 
to 
higher 
growth 
and 
trade 
liberalization,  and 
declining 
thereafter 
to 
1.3 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
FY07. 
The 
framework 
projects a 
realistic 
increase 
of 
GDP growth 
rates 
from 
the 
expected 
5.2 
percent 
in 
FY03 
to 
5.5 
percent 
in 
FY04, reaching 
6.5 
percent 
in 
FY06. 
The 
Government’s 
I-PRSP 
envisages 
that 
these 
rates 
of 
economic 
growth, 
in 
conjunction 
with 
other 
pro-poor 
policies, 
will 
help 
achieve 
the target 
of 
halving 
the 
number 
of 
people 
living 
below 
the 
poverty 
line 
by 
2015.  These 
projections 
are 
subject 
to 
significant 
downside 
risks, 
particularly 
higher 
oil 
prices, 
potential 
losses 
in 
worker’s 
remittances, 
disruptions 
in 
exports 
in 
the 
event 
of 
a 
prolonged 
instability 
in 
the 
Middle-East 
and loss 
of 
market 
share 
in 
RMG 
exports 
following 
the phase-out 
of 
MFA 
in 
2005. 
Liberalizing 
the 
Exchange 
and 
Trade 
Regime 
35. 
Trade 
policy 
reform 
in 
Bangladesh, 
particularly 
since 
1992, has 
emphasized 
the 
liberalization 
of 
imports 
through the 
removal 
of 
quantitative 
restrictions, 
a 
reduction 
in 
nominal 
and 
effective tariffs, 
and 
the 
adoption 
of 
a 
unified 
and 
moderately 
flexible 
exchange 
rate 
regime. 
By 
increasing competition and 
’ 
A 
large 
proportion 
of 
these 
costs are 
expected 
to 
be 
financed outside 
the 
central 
government 
budget, 
for 
example 
through 
the 
issuance 
of 
bonds. 
12 
Page  21
raising 
productivity, 
these 
reforms 
have 
been 
largely 
responsible 
for 
Bangladesh’s greater 
integration 
in 
the 
global 
economy and 
have 
contributed 
to 
an increased 
pace 
of 
economic 
growth. 
36. 
The Government 
has 
resumed 
liberalizing 
the 
trade 
regme, 
a process 
which had 
stagnated 
during 
the latter 
half 
of

199Os, 
by

by 
5 percentage 
points 
to 
32.5

to 
5

As 
a

has

17

89

1. 
There

significant rationalization 
in 
the

The

In

with 
this

the

tariff

will 
be reduced 
to 
30 
percent 
in 
FY04. 
It 
is 
important 
to 
keep 
in 
mind 
that

taxes 
remain 
an 
important 
source 
of 
revenue 
and 
account 
for 50 
percent 
of 
tax 
revenues. Therefore, 
while 
faster

is 
desirable, 
it 
will 
have 
to 
be 
phased 
in 
and 
accompanied 
by 
a

to 
domestic taxation. 
37. 
Good 
progress 
is

reform,

Customs procedures

Entry 
compared

a

As

further

3

for 
95

38. 
Bangladesh 
unified 
its 
exchange 
rate 
and 
moved 
from 
a 
fixed 
to 
a 
moderately 
flexible 
exchange 
regime 
in 
199 
1. 
Since 
then, 
the 
real 
effective 
exchange 
rate 
(REER) 
has 
been 
remarkably 
stable, 
moving 
within 
a 
fairly 
small 
band 
of 
about 
5 
percent 
around 
its 
trend, 
which 
indicates 
slow 
devaluation 
from 
1991 
up 
to 
about 
2001, 
followed 
by 
modest 
appreciation 
since 
then. 
The 
strength 
of 
the 
Taka 
during 
this 
period 
has 
in 
part 
been 
due 
to 
the 
rapid 
growth 
of 
ready-made garment 
(RMG) 
exports, 
which 
increased 
from 
$US 
116 
million 
in

to 
US 
4.8 
billion 
in 
2000, 
and 
growing 
remittances, 
which 
rose 
from 
$390 
million 
to 
$1.9 
billion 
during 
the 
same 
period. 
Bangladesh’s 
exports 
grew 
a 
strong 
14 
percent 
per 
annum 
during 
the 
199Os, 
driven 
by 
a 
remarkable 
17 
percent annual 
growth 
of 
RMG 
exports, 
which 
thrived 
under 
the 
MFA

The reduction 
in

and 
removal 
of 
protective 
QRs 
eliminated 
much 
of 
the 
anti-export 
bias, 
while 
a

of 
slow 
devaluation 
and 
prudent 
management 
of 
the 
REER 
helped 
to 
maintain 
competitiveness 
of 
Bangladesh’s 
exports. 
The 
IMF 
is 
discussing 
further  liberalization 
of 
exchange 
rate 
management 
with 
the 
Government 
and 
expects 
to 
support 
this 
action 
under

operation. 
39. 
The 
phase 
out 
of 
the 
MFA 
in 
2005 
presents 
opportunities 
as 
well 
as 
serious  challenges 
to 
Bangladesh’s 
exports, 
75 
percent 
of 
which 
are 
made 
up 
of 
RMG. 
While 
price 
competitiveness  and a 
motivated labor 
force 
are 
its 
strengths, 
the 
RMG 
sector’s 
position 
as 
a 
“cut, 
make and 
trim” 
(CMT) 
producer, 
without 
direct 
contact 
with 
leading market 
players, 
long 
lead 
times 
due 
to 
delays at 
the 
port 
as 
well 
as 
limited 
backward 
linkage, 
poor 
technology 
base 
and 
lack 
of 
product 
design 
and 
development 
capabilities 
are 
seen 
as 
weaknesses 
in 
a 
more competitive 
post-MFA 
global 
RMG 
market. 
Addressing 
key 
infrastructure 
bottlenecks, 
improving 
the 
regulatory 
environment, 
and 
improving 
competitiveness 
by 
reducing 
the 
cost 
of 
doing 
business 
should 
enable 
Bangladesh 
to 
cope 
with 
these 
impending 
external 
developments 
and 
sustain 
past 
export 
performance.8 
Strategic Development 
and 
Marketing 
Plans

of

Reforming 
State-Owned 
Enterprises 
40. 
As 
discussed 
in 
Section 
IV 
above, 
an 
important 
element 
in 
Government’s strategy 
to 
improve 
the 
climate 
for  private 
sector 
led 
growth 
is 
to 
withdraw 
completely 
from 
participating 
directly 
in 
manufacturing 
activity 
and 
significantly 
reduce 
the 
adverse 
impact 
of 
state-owned enterprises 
(SOEs) 
on 
the  economy. 
With 
physical 
assets 
equivalent 
to 
an  estimated 
35 
percent 
of 
GDP, 
employment 
of 
250,000, 
and 
investment 
equal 
to 
9 
percent 
of 
gross 
domestic investment, 
SOEs 
have remained a 
huge 
drag 
on 
the 
Bangladeshi 
ec~nomy.~ 
Consolidated 
data 
on 
SOEs 
indicate 
that 
their 
financial 
performance 
has 
worsened 
in 
recent 
years, 
leading 
them 
to 
make increasing 
demands 
on 
budgetary 
resources, 
directly 
or 
indirectly. 
Gross 
SOE 
losses 
per 
year, 
counting 
only 
the 
loss-mahng 
entities, 
have 
averaged 
Tk.15 
billion 
annually 
(about 
US$340 
million), 
or 
about 
1 
percent 
of 
GDP 
every 
year. 
SOE 
deficits 
are 
financed 
largely 
through 
the 
Annual 
Development 
Program 
(accounting 
for 
one-third 
of 
the 
central 
government 
budget 
deficit 
during 
FYO 
1 
-02), 
borrowing 
from 
nationalized commercial 
banks 
with 
explicit 
or 
implicit 
government 
guarantees, 
and  default 
on 
debt  service 
and 
other 
liabilities. 
These 
deficits, 
constituting 
9-12 
percent 
of 
total 
domestic 
credit 
during 
FY 
1994-2000, have 
had 
a 
significant effect 
on 
credit 
creation  and 
consequent 
monetary 
expansion 
in 
the  economy. 
The 
cost 
to 
the 
economy 
of 
inefficient 
services 
in 
energy, 
telecommunications, 
railways, 
ports, and other 
public 
utilities 
and banking, 
in 
terms 
of 
increased 
cost 
of 
doing 
business, 
foregone economic 
opportunities 
and 
lost 
consumer 
welfare 
has 
been 
very 
high. 
The 
coverage 
of 
basic services 
like 
power 
and 
gas 
has 
largely 
been 
limited 
to 
the 
urban 
non-poor, 
and 
associated subsidies have 
not 
benefited 
the 
large 
majority 
of 
the 
poor. 
41. 
Government 
policies 
-- 
administered 
pricing 
of 
outputs 
and 
inputs, 
wage 
and 
employment 
rigidities,  off-budget 
transfers 
and 
subsidies 
-- 
have 
undermined 
not 
just 
state 
enterprises’ 
fiscal 
performance 
but 
also 
their 
ability to 
contribute 
to 
development. 
State 
monopolies 
rule 
out competitive 
pricing 
in 
several 
sectors, 
and 
for 
several 
SOEs 
(particularly 
in 
the 
power, 
gas, 
and water 
sectors) 
a 
major 
cause 
of 
the 
high 
operational 
losses 
is 
the 
significant 
proportion 
of 
revenue leakage 
on 
account 
of 
theft, 
pilferage, and 
inadequate 
billing 
and 
collection. 
Most 
public 
utilities 
deliver 
services that 
are 
inadequate 
and 
of 
poor 
quality. 
In 
part 
because 
of 
the 
fiscal 
excesses 
and 
non-viability 
of 
state enterprises, 
public 
financial institutions 
(specialized development 
banks and 
nationalized commercial 
banks) 
suffer 
from 
a 
huge 
burden 
of 
non-performing 
loans 
and, 
consequently, 
capital 
inadequacy. 
42. 
Recognizing 
that 
fundamental 
reforms 
are  necessary 
in 
the 
state 
enterprise  sector 
over  the 
medium-term, 
the 
government has developed 
a 
program 
of 
closures 
and privatization, 
which 
it 
views 
as 
a 
central 
element 
of 
its 
broader 
economic 
refom 
agenda. 
In 
a 
politically 
bold 
decision, 
in 
July 
2002 the 
Government  closed Adamjee 
Jute 
Mills, 
the  largest 
jute 
mill 
in 
the 
world 
employing 
about 
25,000 
workers 
and 
accounting 
for 
about 
10 
percent 
of 
annual 
SOE 
losses. 
Labor 
union 
militancy,  weak 
management 
and pervasive 
corruption 
had 
characterized 
Adamjee’s 
operations. 
Government successfully 
negotiated 
the 
terms 
of 
closure 
with 
the 
workers and 
their 
unions, 
including 
a 
retrenchment package 
that 
has 
been designed 
to 
minimize 
social 
hardships 
caused 
by 
the 
lay-offs. 
Laid 
off 
workers 
from 
Adamjee 
have already 
been 
paid 
about Tk.3.6 
billion 
(US$62 
million) 
in 
retrenchment 
benefits, 
amounting 
to 
two 
months 
salary 
for 
every 
year 
of 
service 
plus 
a redundancy payment 
ranging 
from 
13 
to 
27 
percent 
of 
the 
calculated 
amounts, 
depending 
upon 
length 
of 
service. 
Adamjee’s 
closure has 
been generally 
supported 
within 
the 
country, and there 
have been 
no 
demonstrations 
or 
hartals 
(strikes) 
protesting 
it. 
Buoyed 
by 
its 
success, 
the Government 
has 
closed 
several 
other 
SOEs 
and 
paid-off 
the workers (Table 
4), 
reducing 
total 
SOE 
employment 
by 
about 
16 
percent. 
These 
closures represent an 
especially 
significant 
effort 
on 
the 
part 
of 
Government 
to 
withdraw 
from 
manufacturing activity, 
with 
retrenched workers  accounting 
for 
about 
25 
percent 
of 
all 
SOE 
employees engaged 
in 
manufacturing activity. 
The 
enterprises 
closed 
accounted 
for 
about 
41 
percent 
of 
the 
aggregate losses 
of 
SOEs 
engaged 
in 
manufacturing 
and 
17 
percent 
of 
total 
SOE 
losses.

43.

Deshbandhu 
Sugar

With

from

with

of 
around 
75,000 
workers. 
Strengthening 
the 
Financial 
System 
44. 
The 
I-PRSP 
notes 
that 
“a 
sound 
and 
well- 
functioning financial 
system 
is 
required 
for 
ensuring 
growth 
and 
providing 
access 
to 
financial 
resources 
to 
the 
poor”. 
Poor 
governance, 
lack 
of 
discipline, 
and 
inadequate disclosure are 
fundamental 
problems 
confronting 
the 
financial 
system 
in 
Bangladesh. 
Classified 
(non-performing)  loans amounted 
to 
about 3 
1 
percent 
of 
the 
total loan portfolio 
in 
the 
banking 
system 
at the end 
of 
June 
2002, 
using 
Bangladesh Bank’s 
standards, 
which 
are 
lax 
compared 
to 
intemational 
norms. 
The 
classified 
loans 
of 
the 
nationalized 
commercial 
banks  (NCBs) 
and 
specialized  development  banks 
(SDBs) 
are 
unsustainably 
high 
- 
19 
percent 
of 
loans 
net 
of 
provisions 
or 34 
percent 
on 
a 
gross basis 
for 
NCBs; 
16 
percent 
(net) 
and 
56 
percent 
(gross) 
for 
SDBs 
-- 
and 
show 
little 
change 
over 
time. 
While 
the 
classified 
loans 
of 
the private 
domestic 
banks have 
shown a 
downward 
trend, 
amounting 
to 
around 
16 
percent at the end 
of 
December 2002, the 
level 
is 
still 
high 
given 
their 
limited 
capital 
base. 
Loan 
recovery 
rates 
are 
low 
and, 
as 
of 
the 
end 
of 
June 
2002, 
all 
4 
NCBs, 
2 of 
the 
5 
SDBs, 
and 4 
of 
the 30 
private 
domestic 
banks 
had 
negative 
capital 
after 
the 
full 
provisioning 
requirements 
were 
taken 
into 
account. 
Even 
under  current 
classification 
and 
provisioning 
standards, 
the 
capital 
shortfall 
amounted 
to 
about Tk.55 
billion 
($1 
billion) 
on 
June 
30, 
2002 
-- 
this 
amount 
would 
almost double 
if 
international 
accounting 
standards 
were applied.

45. 
The 
poor 
perfonnance 
is 
due 
to 
various problems 
afflicting public 
and 
private 
banks. 
There 
have 
been 
high 
levels 
of 
insider 
lending 
in 
the 
private 
sector 
banks 
and directed 
lending 
to 
state 
owned 
enterprises. 
Interest 
spreads 
have 
remained 
around 
7 
percent 
for 
the  past 
decade, 
symptomatic 
of 
15 
Page  24
inefficiencies 
and 
portfolio 
problems. 
As 
de 
facto 
industry 
leaders, 
the 
NCBs 
need 
these 
spreads 
to 
survive. 
But 
while 
the 
high 
spreads 
allow 
the 
more 
efficient 
foreign 
and domestic banks 
to 
earn handsome 
profits, 
they 
are 
still 
inadequate 
to 
cover 
the 
provisioning 
expenses 
of 
the 
NCBs 
and 
SDBs. 
The 
weak 
and 
inefficient 
financial 
system 
limits 
access 
to 
credit,  raises 
interest 
rates (real 
borrowing 
rates 
have 
exceeded 
10 
percent 
in 
certain 
years), 
and 
acts 
as 
a 
serious 
drag 
on 
economic 
growth. 
A 
recent 
World 
Bank 
study 
estimated 
that 
if 
financial 
sector 
inefficiencies 
had 
been 
removed 
20 years 
ago, 
real 
GDP 
per 
capita 
would 
have 
been 
15-37 percent 
higher 
by 
1995.'' 
46. 
Soon 
after  talung  office, 
the 
Government 
began 
dealing 
with 
the 
problems 
confronting 
the 
financial 
sector 
by 
appointing 
a 
Banking 
Reform 
Committee, 
which 
has 
recently 
provided 
Government 
with 
its 
recommendations. 
A 
key 
priority 
identified 
is 
to 
strengthen 
the 
central bank's oversight 
over 
the 
bankmg 
system 
and 
give 
it 
autonomy 
over 
monetary 
and  exchange 
rate policies. 
Accordingly, 
the 
Bangladesh 
Bank 
Order 
of 
1972 
has 
been 
amended. 
The 
Bankmg 
Companies 
Act 
of 
1991 and

(Nationalization)

have also

that 
kept

and

outside

Bangladesh 
Bank 
has 
been

and 
systems 
and

with

47. 
Bangladesh 
Bank 
has 
issued 
Circulars 
to 
(i)

(ii)

in

of 
20% 
by 
requiring

for

Minimum

are 
being 
increased 
from 
Tk.200 
million 
(US$3

to 
Tk. 
1 
billion 
(US$17

The

abolished 
four

Savings 
Certificate 
schemes 
that 
paid

instruments attractive

Government

to 
the 
remaining

to

The 
Money 
Laundering Prevention 
Act, 
2002, 
has 
been 
enacted 
to 
check

transactions. 
48. 
To

a

to

New

prudential

bank-by-bank

on

and

with

for

About

countries

In

to

customers’ 
demands. 
60. 
The 
main 
sector 
issues 
can 
be 
classified 
under 
two 
broad 
categories: 
(a) 
sector 
performance, 
and 
(b) 
competition 
and 
private 
investment 
environment. 
Poor 
sector 
performance 
is 
evidenced 
by 
(i) 
limited 
access 
to 
telecommunication 
services, 
(ii) 
low 
rural 
teledensity, 
and 
(iii) 
low 
BTTB 
productivity 
and 
performance. 
Limited 
competition 
and 
private 
investment 
have 
taken  place 
in 
the 
sector, 
but 
the 
environment 
has 
not 
been 
conducive 
to 
large 
scale 
investments. 
Some 
of 
the factors that have 
hindered 
competition and private 
investment 
include 
lack 
of 
separation 
in 
the 
policy 
and 
operational 
roles 
of 
government, 
limited 
opening 
of 
the market,  inadequate 
interconnection 
capacity, 
distorted tariffs,  and 
inadequate 
regulatory 
framework. 
61. 
The 
Government 
has 
undertaken 
several 
reform 
initiatives 
over 
the 
past 
few 
years 
to 
increase 
competition 
in 
the 
sector 
and 
improve 
regulation. 
These 
include: 
adoption 
of 
the 
National 
Telecommunications 
Policy 
of 
1998, 
which 
recognizes 
competition 
and 
private participation 
as 
the 
principal 
means 
of 
promoting 
improved 
sector 
performance;  the  establishment 
of 
the  Bangladesh 
Telecommunications 
Regulatory  Commission 
(BTRC) 
in 
January 
2002, 
as 
stipulated 
in 
the 
Telecommunications 
Act 
of 
2001; 
and 
the 
adoption 
of 
the 
National Information 
and Communications 
Technology 
(ICT) 
Policy 
in 
September 2002, 
which 
aspires 
to 
use 
ICT 
as 
a 
key 
engine 
of 
economic 
growth. 
The 
ICT 
policy 
calls 
for 
accelerated 
deregulation 
of 
and 
private  participation 
in 
the 
telecommunications 
sector. 
At 
the 
direction 
of 
the 
ICT 
Task 
Force, 
a 
high-level 
committee 
has 
been 
established 
to 
recommend 
to 
Government 
the 
most 
appropriate 
options and 
an 
implementation 
timetable 
for 
BTTB’s 
restructuring. 
Key 
objectives 
of 
the 
restructuring 
are 
to 
create 
a 
level playing 
field 
within 
the 
sector 
by 
separating the Government’s 
policy 
function 
from 
operations 
and 
to 
empower 
BTTB 
to 
operate 
as 
a 
commercial 
enterprise. 
In 
addition, 
the 
Government 
has 
decided 
to 
introduce 
competition 
in-local 
and 
long 
distance services 
by 
licensing 
private 
operators. 
The 
Government 
has 
recognized 
the 
need 
to 
strengthen 
BTRC’s 
regulatory 
capacity 
to 
ensure 
a 
level playing 
field 
in 
a 
multi-operator 
environment. 
The 
Government 
has 
sought 
IDA’S 
assistance 
to 
finance technical 
assistance 
to help 
implementation 
of 
these 
reforms. 
There 
has 
been 
dramatic 
growth 
in 
the 
mobile 
telephone 
market 
- 
although cellular 
phones were 
only 
introduced 
in 
the 
1990s, 
mobile 
phone penetration 
exceeded 
fixed 
line 
penetration 
by 
2001. 
According 
to 
the 
investment 
climate 
survey 
mentioned 
above, 
the 
median 
waiting 
time 
for 
a 
cellular 
phone 
now 
is 
only 
one day 
and 
most 
companies 
no 
longer rate telecommunications 
as 
a 
major 
problem. 
B. 
The 
Governance 
Agenda 
62. 
Poor 
governance 
-- 
which 
manifests 
itself 
in 
several forms, such 
as 
poor public 
service 
delivery, 
mis-procurement,  corruption, 
extortion 
and 
toll 
takmg 
-- 
adversely affects 
the

climate 
and 
costs 
Bangladesh 
between 
2 and 
3 
percentage

in 
lost 
economic growth”. 
According 
to 
several

reports 
and

the

for

in 
Bangladesh 
is

as

Corruption

Property

are 
relatively 
insecure, 
and 
utility

and hook- 
ups 
are 
extremely problematic. 
Lack 
of

of

absence 
of 
effective 
mechanisms 
for holding public

accountable, 
ineffective 
appeal 
l7 
Corruption 
in 
Bangladesh, 
Costs 
and Cures,

Bank, 
April 
2000. 
’* 
Bangladesh: 
Improving

for  Reducing Poverty,

This

A

mechanisms and an inadequate

63, 
The 
current Government’s

The

observes 
that  “poor  govemance 
is

to 
current

Whereas

a

aimed

strengthening 
the

of 
integrity” 
is 
being 
implemented. 
Improving 
Financial Management 
64. 
The 
Country Financial 
Accountability 
Assessment 
(CFAA) 
and 
other 
diagnostic 
studies 
reveal 
that 
the 
lack 
of 
financial 
accountability 
in 
Bangladesh 
is 
particularly 
acute, 
and 
the 
chain 
of 
accountability 
between 
the 
public, 
the legislature, 
and 
the 
executive 
needs 
considerable 
strengthening. 
There 
are 
several 
issues 
that 
deserve 
to 
be 
highlighted: 
. 
For 
several 
years 
the 
annual reports 
of 
the 
Comptroller 
and 
Auditor 
General 
(C&AG) 
have 
repeatedly 
documented 
waste, 
fraud, 
and 
the 
abuse 
of 
public 
funds, 
as 
has 
the 
Public 
Accounts 
Committee 
in 
parliament, 
with 
little 
corrective action. 
. 
Although 
the 
Constitution 
stipulates 
that 
the 
C&AG’s 
office 
is 
independent 
of 
government, 
its 
report 
is 
first 
submitted 
to 
the 
Prime 
Minister’s 
office, 
an 
auditee. 
While 
this 
is 
inconsistent 
with 
the 
notion 
of 
an independent 
supreme 
audit 
and 
in 
contrast 
to 
practice 
in 
countries 
with 
effective 
public 
audits, 
Government believes that 
this 
is 
simply 
a procedural 
step 
similar 
to 
that 
followed 
in 
many 
parts 
of 
South Asia. 
The 
C&AG’s 
report 
is 
automatically 
passed 
on 
by 
the 
Prime 
Minister to 
the 
President, 
with 
minimal 
delay. 
There 
is 
concem 
within 
the 
office 
of 
C&AG 
that 
it 
is 
treated 
as 
a 
Division 
of 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance, 
with 
the 
C&AG 
having 
inadequate 
control 
over 
his 
staff 
for 
appointments 
and 
promotions. 
His 
budget 
is 
subject 
to 
the 
approval 
of 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance, 
whereas 
in 
many  countries 
this 
is 
determined 
by 
the 
Public 
Accounts 
Committee 
of 
Parliament. 
Government believes that 
the 
budget 
provided 
to 
the 
C&AG 
has 
never 
been 
a 
binding 
constraint and 
has 
to 
be 
part 
of 
the 
overall 
fiscal 
framework, 
as 
is 
the 
case 
with 
the 
budget 
provided 
to 
the Supreme Court, hence 
the 
involvement 
of 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance. 
The 
C&AGs 
that 
have been appointed 
in 
recent 
years 
have been 
senior 
civil 
servants 
close 
to 
the 
age 
of 
retirement and have 
not 
been 
in 
place 
long 
enough 
to 
follow 
up 
on 
the 
financial 
management 
reform 
agenda. 
. 
.

Page  30
. 
The 
C&AG's 
audit 
staff 
are 
drawn 
from 
the 
same 
cadre 
as 
the accounts 
staff 
and 
trained 
auditors 
can 
be 
transferred 
back 
into 
accounting 
posts, 
creating

of

and  perceptions 
of 
lack 
of 
audit 
independen~e.'~ 
Although 
separation was

in 
1983 
(Ordinance 
XXVIII), 
it 
was 
never 
implemented, 
largely 
due 
to

from

who 
fear 
loss 
of 
opportunities 
of 
transfer 
into 
coveted audit 
posts, 
which 
have 
more travel 
and 
promotion 
prospects. 
In 
addition, 
internal 
control 
climate 
is 
poor, 
as 
reported 
by 
the 
C&AG 
repeatedly 
in 
his 
reports. 
Internal 
audit 
is 
still 
at 
an 
early 
stage 
and 
exists 
only 
in 
large 
ministries 
and 
major 
autonomous organizations, and 
generally 
only 
pre-audit functions 
are 
carried 
out. 
Elections 
were 
held 
in 
October 2001, 
but 
parliamentary 
standing committees have 
not 
yet 
been 
formed. 
. 
65. 
An 
IMF 
Safeguard 
Assessment, 
conducted 
in 
September 
2002, 
concluded 
that 
financial 
management 
in 
Bangladesh 
Bank 
was 
weak 
and comprehensive 
reforms 
were  necessary 
to 
improve 
accounting, 
financial 
reporting, 
auditing 
and 
control 
systems 
as 
well 
as 
the 
legal 
framework. 
The 
Assessment 
also 
noted 
that 
the 
accounting 
department 
did 
not 
have 
sufficiently qualified 
staff 
and 
the 
financial 
statements 
that 
were 
prepared 
contained 
limited 
information 
on 
cash 
flows, accounting policies, 
disclosure 
of 
government 
balances, 
contingent 
liabilities, 
and 
foreign liabilities. 
66. 
Since 
takmg 
office, 
the 
Government 
has 
made 
encouraging 
progress 
in
