several 
CFAA

through 
a

Management 
Reform 
Program 
(FMRP), 
supported 
by 
UK- 
DFID 
and 
the 
Netherlands. 
Implementation 
of

recommendations 
to

institutional

of 
the

of 
C&AG 
is 
also

through

The

and

are 
being 
separated. 
The 
Controller 
General 
of 
Accounts 
(CGA) 
no 
longer reports 
to 
the 
C&AG. 
From 
July 
1, 
2002, 
positions 
of 
Chief 
Accounts 
Officers 
(CAOs) 
were 
created 
in 
each 
Ministry 
to

directly

the

the

reports 
ultimately 
to 
the

Secretary. 
Internal 
audit 
teams are 
being 
piloted 
in 
a 
limited 
number 
of 
ministries. 
To 
assure 
quality 
of 
accounting 
data, 
a Central 
Reconciliation 
Unit 
has 
been 
established 
in

office. 
This 
unit 
carries 
out

of 
the 
checks 
issued 
electronically 
by 
the 
CAOs 
based 
in 
Dhaka 
and 
paid 
by 
Bangladesh 
Bank. 
To 
facilitate 
the 
flow 
of 
information 
on 
Government 
receipts,  expenditures 
and 
borrowing 
position, 
the

report 
their 
individual 
transactions 
by 
the 
banks 
to 
the 
Finance 
Division 
on 
a 
weekly 
basis. 
Field 
accounting 
offices 
have 
been reorganized 
through 
the 
abolition 
of 
20 
Regional 
Accounts 
Offices 
and 
the

of 
6

Offices. 
Information

the 
Thana 
and 
district 
level 
is 
now 
monitored 
and consolidated 
by 
the

Divisional 
Controllers 
and electronically 
transferred 
to 
the 
Central 
Data 
Processing 
Unit 
of 
the 
CGA 
and 
from 
there 
to 
the

Division. 
On- 
line 
electronic 
data 
transmission 
now 
allows 
the 
central government 
accounts 
to be compiled 
and 
reported 
within 
five 
weeks 
of 
the end 
of 
each 
month. 
A

is 
under 
way to 
complete 
the

of 
accounts 
in 
all 
districts. 
This 
will

officers 
to 
check

of

the statements 
of 
Bangladesh 
Bank 
on

Overhauling 
Procurement 
68. 
The 
integrity 
of 
public 
procurement 
is 
closely 
linked to 
financial 
accountability. 
The 
public 
sector 
in 
Bangladesh annually 
procures about 
US$3 
billion 
worth 
of 
goods, 
works 
and 
services. 
The 
Bangladesh 
Country 
Procurement 
Assessment 
Review 
(CPAR)20 observed that 
public 
sector 
procurement 
suffers 
from 
systemic deficiencies 
including 
opaque 
procurement 
practices, outdated 
procurement 
rules 
and 
procedures, 
inadequate  management  capacity, 
and 
lack 
of 
transparency 
and 
accountability 
mechanisms. 
Furthermore, 
public 
procurement 
has 
been 
infested 
by 
entrenched 
and pervasive 
rent- 
seeking 
and 
corruption. 
Survey 
work 
shows that economic 
losses 
due 
to 
procurement-related 
corruption 
and delays 
are 
significant. 
The situation 
leads 
to 
the waste 
of 
scarce 
public 
resources, 
be 
it 
domestic 
savings 
or 
external 
aid, 
and 
poor quality 
of 
goods, 
works 
and 
services. 
At 
a 
more 
fundamental 
level, 
it 
causes 
erosion 
of 
public 
confidence and 
deterioration 
of 
the 
investment climate. 
Thus, 
reform to 
address 
the 
underlying 
causes 
of 
poor 
procurement 
is 
high 
on 
the 
country’s 
governance 
agenda. 
69. 
Recognizing 
the 
critical 
need 
to 
improve 
public 
procurement, 
the Government 
is 
in 
the process 
of 
implementing, 
with 
the support 
of 
an 
IDA 
credit, 
the 
recommendations 
of 
the 
CPAR 
by 
creating 
basic 
policy 
and institutional 
frameworks 
in 
the 
medium 
term, 
through 
(a) 
reforming 
the procurement 
rules 
and 
mandatory 
use 
of 
standard 
bid 
documents 
to 
reduce 
procurement 
delays; 
(b) 
introducing 
mechanisms 
to 
ensure 
transparency 
in 
procurement  process 
and 
restore 
public 
confidence; 
and 
(c) 
establishing 
procurement training, 
monitoring 
and evaluation capacity 
to 
track 
procurement performance 
of 
public 
sector 
entities. 
70. 
A 
Central 
Procurement 
Technical 
Unit 
has 
been 
established  and 
is 
scheduled 
to 
submit 
to 
Government 
the 
final 
draft 
procurement 
rules 
and 
standard 
bidding 
documents 
by 
May 
31, 
2003. 
New 
streamlined procurement approval 
procedures, 
with 
significantly 
reduced 
number 
of 
layers, have been 
developed. 
Government 
has 
issued orders 
mandating 
publication 
of 
awards 
for 
contracts 
of 
US$200,000 
and 
above 
and 
has 
started 
actual 
publication. 
To 
improve 
procurement 
management  capacity, 
Government 
initiated 
an 
intensive 
Training 
of 
Trainers 
program 
in 
February 
2003, 
and 
1,600 
persons 
are 
expected 
to 
be 
trained over 
the 
next 
three 
years. 
Establishing 
an 
Independent 
Anti-Corruption 
Commission 
7 
1. 
The Bureau 
of 
Anti-Corruption (BAC) 
is 
the 
principal 
organization responsible 
for 
investigating 
allegations 
of 
corruption 
in 
Bangladesh 
and 
following 
inquiry 
with 
legal 
action.21 
Ever 
since  the 
introduction 
of 
the 
parliamentary 
system 
in 
Bangladesh, 
BAC 
has 
remained under 
the 
control 
of 
the 
Prime 
Minister’s 
office, 
prior 
to 
which 
it 
reported 
to 
the 
office 
of 
the President. 
Between 
1994 
and 
2000, 
BAC 
received 
a 
total 
number 
of 
70,201 
complaints, 
resulting 
in 
cases 
being 
filed 
for 
9 percent 
of 
the 
complaints 
and 
files 
being 
“closed” 
for 
an 
additional 
32 
percent, 
where 
the 
allegations 
could 
not 
be 
substantiated. 
Fifty 
nine 
percent 
of 
the 
complaints 
are 
pending.22 
72. 
Numerous 
observers have expressed reservations about 
BAC’s 
operations. 
Among 
the 
factors 
affecting 
its 
operations, 
an important 
constraint 
is 
the 
excessive 
political 
interference 
in 
the Bureau’s 
2o 
Bangladesh: Country Procurement 
Assessment, 
World 
Bank, 
December 1999. 
” 
The 
Bureau 
operates 
under 
the 
provisions 
of 
the 
Prevention 
of 
Corruption Act 
1947, 
the 
Anti-Corruption 
Act 
1957, 
the 
22 
For a 
more detailed discussion 
see 
Bureau 
of 
Anti Corruption 
- 
Prime 
Minister’s 
O#ce: 
Fact Finding 
Report, 
Transparency 
Criminal Law 
Amendment 
Act 
1958, 
and 
the 
Manual 
of 
the 
Bureau 
of 
Anti 
Corruption 
1970. 
Intemational 
Bangladesh, 
December 
200 
1. 
23 
Page  32
functioning. 
Charges 
are 
never 
brought 
against 
persons 
affiliated 
with 
the 
ruling 
party, 
as 
prior 
permission 
for 
this 
is 
required 
from 
the 
Prime 
Minister. 
The authority 
of 
the 
Bureau’s 
Director 
General 
in 
conducting 
cases 
against 
senior 
government 
officials 
is 
severely 
limited, 
causing 
the 
Public 
Administration 
Reform 
Commission 
(PARC) 
to 
compare 
BAC’s 
operations 
to 
a person “who 
has 
been 
asked 
to 
swim 
with 
tied 
hands”.23 
Excessively 
bureaucratic procedures, 
lack 
of 
proper 
planning 
and 
investigation, 
weak 
framing 
of 
cases, 
poorly 
trained 
and 
negligent 
officials, 
and 
corruption 
are 
some 
of 
the 
causes 
that have 
been 
identified for 
the 
Bureau’s 
poor 
performance. 
To 
address these 
causes, 
the 
PARC 
report 
had 
recommended 
the establishment 
of 
an 
independent 
Anti-Corruption 
Commission 
vested 
with 
the authority 
to 
investigate allegations 
of 
corruption 
against 
all 
officers and 
citizens 
of 
the 
country, 
irrespective 
of 
rank 
and 
position. 
Consistent 
with 
its 
election 
manifesto, 
the 
Government 
has 
reaffirmed 
this 
intention, 
and 
Cabinet 
has 
approved a 
draft 
bill 
approving 
the 
establishment 
of 
this body. 
It 
is 
intended 
that the 
commission 
will 
report 
annually 
to 
parliament. 
Members 
of 
the 
commission 
would 
be 
appointed 
for 
a 
fixed four 
year 
nonrenewable 
term 
and 
could 
only 
be 
removed 
from 
office 
in 
instances 
of 
abuse 
of 
power 
or corruption, 
following 
the recommendations 
of 
a 
judicial 
committee. 
Initiating 
Public 
Administration Reform 
73. 
The 
problems 
afflicting 
Bangladesh’s 
public 
administration 
are 
well 
known 
and  have 
been 
discussed 
widely. 
As 
stated 
in 
the 
Public 
Administration Reforms 
Commission24 (PARC) 
report, 
the 
system 
of 
public 
administration 
is 
“rigid, 
unresponsive, 
inefficient 
and 
ineffective,  pre-occupied 
with 
process rather 
than 
results”. 
Although 
the 
total 
size 
of 
the 
civil 
service 
as 
a 
proportion 
of 
population 
(about 
1 
percent) 
is 
less 
than 
the 
average 
for 
South 
Asia 
and 
most 
developing 
and 
developed countries, 
there 
is 
excess 
manpower 
at 
lower 
levels 
(Class 
111 
& 
Class 
IV 
employees) 
and a 
shortage 
of 
skilled 
civil 
servants 
at 
higher 
levels. 
Hiring 
decisions, 
particularly 
at 
lower 
levels, 
are 
influenced 
by 
political 
pressures, 
and 
recruitment 
has 
historically 
been 
based 
on 
a 
geographic  quota  system 
with 
special 
preferences 
given 
to 
specific 
groups 
of 
candidates. 
Promotions 
are 
perceived 
as 
being 
based 
on 
time 
served 
and good 
contacts, 
and 
there 
has 
been erosion 
in 
terms 
of 
the package 
of 
financial 
benefits 
offered 
to 
public 
servants.25 
74. 
While 
public 
administration 
reform 
has 
not 
yet 
started 
in 
earnest, 
the 
Government 
has 
taken a 
few 
initial 
steps 
aimed 
at 
improving 
the 
system. 
All 
routine recruitment 
against vacant posts at 
lower 
levels 
in 
administration 
has 
been 
stopped, 
although recruitment 
is 
continuing 
for 
critical 
posts such 
as 
doctors, 
nurses 
and 
teachers. 
To 
deal 
with 
the 
acute 
skills 
shortage, 
Government 
has 
started 
recruiting 
senior 
managers 
on 
contract 
from 
the 
private 
sector 
and 
from 
abroad. 
These 
include 
the 
Governor 
of 
Bangladesh Bank, the 
Chairman 
of 
the 
Tariff 
Commission, 
Member 
Planning Commission 
and Chairman 
of 
the 
Board 
of-Investment. 
Efforts 
are also 
underway 
to 
tighten criteria 
for 
recruitment 
and 
promotion. 
In 
addition 
to 
seniority, 
Government 
has 
issued 
new 
guidelines 
that 
require 
taking 
academic 
qualifications, 
negative 
remarks 
or 
reports 
about 
individual 
staff 
behavior 
and 
performance, 
and 
average 
scores 
from 
the 
five 
preceding 
annual evaluation reports 
into 
consideration 
when 
making 
promotion 
decisions.

Report 
of

Separating 
the 
Judiciary 
from 
the Executive 
75. 
Separation 
of 
judiciary 
from 
executive 
control 
is 
an 
important 
element 
in 
establishing 
the 
proper 
rule 
of 
law 
and 
improving 
governance, 
and Bangladesh’s 
political 
parties have 
all long 
supported 
this 
objective. 
The 
present  Govemment 
has  also 
expressed 
its 
commitment 
to 
complete 
the  separation 
process. 
The Appellate 
Division 
of 
the 
Supreme 
Court, 
through 
a 
judgment 
passed 
on 
December 
2, 
1999, 
identified 
five 
conditions 
of 
judicial 
independence: 
(i) 
security 
of 
tenure; 
(ii) 
security 
of 
salary; 
(iii) 
institutional 
independence 
of 
the 
Subordinate 
Judiciary 
(from 
the 
District 
Court 
to 
the 
Assistant 
Judge’s 
Court) 
from 
the 
Parliament 
and 
the 
Executive; 
(iv) 
recruitment 
to 
Judicial 
service 
by 
a 
separate 
Judicial 
Service 
Commission; 
and 
(v) 
financial 
independence 
of 
the 
Supreme Court, 
i.e. 
freedom 
to 
spend 
funds 
allocated 
to 
it 
in 
the 
budget 
without 
the 
approval 
of 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance 
or 
any other 
ministry. 
76. 
The 
Govemment 
has 
already delegated 
full 
financial 
autonomy 
to 
the 
Supreme 
Court 
to 
incur 
any 
expenditure 
from 
the 
funds 
allocated 
to 
it 
in 
the 
budget. 
Judge’s 
security 
of 
tenure 
and 
salary have 
been 
ensured, 
once 
they 
are appointed. 
At 
present, 
the 
Supreme 
Court 
and 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Law 
have 
dual 
control over 
disciplinary 
and 
promotion 
processes 
for 
the 
Subordinate 
Court 
judges. 
The 
Government 
has 
agreed 
to 
implement 
the 
Supreme 
Court 
directives 
but 
needs 
more 
time 
to 
resolve 
issues 
relating 
to 
the 
services 
of 
magistrates, 
who 
are 
part 
of 
the 
Civil 
Service  and 
under 
the 
control 
of 
Ministry 
of 
Establishment. 
Since 
the 
Supreme 
Court’s judgment, 
the 
Establishment 
Division 
has 
referred 
transfer 
and 
discipline 
matters 
of 
full-time 
(judicial) 
Magistrates 
in 
the 
Metropolitan 
Magistrates’  Courts 
to 
the 
Supreme 
Court. 
However, 
civil 
administrative 
officers 
who 
perform 
judicial 
functions 
as 
part-time 
Magistrates 
are 
still 
under 
the 
control 
of 
the 
Establishment 
Division. 
The 
Government 
is 
considering 
ways 
to 
address 
this 
issue. 
Cleaning 
the 
Natural 
Environment 
77. 
The 
Government 
is 
currently 
engaged 
in 
a 
number 
of 
programs 
aimed 
at strengthening capacity 
for 
environmental 
management, 
including 
the 
UNDP-supported 
Sustainable 
Environment 
Management 
Program, 
the 
CIDA-supported 
Bangladesh 
Environmental 
Management 
Program, 
and 
the 
IDA-supported 
Air 
Quality 
Management  Project. 
These 
initiatives 
support 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
National 
Environmental 
Management 
Action 
Plan 
and 
assist 
the 
Department 
of 
Environment 
in 
the 
administration 
of 
its 
regulatory 
program 
of 
environmental 
assessments, 
pollution 
control, 
and 
monitoring 
of 
environmental 
quality. 
78. 
Particular 
emphasis 
has 
been 
given 
to 
the 
control 
of 
urban air pollution, 
and 
some success 
has 
been achieved 
in 
this 
regard. 
High 
levels 
of 
vehicular 
air 
pollution 
have 
repeatedly 
been 
identified 
as 
a 
major 
health 
hazard 
in 
Dhaka. 
Govemment 
has 
banned 
the 
movement 
of 
buses 
more 
than 
20 
years 
old 
and 
trucks 
more than 
25 years 
old on 
the 
streets 
of 
Dhaka, 
starting 
in 
January 2002. 
Most 
of 
these 
buses 
and trucks, 
many 
of 
which 
were 
owned 
by 
senior 
police 
officials, 
were 
heavily 
polluting 
and were 
rarely 
legally 
registered. 
This 
ban 
resulted 
in 
the 
withdrawal 
of 
about 
1,000 
buses 
from 
the 
streets 
of 
Dhaka 
and 
has 
had 
a 
positive impact. 
The Govemment 
has also 
banned 
the 
movement 
of 
all 
two 
stroke 
three 
wheelers (baby-taxis) 
registered 
prior 
to 
1993 
on 
accounts 
of 
their 
pollution, 
removing 
about 
14,000 
baby 
taxis 
and 
2,000 
tempos. 
Private 
transport  operators 
have 
imported 
about 
2,000 
improved 
(four 
stroke 
engine) 
baby 
taxis 
to 
replace 
the 
old 
models. 
Under 
the 
World 
Bank 
supported 
Dhaka 
Urban 
Transport 
Project, extensive 
dialogue 
has 
taken 
place 
between 
rickshaw 
owners, 
drivers 
association 
and 
the 
Dhaka 
Metropolitan 
Police 
(DMP), 
aimed 
at 
malung 
several 
major 
bus 
routes 
free 
of 
non-motorized 
transport, 
which would 
further 
limit 
congestion, 
and 
thereby reduce 
pollution. 
25 
Page  34
C. 
The 
Human 
Development Agenda 
79. 
In 
the 
area 
of 
human development, safety 
net 
provision 
and disaster 
management, 
Bangladesh 
is 
building 
on 
the 
progress  already  achieved. 
The 
expansion 
of 
coverage  and 
access 
to 
education, 
encouraging 
record 
of 
improvements 
in 
health 
outcomes and 
family 
planning 
as 
well 
as 
improved 
disaster 
preparedness 
and 
flood 
relief 
(discussed 
in 
Section 
I1 
above) 
were 
made 
possible 
by 
the 
shift 
in 
public 
policy 
toward 
the development 
of 
human 
resources and 
the 
rural 
economy. 
These 
successes 
must 
be 
balanced 
against 
the 
remaining 
serious 
human 
development challenges the 
country 
faces. 
While 
primary 
school 
gross 
enrollment 
rates 
are 
close 
to 
100 
percent, 
attendance 
rate 
is 
only 
60-65 
percent 
and 
primary 
cycle completion rate 
is 
67 
percent. 
Adult 
literacy 
rate remains 
lower 
than 
the 
South 
Asia 
regional 
average. 
Maternal 
mortality 
is 
very 
high 
(392 
per 
100,000 
live 
births), 
child 
malnutrition 
is 
among the 
highest 
in 
the 
world, 
and 
there 
are 
lingering 
problems 
of 
low 
birth 
weight 
and 
persistence 
of 
infectious 
diseases. 
80. 
Public 
spending 
in 
the 
social 
sectors 
- 
which 
takes 
more 
than a 
quarter 
of 
the budget 
--meets the 
minimum 
standards 
of 
efficiency 
and 
equity, 
and 
tends 
to 
be 
directed 
to 
poorer 
regions.26 
Education 
is 
the 
largest 
recipient 
of 
public 
spending 
in 
Bangladesh 
(2.3 
percent 
of 
GDP) 
and 
basic education 
receives 
an 
overwhelming 
share 
of 
the 
total 
education 
spending. 
Health 
and 
the 
social 
safety 
nets 
each take 
up 
about 
1 
percent 
of 
GDP. 
Spending 
on 
the 
Essential 
Services 
Package 
(ESP) 
accounts 
for 
60 percent 
of 
the 
total 
health 
budget 
with 
26 percent 
for 
reproductive 
health 
and 
21 
percent 
for 
child 
health. 
The 
two 
main 
relief 
programs-Gratuitous 
Relief 
and 
Vulnerable 
Group 
Feeding-played 
an 
important 
role 
in 
assisting 
flood-affected 
households 
during 
the 
massive 
flood 
in 
1998. 
The 
country’s 
two 
main 
development 
programs-Vulnerable 
Group Development 
and 
Food 
For 
Work-target 
the 
poor relatively 
well. 
On 
the 
account 
of 
low 
revenue 
mobilization, 
however, even 
though 
the 
share 
of 
social 
sector 
public 
spending 
is 
high, 
Bangladesh’s 
public 
spending 
per 
capita 
($12) 
falls 
short 
of 
such 
neighboring 
countries 
as 
India 
($21) 
and 
Sri 
Lanka 
($37). 
Due 
to 
weak institutions 
of 
service 
delivery, even 
though 
the 
intra- 
and 
inter- 
sectoral 
allocation 
of 
expenditures 
have 
many pro-poor 
attributes, the 
effectiveness 
of 
these 
expenditures 
is 
below 
the 
full 
potential 
and 
resources 
reaching 
the beneficiaries 
fall 
short 
of 
the 
level 
intended. 
8 
1. 
There 
is 
consensus 
that 
institutional 
and 
governance 
reforms 
are 
the 
key 
for 
improving 
access, 
quality 
and 
efficiency 
of 
education 
and 
health 
services, 
as 
well 
as 
targeting and 
effectiveness 
of 
the 
safety 
net. 
In 
terms 
of 
public 
expenditure 
priorities, 
the 
education 
sector 
is 
to 
receive 
increased 
allocation, 
while 
continuing 
emphasis 
on 
basic 
education 
and 
improvements 
in 
its 
quality 
and 
equity through 
rebalancing 
expenditure 
on 
labor 
and 
non-labor 
inputs 
and expanding 
involvement 
of 
NGOs 
and 
private 
sector 
in 
provision 
of 
education 
services. 
Also 
in 
the 
health 
sector, 
the 
priority 
is 
to 
allocate 
more 
funds 
on 
ESP 
and capitalize 
on 
the 
existing potential 
for 
greater 
involvement 
of 
the 
private 
sector, 
NGOs, 
and 
grass- 
roots community 
organizations 
by 
de-linhng 
the 
Government’s 
provider 
and 
financier 
functions. 
The 
Government 
is 
now 
focusing 
on 
a 
second generation 
set 
of 
reforms 
to 
address

shortcomings. 
(Annex

broad 
reforms aimed 
at: 
With

for 
Increasing

both

in

for

Simultaneously,

of

will

to

greater

Improving 
standards 
for 
teacher 
recruitment  and training. 
An 
autonomous Teacher 
Recruitment 
Commission 
will 
be 
established 
to

for

Schools 
will

the

Simultaneously,

will

and become

opportunity

in

. 
A

9-10. 
These

Adjustment 
Credit,

The

Over

85. 
In 
the 
health 
sector, 
the 
consortium 
of 
donors 
has 
been 
supporting Government’s 
Health 
and 
Population 
Sector 
Program 
(HPSP) 
since 
its

in 
1998 
(approximately 
$800 
million 
over 5 
years). 
The 
HPSP 
was 
prepared  based 
on 
a 
sector-wide 
program 
approach 
including 
integrating  financial 
management and 
streamlining  procurement 
systems. 
This 
has 
led 
to 
prioritization 
of 
expenditures 
and 
secured 
allocation 
for 
cost 
effective 
programs, 
especially 
those 
which primarily 
benefit 
children, 
women 
and 
the 
poor, 
and 
has 
considerably reduced 
the 
transaction 
costs 
of 
both 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Health 
and 
Family 
Welfare and 
the 
development 
partners. 
Included 
in 
the 
HPSP 
was 
a 
substantial 
reform 
agenda 
to 
improve 
the 
efficiency 
and 
effectiveness 
of 
public 
service 
provision. 
The 
reforms  included 
de- 
concentration 
of 
line 
authority 
to 
the 
district 
and 
health 
facility 
level, 
unification 
of 
the 
separate 
family 
planning 
and 
health 
wings 
of 
the service 
delivery 
system 
at 
the 
thana 
level 
and below, 
development 
and 
integration 
of 
gender 
equity 
concerns 
and 
institutionalization 
of 
stakeholder 
participation 
and 
community 
oversight 
of 
the 
program. 
While 
there 
is 
progress 
on 
most 
of 
the 
indicators 
of 
health 
outcomes, 
several 
aspects 
of 
the 
reform 
agenda 
still 
remain 
to 
be 
implemented. 
86. 
The

The

is

and

of

Donors

(agreed 
under 
the

in

of

in 
the 
sector.

Page  36
87. 
To 
strengthen 
the

of

has,

(FFE)

- 
in-kmd

-

replaced 
it 
by 
an expanded 
Primary 
Education

Program

Under 
this 
new 
program, 
the 
bottom 
40

in

month for

one

per

if

more than 
one 
child. 
The

number 
of 
likely 
beneficiaries 
is 
estimated 
at 
7 
million 
children, 
and the 
net additional 
burden 
on 
the 
budget 
as 
a 
result 
of 
this 
program 
is 
estimated at about $35-40 
million 
annually. 
Experience 
from 
the 
Government’s 
female 
stipend 
program 
in 
secondary education, 
which 
is 
operated 
on 
a

suggests 
that 
the

are 
lower 
than 
in-kind 
food 
programs,

will

to

The

in

(FFW)

88. 
Another terrible 
aspect 
of 
vulnerability 
- 
acid-attacks 
on 
women 
- 
experienced 
a 
surge 
in 
recent 
years, 
and there 
were 
no 
laws 
and regulations 
for 
handling 
acid-related violence. 
Two 
Acts 
to 
deal 
with 
this 
issue 
have 
been 
approved 
by 
Parliament. 
The 
Acid 
Control 
Act 
controls 
the 
import, marketing 
and 
production 
of 
acid, 
while 
the 
Acid 
Related 
Offense 
Bill 
empowers 
the 
judiciary to 
prosecute, 
under 
strict 
time 
limits, 
offenses 
relating 
to 
acid 
throwing.  The 
Bill 
also makes 
provision for 
the 
rehabilitation and 
treatment 
of 
acid 
burn 
victims. 
VI. 
THE 
BANK’S 
STRATEGY 
IN 
BANGLADESH 
Overall 
CAS 
Context 
89. 
The Bank’s country 
assistance 
strategy 
(CAS) 
for 
Bangladesh 
for 
FYO1-03 
identifies 
four 
main 
thrusts 
for 
the 
Bank 
Group’s 
assistance 
to 
Bangladesh. 
These 
include 
(i) 
helping 
to 
build 
stronger 
institutions 
and 
governance 
across sectors; 
(ii) 
consolidating 
gains 
in 
human 
development 
and supporting 
initiatives 
to 
address 
the 
next 
generation 
of 
development  challenges 
in 
education, 
health 
and 
nutrition; 
(iii) 
implementing 
an 
integrated 
approach 
to 
rural 
development, 
including 
supporting 
growth 
in 
agriculture 
and 
non-farm 
activities, 
making 
opportunities and 
assets 
available 
to 
the 
poor 
and 
improving 
rural 
infrastructure; 
and 
(iv) 
accelerating and 
broadening 
private 
sector-led 
growth 
by 
helping 
to 
remove 
structural  impediments 
and 
establish 
an 
environment  conducive 
to 
private 
investment, 
strengthen 
the 
financial 
sector, 
and 
support 
private 
investments 
in 
energy, 
infrastructure, 
manufacturing and 
services. 
90. 
In 
particular, 
the 
CAS 
notes 
that 
“unless 
the 
underlying institutional 
and 
governance 
issues 
are 
addressed, 
neither 
foreign 
nor 
domestically 
fimded 
programs 
will 
achieve 
their potential 
for 
accelerating 
economic 
growth 
and 
reducing 
poverty.” 
In 
this 
regard, 
it 
stresses 
the 
need 
for 
Government 
to 
improve 
the 
law 
and order situation; 
tackle 
corruption 
in 
banlung, 
power 
and 
revenue 
administration; 
reform 
the 
civil 
service, 
public 
audit and 
accounting 
bodies, 
public 
procurement and 
the 
civil 
justice 
system; 
and 
ensure 
that 
policies 
provide 
incentives 
for 
increasing 
efficiency, accountability 
and 
transparency. 
91. 
The 
Bangladesh 
CAS 
2000 
was designed 
to 
take account 
of 
Bangladesh’s 
election 
cycle. 
The 
CAS 
was 
prepared about 
a year 
before 
the 
national 
elections 
held 
in 
October 200 
1. 
At 
the 
time 
of 
the 
CAS’S 
preparation, 
Bangladesh 
was 
in 
a 
low-case 
lending 
scenario due 
to 
weak 
implementation 
capacity, 
poor 
govemance 
and stalled reforms. 
The 
CAS 
strategy was 
to 
use 
existing 
and 
new 
analytical 
work 
to 
engage 
the 
new 
Government 
in 
dialogue, 
identify 
areas 
of 
mutual 
agreement and 
reform 
champions, 
and 
develop an expanded 
program 
of 
support 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
indications 
from 
the 
Government 
about 
the 
areas 
28 
Page  37
in 
which 
it 
could 
move forward 
with 
reforms.27 
The 
strategy 
was 
thus 
to be 
opportunistic 
in 
seehng 
areas 
with 
good 
prospects 
for 
reform 
and 
responsive 
to 
Government initiatives. 
92. 
Overall 
progress made 
since 
FY02 
in 
relation 
to 
the 
CAS 
triggers 
justifies 
increasing 
IDA 
lending 
to 
the 
base 
case 
level 
starting 
in 
FY03.” 
The 
macroeconomic situation 
has 
improved, 
effective 
initial 
actions have 
reduced 
domestic 
financing 
of 
debt, 
and 
official 
foreign 
exchange 
reserves have 
stabilized 
at 
about 
US$1.6 
billion 
(although 
still 
below 
a 
safe 
level for 
Bangladesh). 
Portfolio 
performance 
has 
improved, and 
it 
is 
projected 
that the 
proportion 
of 
projects-at-risk 
will 
be 
below 
the 
15 
percentage 
points 
required 
under 
the 
base 
case. 
The 
I-PRSP 
has 
been 
completed. 
The 
proposed 
Development 
Support 
Credit 
is 
the 
first 
adjustment 
operation 
in 
Bangladesh 
since the 
mid-l990s, 
representing 
a gradual 
move 
by 
the 
Bank 
into 
a 
more 
programmatic 
approach 
to 
support 
implementation 
of 
the 
medium-term 
reform 
program 
articulated 
in 
the I-PRSP. 
Analytical and 
Advisory 
Activities 
93. 
Extensive 
analytical 
work 
by 
the 
Bank, 
including 
the 
completion 
of 
all 
core 
diagnostics, 
and 
several  governance-related 
reports 
(a 
corruption 
study, 
an 
institutional 
and 
governance 
review,  and 
procurement  and 
financial 
accountability 
assessments) 
provided 
the 
context 
for 
discussions 
with 
the 
Government that 
came 
to 
power 
in 
October 
2001. 
Eighteen 
policy 
briefs 
prepared 
by 
Bank 
staff 
were 
used 
to 
initiate 
relations 
with 
the 
new 
Ministers 
and 
Secretaries.  These 
briefs 
covered 
macro 
issues 
such 
as 
govemance, state-owned 
enterprises, 
the 
financial 
sector, 
globalization 
and 
trade, 
as 
well 
as 
sectoral 
priorities 
in 
health,  education,  environment, 
agriculture 
and infrastructure. 
Three 
major 
studies 
-- 
a 
Poverty 
Assessment, 
a 
Public 
Expenditure 
Review 
and 
a 
Review 
of 
Public 
Enterprises 
-- 
were 
also 
prepared  and 
discussed 
during 
2002 
to 
contribute 
to 
the 
Government’s 
formulation 
of 
its 
poverty 
reduction 
strategy 
and 
I-PRSP. 
The 
PA 
and 
PER 
were conducted 
jointly 
with 
the 
ADB, 
and 
all 
three 
reports 
were  disseminated 
widely 
and 
discussed 
in 
national 
workshops. 
During 
2002 the 
Bank 
also 
published 
two 
reports 
of 
user 
surveys 
on 
the 
quality 
of 
public 
service 
delivery. 
94. 
As 
the 
relationship 
with 
the 
new 
Government 
developed, 
the 
Bank 
provided 
a substantial 
amount 
of

Short

In

on

Another

on 
energy 
pricing, 
and

an

about the

The 
Bank 
resumed 
its 
financial 
sector dialogue,

a

Governor

named 
in

the

the preparation

(FSAP). 
In

to

agenda, 
this 
rich 
menu 
of 
ESW 
has 
greatly 
influenced 
the 
design 
of 
the 
DSC. 
Lending 
Activities 
95. 
The 
Bank’s 
lending 
support 
is 
designed 
to 
complement 
our 
AAA 
initiatives 
and 
support 
the 
Government’s 
poverty reduction 
strategy. 
A 
proposed education 
sector 
adjustment 
operation and 
the 
Second 
Primary Education Development Program 
are 
under preparation 
to 
support the 
Government’s 
efforts to improve 
education 
quality 
and 
make 
it 
more 
equitable and 
efficient.  The 
Bank 
is 
the 
lead 
donor 
27 
With 
Bangladesh 
being 
in 
a 
low-case 
lending 
scenario, 
Bank’s 
annual 
lending 
to 
the 
country 
has 
averaged about 
US$300 
million 
in 
FY01-02. 
According 
to 
the 
CAS, 
base case 
lending would 
imply 
increasing 
annual 
financial 
support 
to 
about 
US$400-500 
million 
annually, 
which 
would 
be 
triggered 
by 
an 
improvement 
in 
the  macroeconomic  environment; 
improvements 
in 
govemance, 
especially procurement 
and 
financial 
management; 
and progress 
in 
sectoral 
reforms, especially 
in 
the energy and 
financial 
sectors. 
28 
The 
People’s Republic 
of

in 
the 
Health 
and 
Population 
Sector 
Program and 
is 
assisting Bangladesh deal 
with 
emerging

challenges 
such 
as 
HIV/AIDS 
and 
arsenicosis. 
An 
important 
element 
in 
the 
Bank’s 
strategy 
has 
been 
to 
help 
increase 
rural 
employment 
and incomes 
through 
an 
integrated 
approach 
to 
rural 
development. 
In 
conjunction 
with 
development partners, the 
Bank 
has 
been 
worlung 
with 
Government 
in 
key 
areas 
such 
as 
agribusiness development, fisheries 
and 
agncultural 
technology. 
Investments 
supported 
by 
the 
Bank 
in 
roads  and markets, 
rural 
electrification 
and 
micro-credit 
programs 
have 
provided 
income 
generating 
opportunities 
for 
the 
poor. 
The 
Bank’s 
strategy 
in 
the 
water 
sector  focuses 
on 
institutional 
reform, 
enhanced 
community participation 
and close 
attention 
to 
environmental 
and 
social 
dimensions 
of 
flood 
control 
and drainage 
schemes. 
Simultaneously, 
the 
Bank 
has 
promoted innovative community-driven 
development 
(CDD) 
approaches 
to 
deliver 
critical 
social 
assistance 
and 
infrastructure 
services 
to 
the 
poor. 
Through 
the 
Social 
Investment 
Program, 
for 
instance, 
the 
Bank 
is 
supporting 
the 
piloting 
of 
community- 
driven 
initiatives 
to 
replace 
top-down interventions 
traditionally employed 
in 
the 
country. 
Donor Coordination 
96. 
The 
Bank 
has 
continued 
to 
work 
in 
close partnership 
with 
Bangladesh’s 
active donor 
community. 
Donors, 
of 
whom 
IDA, 
ADB 
and 
Japan are 
the largest, 
finance approximately  50 
percent 
of 
the annual 
development budget. 
The 
Bank 
has 
collaborated closely 
with 
UK-DFID 
and 
CIDA 
in 
the 
provision 
of 
micro-finance; 
with 
UK-DFID 
and 
FA0 
in 
agribusiness development; 
with 
the 
Netherlands 
in 
the 
water 
sector; 
and 
with 
a consortium 
of 
donors 
in 
the 
health and education 
sectors. 
Several 
of 
the governance- 
related 
reports, 
mentioned 
above, 
have 
been prepared 
in 
partnership 
with 
donors 
such 
as 
AUSAID, 
Canada, 
Netherlands, 
UNDP 
and 
USAID. 
The 
Public 
Expenditure 
Review 
and 
the 
Poverty 
Assessment 
were 
jointly 
prepared 
with 
the 
Asian 
Development 
Bank. 
97. 
The 
Bank 
and 
the 
IMF 
teams 
have 
worked 
in 
close 
collaboration, 
especially 
in 
areas 
relating to 
the 
macroeconomic 
framework,  the 
financial 
sector 
and 
reforms 
of 
state 
owned 
enterprises. 
The 
IMF, 
which 
has 
taken 
the 
lead 
in 
discussions 
on 
Government’s 
medium-term 
macroeconomic framework, 
is 
processing 
a 
PRGF 
in 
parallel. 
A 
Joint 
Staff 
Assessment 
(JSA) 
of 
the I-PRSP 
is 
planned 
for 
the 
end 
of. 
May 
2003 
and 
it 
is 
envisaged 
that, 
following 
the 
JSA, 
both 
institutions 
will 
complete the preparatory 
work 
needed 
to 
process 
the 
DSC 
and PRGF 
by 
June 
2003. 
VII. 
THE 
PROPOSED 
DEVELOPMENT 
SUPPORT 
CREDIT 
98. 
Credit 
Design. 
Following 
a 
request 
from 
the 
Government,  the 
proposed Development 
Support 
Credit 
will 
support 
the 
first 
phase 
of 
implementation 
of 
the Government’s 
medium-tenn 
reform 
program, 
as 
discussed 
in 
its 
I-PRSP. 
The 
proposed credit amount 
would 
be 
US$300 
million 
equivalent,  and 
the 
borrower 
would 
be 
the People’s 
Republic 
of 
Bangladesh, represented 
by 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance. 
The 
Credit 
will 
be 
on 
IDA 
terms, 
with 
a 
maturity 
of 
40 
years 
including 
a 
grace 
period 
of 
10 
years. 
Strength 
of 
reform 
effort, incremental 
fiscal 
costs 
associated 
with 
the 
SOE 
retrenchment 
program, 
and 
the 
country’s 
balance 
of 
payments 
position 
were 
important 
considerations 
in 
determining 
the 
credit 
amount. 
Since 
experience 
with 
multi-tranche 
adjustment  operations 
in 
Bangladesh 
in 
the 
early-to-mid 
1990s 
was 
not 
good, 
with 
cancellations 
of 
second 
and 
third 
tranches, 
it 
is 
intended 
that 
the 
credit 
will 
be 
disbursed 
in 
a 
single 
tranche, 
upon 
completion 
of 
the 
prior 
actions 
summarized 
in 
Box 
2. 
Box 
3 
summarizes 
indicative 
triggers 
for 
a 
second, 
follow-up 
adjustment  operation. 
The 
prior 
actions 
are 
consistent 
with 
the 
Government’s 
medium 
term 
policy 
framework 
summarized 
in 
Annex 
B, 
and 
are 
a 
sub-set 
of 
the 
policy 
framework articulated 
in 
the 
I-PRSP. 
Columns 
2 and 3 
of 
the 
matrix 
in 
Annex 
B 
provide 
indications 
of 
the 
actions that 
Government 
is 
contemplating 
for 
FY04 
and 
FY05. 
The 
detailed 
coverage 
of 
the 
medium- 
term 
outcomes 
and 
monitorable 
indicators 
associated 
with 
this 
policy 
framework 
are 
presented 
in 
Column 
5 
of 
Annex 
B. 
This 
credit 
is 
envisaged 
as 
the 
first 
in 
a 
sequence 
of 
adjustment operations 
over 
the 
next 
30 
Page  39
several 
years. 
Govemment’s 
completion 
of 
a 
full 
PRSP 
in 
2004 
would 
provide 
the 
basis 
for 
PRSCs 
as 
the 
main 
instrument 
of 
adjustment 
operations. 
This 
approach 
underpins 
a 
long-term 
engagement 
that 
is 
dependent 
on 
the 
Government’s 
commitment 
to 
continue 
implementing 
its 
reform 
program. 
99. 
Credit 
Amount 
and 
Disbursements. 
Disbursements under the proposed 
Credit 
will 
be 
made 
to 
a 
special deposit account 
of 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance 
established at 
Bangladesh 
Bank for 
this 
purpose. 
The 
counterpart 
funds 
generated 
by 
the 
DSC 
will 
be 
used 
to 
finance 
part 
of 
the  Govemment’s 
budgetary 
expenditures 
related 
to 
implementing 
its 
reforms 
program, 
especially 
SOE 
reform. 
The 
credit 
will 
be 
disbursed 
according 
to 
the 
Bank’s 
simplified 
disbursement procedures 
for 
adjustment 
operations. 
Thus 
disbursements 
will 
not 
be linked 
to 
specific 
purchases 
and 
there 
will 
be 
no 
procurement 
requirements. 
If 
the 
credit 
proceeds 
are 
used 
for 
ineligible 
purposes, 
the 
International Development Association 
(IDA) 
will 
require 
the 
Borrower 
to 
either 
(a) 
return 
that amount 
to 
the 
account 
for 
use 
for 
eligible 
purposes; 
or 
(b) 
31 
Page  40
refund 
the 
amount 
directly 
to 
IDA, 
in 
which 
case 
IDA 
will 
cancel 
an 
equivalent undisbursed amount 
of 
the credit. 
IDA 
reserves 
the 
right 
to 
an 
audit 
of 
the special deposit account 
by 
the statutory auditor 
of 
Bangladesh 
Bank 
with 
terms 
of 
reference 
acceptable 
to 
IDA. 
The 
Ministry 
of 
Finance  and  the 
Bangladesh 
Bank 
have 
in 
place the 
basic 
financial 
management 
systems 
in 
terms 
of 
accounting, 
reporting, 
auditing 
and 
internal 
controls. 
The 
Bangladesh 
Bank 
agreed 
to 
adopt 
IAS 
in 
preparing 
its 
financial 
statements 
and 
will 
be 
audited 
by 
internationally 
recognized 
auditing 
firms. 
32 
Page  41
100. 
Fiduciary 
Risks 
and 
Mitigation. 
Efficient 
and 
accountable management 
of 
public 
finances 
is 
central 
to 
good 
governance. 
Bangladesh 
is 
particularly 
weak 
in 
this 
area, 
as 
discussed 
in 
the 
annual 
reports 
of 
the 
Comptroller 
and 
Auditor 
General 
and 
the 
recently 
concluded 
Country  Financial 
Accountability 
Assessment 
(CFAA) 
and 
the 
IMF 
Fiscal 
ROSC 
and  Safeguard 
Assessment 
reports. 
Inadequate 
internal 
controls 
in 
the 
public 
sector 
annually 
result 
in 
a large number 
of 
irregularities 
and 
incidents 
of 
malfeasance. 
As 
discussed 
in 
paragraph 
65, 
a recent 
IMF 
Safeguards 
Assessment 
found 
major 
weaknesses 
in 
the accounting, 
reporting 
and 
control 
environment 
of 
Bangladesh 
Bank. 
101. 
The 
proposed operation 
supports 
a number 
of 
initiatives 
recommended 
by 
the 
CFAA 
such 
as 
separating 
the 
audit 
and accounting 
functions, 
a 
greater 
independence 
for 
the 
Auditor 
General, 
adoption 
of 
public 
sector 
international auditing 
standards (cash basis, 
as 
a 
first 
step), 
preparation 
of 
a 
strategic 
plan 
and an independent 
review 
of 
the 
C&AGs 
office, 
overhauling 
procurement 
procedures and guidelines, 
and establishing 
an 
independent 
Anti-Corruption 
Commission. 
During 
the 
next 
few 
years, 
government 
intends 
to significantly 
strengthen 
financial 
management 
through 
the 
installation 
of 
financial 
management 
units 
in 
every 
ministry, 
survey 
of 
internal 
controls 
and 
reform 
activity, 
publication 
of 
annual 
performance 
reports 
by 
ministries, 
training 
of 
public 
sector accountants, 
updating financial 
manuals and 
guidelines, 
closing 
non-essential 
bank 
accounts, 
and 
establishing 
internal audit 
committees 
in 
all 
ministries. 
These 
initiatives 
are 
being 
supported 
by 
the 
Financial 
Management 
Reform 
Program, 
financed 
by 
UK-DFID 
and 
the  Government 
of 
Netherlands. 
As 
indicated 
in 
paragraph 
67, 
the 
recommendations 
of 
the 
IMF 
safeguards 
assessment 
are 
being 
implemented 
to 
enhance 
the 
safeguards 
framework 
for 
Bangladesh 
Bank. 
102. 
Environmental 
Aspects. 
As 
the 
proposed 
operation 
is 
a structural 
adjustment credit, 
it 
is 
guided 
by 
OD8.60, 
rather than 
by 
OP4.01 
and therefore 
does 
not 
require an 
environmental 
assessment, 
although 
a 
review 
of 
environmental 
policies 
is 
good 
practice. 
In 
preparation 
for 
the 
sequence 
of 
IDA-supported 
adjustment 
credits 
expected 
to 
take 
place 
over the 
next 
several 
years, 
discussions 
are 
underway 
with 
the 
Government 
to 
collaborate 
with 
the 
Bank 
in 
the 
preparation 
of 
a 
Country 
Environment 
Analysis, 
which 
would 
review 
the 
state 
of 
the 
environment,  analyze 
existing 
policies,  and 
assess 
current 
institutional 
capacity 
for 
environmental 
management.  Based 
on 
this 
assessment, 
further 
environmental 
reforms and 
institutional 
strengthening 
consistent 
with 
the 
PRSP 
may 
be 
incorporated 
into 
the actions supported 
by 
future 
adjustment  credits. 
In 
addition, 
as 
part 
of 
the 
Government's 
privatization 
program, 
an 
environmental 
due 
diligence 
process 
will 
be 
designed 
and 
implemented 
to 
help 
identify, 
assign 
and 
mitigate 
the 
environmental 
risks 
associated 
with 
the 
facilities 
to 
be 
privatized 
and closed. 
103. 
Social 
Impact 
and Poverty 
Monitoring. 
The 
I-PRSP 
acknowledges 
that 
the 
success 
of 
the 
poverty reduction 
strategy 
will 
require 
a 
systematic approach 
to monitoring 
and 
evaluation, and 
outlines a 
comprehensive 
set 
of 
input, 
intermediate,  and outcome 
indicators that 
will 
be 
used 
to 
track 
progress 
in 
implementing 
the 
poverty 
reduction 
strategy 
(Annex 
C). 
One 
of 
the 
key 
outputs 
of 
the 
poverty 
monitoring 
system 
envisaged 
under 
the I-PRSP 
is 
an 
Annual 
Report 
on 
Poverty Reduction 
and 
Social 
Development, 
providing 
an overview 
of 
progress 
in 
achieving 
economic growth, 
human 
development, 
poverty 
reduction, 
gender 
equality 
targets and analysis 
of 
factors 
behind 
the observed 
trends 
and 
their 
policy 
implications. 
A 
Poverty 
Focal Point 
is 
envisaged 
in 
the 
Planning Commission 
for 
effective 
poverty 
and 
MDG 
monitoring 
and 
trackmg 
progress 
in 
implementing 
anti-poverty 
policies 
and 
programs. 
In 
addition, 
a 
National 
Poverty Reduction 
Council 
(NPRC) 
chaired 
by 
the 
Prime 
Minister 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
formed, 
with 
strong 
inter-ministerial 
linkages and representation 
from 
the 
private 
sector, 
academia, 
research 
institutes, 
NGOs, 
and 
the 
civil 
society. 
Implementation 
of 
the 
poverty 
reduction 
strategy 
will 
be 
the central task 
of 
this 
Council, along 
with 
addressing strategy 
and policy-related 
issues 
arising out 
of 
the 
monitoring 
and evaluation 
work 
of 
the 
Poverty 
Focal 
Point, 
which 
is 
expected 
to 
act 
as 
the Secretariat 
for 
NPRC. 
104. 
A 
relatively 
comprehensive 
information 
base 
already exists 
in 
Bangladesh, 
providing 
extensive 
geographically 
disaggregated  data  needed 
to  monitor 
most 
of 
the 
important  poverty 
and 
social 
33 
Page  42
development 
indicators. 
The 
country 
has 
in 
place 
extensive 
data 
collection 
systems 
on 
a 
wide 
range 
of 
indicators 
related 
to 
poverty, 
demography, 
health, education, 
nutrition, 
and 
access 
to 
services. 
In 
addition 
to 
the 
decennial 
population 
censuses, 
at 
regular 
intervals 
Bangladesh 
Bureau 
of 
Statistics 
(BBS) 
carries 
out 
large-scale 
nationally 
and 
regionally 
representative 
surveys, 
such 
as 
Household 
Income  and 
Expenditure  Survey 
(HIES), 
Labor 
Force  Survey 
(LFS), 
and 
Child Nutrition 
Survey 
(CNS). 
Other 
government 
agencies 
(e.g. 
BANBEIS 
- 
the 
Bangladesh 
Bureau 
of 
Educational 
Information 
and Statistics) 
also 
maintain 
an 
extensive 
database 
of 
a number 
of 
sector-specific 
indicators. 
Other 
independent (or 
semi-autonomous) 
data 
collection initiatives include 
the 
Bangladesh 
Health 
and 
Demographic 
Surveys 
(DHS), 
the 
Helen 
Keller 
International’s 
Nutritional 
Surveillance 
Project,  and the 
CAMPE 
Education 
Watch 
project 
105. 
Donors, 
notably 
the 
ADB, 
DFID, 
and 
the 
World 
Bank, 
are 
coordinating 
efforts 
to 
provide 
technical 
and 
financial 
assistance 
needed 
to 
strengthen 
capacity 
in 
the 
Poverty 
Focal 
Point 
to 
fulfill 
its 
designated 
function. 
Further, 
building 
on 
a long-standing 
collaboration 
between 
the

Bank 
and 
BBS, 
which 
has 
included 
extensive

for

earlier 
HIES 
rounds, 
efforts 
are 
underway 
to 
prepare an 
IDF 
grant 
to 
support 
the 
next HIES, 
and 
to 
reinforce 
and 
expand 
on 
earlier 
initiatives 
to 
improve 
in-country 
capacity 
for 
poverty 
and 
social 
impact 
monitoring 
and 
analysis. 
106. 
Government 
is 
keenly 
aware 
of 
the 
potential 
social 
costs 
associated 
with 
some 
of 
the 
adjustment 
measures 
and 
would 
like 
to 
ensure 
that 
social 
hardships 
resulting 
from 
the 
reforms 
are 
minimized. 
In 
particular, 
there 
are 
concerns about 
workers that 
have 
been 
laid 
off 
as 
a 
result 
of 
SOE 
closures 
and the 
increase 
in 
the  cost 
of 
living 
resulting 
fi-om 
recent 
increases 
in 
utility 
prices2’. 
Learning 
from 
past 
experience, 
to 
mitigate 
adverse 
social 
impacts, 
Government 
has 
engaged 
labor 
representatives 
in 
extensive discussions 
prior 
to 
embarkmg 
upon 
its 
SOE 
closures 
and 
privatization 
program. 
While 
the 
legal 
minimum 
gratuity required 
under 
the 
labor 
code 
is 
one 
month’s 
salary 
per 
year’s 
service, 
the 
negotiated 
severance 
package 
is 
generous 
(two 
months 
salary 
for 
every 
year 
of 
service, 
plus 
a redundancy 
payment 
ranging 
fi-om 
13 
to 
27 
percent, depending 
upon 
length 
of 
service) 
and 
will 
mitigate 
against 
some 
of 
the 
hardship that 
retrenched 
workers 
might 
face. 
The 
average severance 
pay 
received 
by 
blue collar 
workers 
is 
about 
Tk.

Governance

The

Letter

1.0 
Introduction 
1.1 
Since the assumption 
of 
office 
in 
October 
2001, 
the 
present 
Government 
took 
immediate 
steps 
to 
stem 
the 
slide 
in 
macroeconomic 
management, 
and, 
through 
swift 
corrective 
measures, 
has 
been 
able 
to 
restore macroeconomic 
stability, 
and 
set 
the 
economy 
on 
a 
course 
of 
sustained 
growth 
and 
poverty 
reduction. 
In 
sharp 
contrast 
with 
the 
lack 
of 
reforms 
during 
the 
five 
years 
of 
the 
previous 
Government, 
the 
new 
Government 
is 
keen 
to 
undertake 
wide-ranging 
reforms 
of 
institutions 
and 
policies relating 
to 
broad-based 
governance, 
banking 
and 
financial 
sector, 
state- 
owned 
enterprises, energy, 
infrastructure, 
and 
the environment. 
1.2 
This 
letter 
of 
Development 
Policy 
lays 
out 
what 
the 
Government 
has 
already done 
in 
the 
field 
of 
structural 
reforms,  the 
reform 
actions 
planned 
under 
its 
three  year 
National 
Strategy 
for 
Economic 
Growth,  Poverty 
Reduction 
and 
Social 
Development, 
and 
seeks 
adjustment support 
from 
the 
World 
Bank. 
Our 
strategy 
is 
to 
undertake 
reforms 
on 
the basis 
of 
the 
Action 
Plan 
embodied 
in 
the 
NSEGPWI-PRSP. 
In 
light 
of 
this 
strategy,  a 
three-year 
“Rolling 
Investment 
Program” 
is 
being 
formulated, 
which 
will 
be 
the  basis 
of 
the  Government’s 
medium-term 
development 
strategy. 
The 
major 
thrust 
of 
that  strategy 
is 
on 
rapid 
poverty reduction 
and 
accelerated economic 
growth 
with 
macroeconomic 
stability.

2

2.0 
Background 
2.1 
On 
October 
10, 
2001, 
when 
the 
present 
Government 
took 
charge, 
the 
macroeconomic 
situation 
was 
fragile 
with 
a 
fiscal 
deficit 
that 
appeared 
quite 
unsustainable. 
By 
June 
2001, 
the 
consolidated  government 
deficit 
had 
risen 
to 
nearly 
7.5% 
of 
GDP, 
from 
4.4 
percent 
in 
FY98. 
Consequently,  domestic 
bank 
borrowings 
of 
the 
govemment 
rose 
dramatically 
such that 
the 
outstanding domestic 
public 
debt 
rose 
threefold 
between 
FY96 
and 
FYOI, 
from 
Tk. 
60 
billion 
to 
Tk. 
180 
billion. 
Monetary 
policy, 
which 
simply 
accommodated the 
fiscal 
excesses, 
was 
highly 
expansionary, 
and 
led 
to 
pressures 
on 
the balance 
of 
payments 
and 
depletion 
of 
foreign 
exchange 
reserves. 
In 
October 
2001 
, 
these 
reserves 
stood 
at 
$1.1 
billion, 
barely sufficient 
to 
finance 
six 
weeks’ 
import 
bill. 
Global 
economic 
recession, 
which 
was 
further 
deepened 
in 
the 
aftermath 
of 
September 
11 
events, 
made matters worse 
by 
causing a 
fall 
in 
Bangladesh’s 
export 
earnings, 
for 
the 
first 
time 
in 
many 
years. 
3.0 
Immediate 
Steps 
to 
Restore Macroeconomic 
Stability 
3.1 
The new 
Government 
being 
fully 
aware 
of 
the 
danger 
posed 
by 
macroeconomic 
imbalances 
had 
to 
undertake 
urgent recovery 
measures 
to 
address 
the 
issue. 
The 
Government 
formed 
five 
Secretaries 
Committees 
on 
17 
October, 
2001 
to 
prepare 
reports 
on 
adrmnistrative 
reforms, 
improvement 
of 
law 
and 
order situation, 
poverty 
alleviation, 
human 
resource development 
and 
economic 
management. 
The  commitment 
of 
the 
Government 
in 
respect 
of 
the 
reforms 
and 
improved 
governance 
is 
best 
reflected 
in 
the 
adoption 
of 
a 
100-day 
program 
to 
address 
25 
most 
urgent 
issues 
facing 
the country. 
Budgetary Management 
3.2 
A 
number 
of 
measures 
have been introduced over 
the 
past 
year 
to improve 
the 
government’s 
budgetary 
management.  These 
include: 
0 
In 
FY02 
there 
was 
skepticism that government 
would 
be 
able 
to 
keep 
the 
fiscal 
deficit to 
within 
five 
percent 
of 
GDP. 
Yet, 
with 
strong  determination 
and 
steadfast 
efforts,  the 
government made 
prudent 
though 
painful 
changes 
in 
fiscal 
and 
financial 
management 
which 
made 
it 
possible 
to 
restore 
fiscal 
balance 
and 
contain the 
fiscal 
deficit 
to 
4.7%. 
This 
was 
accomplished 
through 
a 
combination 
of 
expenditure 
rationalisation 
and 
improvements 
in 
tax 
administration. 
On 
the basis 
of 
data 
fed 
by 
computerised 
accounting 
and 
budgeting 
system, 
the Finance 
Division, 
after 
exhaustive 
interaction 
with 
the 
line 
ministries over 
a 
period 
of 
2 
months,

Macroeconomic 
Management 
3.3 
Several 
measures 
have 
been 
put 
in 
place 
and more 
are 
being 
taken 
to 
improve 
macroeconomic management. 
These 
are 
listed 
below: 
All 
projects 
financed 
by 
suppliers' credits 
have 
been 
put 
on 
hold. The 
FY03 
budget, 
with 
a 
central 
government 
deficit 
target 
of 
3.9 
percent 
of 
GDP, 
was 
approved 
by 
parliament 
in 
June 
2002. 
Numerous 
revenue-enhancing 
measures 
- 
both 
in 
tax 
and 
non-tax were 
announced 
in 
the 
budget 
for 
FY03. 
These 
include: 
lowering 
of 
the personal 
income 
tax exemption 
limit, 
increase 
in 
the 
minimum 
income tax 
payable 
by 
individuals, 
containment 
of 
tax

additional 
tax 
on

of 
listed 
companies, 
reduction 
in 
corporate

incomes (except

of

VAT

of 
activity, 
increase 
in

and

vehicle 
taxes, 
and 
appropriate 
rationalization 
of 
the 
rates 
of 
non-tax 
revenue items. 
To 
make 
our 
development 
effort 
self-reliant 
and 
self-sustaining, 
we 
are 
conscious 
of

to

In

of

This 
is 
why 
a

of

have 
been

both 
in 
tax revenue 
and

The

has

from 
10.2 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
FY02 
to 
10.4 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in

Some

500

During

NBR

has

by 
about 23% 
much

Though collection

Our

(at 
15% 
of

is

We

inflastructure

Both

All

SOEs

down. 
Already 
Adamjee

19

The

Long

Energy

on

(22%),

(lo%), 
power

(74%) 
and

(10%). 
In

2003,

and

increased 
by 
a 
further 
17%. 
In

use,

has

new 
electricity, 
gas 
and

framework  to

efficiency 
and

This

will 
provide 
for

flexible

mechanism 
that 
rationalizes 
the 
prices 
of 
energy 
products 
and 
enables 
cost 
recovery 
for 
all 
operating, maintenance, 
and 
capital 
costs.

4.2 
Telecommunication

New

/Communication

0 
In

The

has

modem

The 
Act 
provides 
for 
the 
creation 
of 
the 
Bangladesh Telecommunication Regulatory 
Commission 
(BTRC) 
to facilitate 
the growth  and 
development 
of 
telecommunication 
activities 
in 
a 
multi-operator  competitive  environment 
where 
the 
private 
sector 
will 
play 
the 
key 
role. 
Moreover, 
Govt. 
has 
decided 
to 
corporatize 
BTTB 
and 
a 
committee 
has 
been 
set 
up 
to 
recommend 
how 
this 
is 
to 
be 
done. 
A 
high-powered  committee 
has 
been 
formed 
in 
this 
regard 
and 
the 
Committee 
is 
expected 
to 
put 
forward 
necessary 
recommendations 
very 
soon. 
Already four 
cellurar 
telephone companies 
and

in 
rural 
Page  52
Annex

8

The

4.3 
Reforms 
in 
Banking 
and 
Financial 
Sector 
To 
bring 
more 
discipline 
in 
theflnancial 
sector 
and 
increase operational autonomy 
of 
the 
Bangladesh 
Bank, 
necessary  amendments 
to 
the 
Bangladesh 
Bank 
Order, 
Banking 
Companies 
Act 
and 
Bangladesh 
Banks 
(Nationalization)  Order 
have 
been 
enacted 
by 
the 
parliament 
in 
March 
2003. 
Several Task 
Forces 
have been 
set 
up 
to 
enforce 
the 
Money 
Laundering Prevention 
Act. 
More 
than 
100 
unprofitable 
branches 
of 
Nationalized 
Commercial 
Banks 
have 
been closed 
and 
many 
more 
will 
be 
closed 
in 
phases. 
The 
interest rates 
for 
the 
four 
National 
Savings 
Certificate 
scheme 
were 
reduced 
and 
four 
other 
National 
Savings 
Instruments 
have 
been 
abolished. 
New 
incentive 
scheme 
has 
been 
introduced 
for improving 
performance 
including 
collection 
of 
NPLs. 
Loan 
recovery 
departments 
have been strengthened. Necessary amendments 
to 
the 
Money 
Loan 
Courts 
Act 
have  been  enacted 
by 
the 
parliament 
in 
this 
Spring 
Session. 
Prudential 
regulations 
and  banking 
supervision 
have 
been  strengthened,  Powers 
and 
responsibilities 
of 
Banks 
and 
management 
have been 
clearly 
spelt 
out. 
Fit 
and proper 
tests 
for 
Chief 
Executives 
of 
private 
commercial 
banks 
have 
been 
introduced. 
Similar 
tests 
will 
be 
introduced 
for 
directors 
as 
well. 
ESfective 
internal 
control 
system 
is 
being 
set 
up. 
Capital 
adequacy 
requirements  have 
been 
increased 
fiom 
8% 
to 
9% 
on 
risk-weighted 
basis. 
Dividend 
declaration 
by 
banks 
in 
excess 
of 
20% 
is 
allowed 
only 
if 
an 
equivalent amount 
is 
set 
aside 
for 
reserve. 
The 
single 
borrower 
limit 
has 
been 
reduced 
by 
half. 
Regulations 
have 
been 
issued 
by 
the Bangladesh 
Bank 
to 
control loan 
default. 
A 
Committee 
on 
Default 
Loans 
was 
set 
up 
to 
suggest measures 
for 
reducing 
the 
burden 
of 
debt 
default 
and 
for improved 
loan 
management 
perfomance 
of 
the 
banking 
sector. 
A 
loan 
write-off policy 
has 
been introduced 
by 
BB 
for 
the 
first time 
in 
Bangladesh. 
BB 
has 
also 
tightened 
loan 
rescheduling 
guidelines. 
On 
the 
basis 
of 
the 
recommendations 
of 
the 
Committee, 
some 
bad 
loans 
have 
been 
rescheduled 
and

The 
recommendations 
of 
the 
Bank-Fund 
FSAP 
mission 
are 
being 
reviewed 
to 
carry 
out 
further 
reforms 
in 
the 
financial 
sector.

4.4 
Improvement 
in 
Trademxternal 
Sector 
The 
trade 
regime 
has 
been 
further 
liberalized 
by 
reducing 
the 
maximum 
tariff 
rate 
by 
5 
percentage 
points 
(to 
32.5 
percent). 
Supplementary 
duty 
scheme has 
been 
rationalized 
by 
reducing 
the 
number 
of 
rates 
from 
31 
to 
5. 
Import 
license 
fee 
on 
all 
imports 
has 
been 
withdrawn. 
In 
consequence, 
the 
average 
nominal 
protection 
accorded 
by 
the 
tariff 
structure 
has 
declined 
further. 
This 
trend 
will 
be 
maintained 
in 
the 
forthcoming 
budget 
and 
efforts 
will 
be 
made 
to 
further 
streamline 
the 
tariff 
structure. 
A 
wide 
range 
of 
measures 
and 
improved 
logistics 
for 
monitoring 
were  introduced to 
increase 
the 
volume 
of 
remittance. 
In 
a 
number 
of 
countries, 
banks 
and 
exchange houses 
have been 
set 
up. 
The 
Money 
Laundering 
Prevention Act,  2002 
has 
been 
introduced to 
curb 
illegal 
transactions. 
It 
is 
because 
of 
these 
immediate 
steps 
taken 
by 
the 
govemment 
the 
volume 
of 
remittance 
inflows 
grew 
at 
a rate 
of 
about 
33% 
from 
US$1882 
in 
FY2000- 
01 
to 
US$2501 
million 
in 
FY 
2001-02. 
The 
same 
growth 
trend 
has 
been 
continuing 
until 
now. Foreign 
exchange reserves 
have 
been 
rising 
gradually, 
reaching 
US$2 
billion 
as 
of 
March, 
2003, 
sufficient 
to 
cover 
two 
and 
a 
half 
months’ 
imports. 
4.5 
Socially Responsible 
Fiscal 
Strategy 
The 
FY03 
budget 
continues 
Bangladesh’s 
long-standing 
tradition 
of 
emphasizing 
investment 
in 
the social 
sectors 
and 
rural 
infrastructure. 
The education 
sector 
is 
given 
top 
priority 
and 
accounts 
for 
15.4 
percent 
of 
total 
expenditures; transport 
and 
communication 
account 
for 
another 
13.4 
percent; 
health 
7 
percent 
and 
agriculture 
7 
percent. 
Defence 
expenditures 
remain 
low 
by 
developing 
country 
and 
South 
Asian 
standards, 
accounting 
for 
less 
than 
1.5 
percent 
of 
GDP. 
Adequate 
allocation 
will

made 
in 
the 
revised 
budget 
for 
FY 
2002-03 
to 
defray 
the

benefits 
payable 
to 
the retrenched labourers 
and 
employees 
of 
the 
SOEs 
selected 
for 
privatisation. 
More

will 
be 
provided 
in 
the 
Revenue 
budget 
for 
O&M 
expenditure 
and 
to 
clear 
up 
arrears. 
Recognizing 
the 
need to 
strengthen the governance 
of 
targeted food  transferprogrammes, 
the 
Food 
for 
Education 
Programme 
and 
the 
Primary 
Education 
Stipends Programme have 
been 
discontinued 
and 
replaced 
by 
a 
cash 
stipend 
programme 
for

school 
students. 
It 
may 
be 
noted 
here that 
to 
ensure 
transparency 
the 
government 
has 
already monetized 
some 
of 
its 
Food-for-Work 
(FFW) 
programs. 
The 
Government 
is 
determined 
to 
continue 
supporting 
the 
secondary 
education 
of 
girls 
and 
has 
decided 
to

Programme 
to 
Class 12.

4.6 
Governance 
Reforms 
Recognizing 
the 
critical 
need 
to 
improve 
public procurement, 
the 
Government 
is 
creating 
basic 
policy 
and 
institutional fiameworks 
in 
the 
medium 
term, 
through 
(a) 
reforming 
the 
procurement 
rules 
and 
mandatory 
use 
of 
standard 
bid 
documents 
so 
as 
to 
cut 
procurement 
delays; 
(b) 
introducing 
mechanisms 
to 
ensure 
transparency 
in 
procurement 
process 
and 
restore 
public 
confidence; 
and 
(c) 
establishing 
procurement  training, 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
capacity 
to track procurement 
performance 
of 
public 
sector 
entities. 
Recommendations

Public Administration 
Reforms 
Commission 
are 
being 
reviewed 
by

committee

implemented. 
Merit-based 
promotion 
system 
has 
been introduced 
for 
posts

In

reduce the 
size 
of 
the 
Government recruitment to most 
of 
the

Moreover,

has

to

by

committee 
headed 
by

Secretary. 
Auditing

functions have 
been 
separated 
and 
a 
TA 
Project 
has 
been 
initiated 
to 
further 
strengthen the 
audit function. 
The 
Govemment 
is 
fully 
determined 
to 
ensure 
law 
and 
order 
and, 
with 
this 
end 
in 
view 
launched 'operation 
clean 
heart' 
in

defense 
and

administration 
worked 
together 
to 
recover 
illegal 
arms 
and 
curb terrorism. The 
move 
was 
welcomed 
by

community 
and 
received 
broad-based support 
of 
the people. Several actions 
have 
been 
taken 
to 
strengthen the 
police 
force 
and 
to 
raise 
their 
efficiency.  The 
Speedy 
Trail 
Act 
has 
been 
passed 
and 
Speedy 
Trail 
Courts 
set 
up. 
The 
Govemment 
is 
determined 
to 
improve 
the 
skills 
and 
efficiency 
of 
administration 
and 
several actions have been

to

The

5.0 
Preparation 
of 
I-PRSP: 
A 
Process 
of 
Policy Ownership: 
Key 
Elements 
of 
PRSP 
5.1 
Shortly 
after 
taking 
office, 
the 
present 
government accelerated 
the 
preparation 
of 
a 
three-year 
National 
Strategy 
for 
Economic 
Growth, 
Poverty 
Reduction 
and 
Social 
Development 
and 
organized 
a 
series 
of 
national 
consultations 
to 
solicit 
inputs 
for 
the 
preparation 
of 
its 
poverty reduction 
strategy. 
The 
strategy has 
been 
worked 
out 
based 
on 
the 
rich 
experience 
of 
existing poverty 
research 
and 
participatory poverty 
assessments, 
impact 
and 
outcomes 
of 
past 
strategies 
and 
policies, 
and 
assessment 
of 
development 
and 
reform 
issues. 
An 
initial 
draft 
of 
the 
I-PRSP  was  prepared 
in 
April 
2002 
and 
posted 
on 
the 
Government’s 
web-site, 
followed 
by 
a 
four 
month period 
during 
which 
the 
draft 
was 
discussed 
with 
various  government 
and 
non-governmental 
organizations, members 
of 
civil 
society 
and 
Bangladesh’s development 
partners. 
The 
process 
of 
policy 
ownership, 
as 
adopted 
in 
Bangladesh, aims 
at 
providing 
a 
well-prioritized national 
strategy 
for 
economic  growth, 
poverty 
reduction, and 
social 
development. 
5.2 
Social 
development 
captures 
inter-related 
issues 
of 
human 
development, 
gender 
equality, 
social  deprivations 
and 
environmental 
sustainability. 
For 
this, 
the 
process 
has 
combined 
policymaking 
and 
broad-based consultations 
with 
different 
stakeholders. 
Additional 
inputs 
for 
preparing 
the strategy paper were gathered 
through 
commissioning 
twelve 
studies 
in 
key 
poverty 
related 
areas 
such 
as 
economic,  social, 
and 
infrastructure 
development; 
public 
expenditure  analysis; 
poverty 
diagnostics; 
and 
governance 
issues. 
In 
each 
division 
of 
the 
country, consultations were organized 
at 
the 
upazila 
level 
with 
the 
poor 
including women 
and 
men, 
members 
of 
non-government 
organizations 
(NGOs), 
and 
various occupational 
groups. 
Similar 
consultations were also organized 
with 
field-level 
hctionaries 
of 
the 
Government 
Ministries 
and 
Agencies, 
NGOs, 
women’s 
groups, 
local 
and 
religious 
leaders, 
local 
government representatives, 
and 
cross-section 
of 
the 
civil 
society. 
Finally, 
three 
separate 
consultations 
were 
held 
at 
the 
national 
level 
involving 
the 
Government 
officials, 
representatives 
of 
professional 
groups 
and 
the 
civil 
society, 
and 
the 
development 
partners. 
The 
dialogue  process 
reviewed 
both 
diagnostic 
and 
prescriptive 
aspects 
of 
the 
poverty 
reduction 
efforts 
proposed 
in 
the 
draft 
document. 
The 
consistency 
among 
the 
poverty 
reduction 
strategy, 
the 
medium-term 
policy 
framework, 
and 
the 
social 
targets was 
closely 
examined 
during 
the 
dialogue 
process. 
Page  56
Annex 
A 
Page 
12 
of 
22 
5.3 
The 
National 
Strategy 
of 
Economic Growth, Poverty 
Reduction 
and 
Social Development 
has 
been 
finalized 
by 
Govemment. 
The 
strategy 
has 
been 
jointly 
assessed 
by 
the 
IMF 
and 
World 
Bank. 
The 
key 
emphasis 
of 
this 
strategy 
are 
as 
follows: 
The 
first 
set 
of 
policies 
aims 
at 
accelerating the pace 
and 
expanding 
the 
scope 
for 
pro-poor 
economic 
growth 
in 
order 
to 
increase 
the 
employment opportunities 
and 
income 
of 
the 
poor. 
The 
second 
set 
aims 
to 
foster 
the 
human 
development 
of 
the 
poor 
through 
education, 
health, 
nutrition 
and 
other 
social 
interventions. 
The 
third 
set 
of 
policies 
supports 
women’s development 
and 
aims 
at 
closing 
the 
gender 
gap 
in 
development. 
The 
fourth 
set 
provides social 
safety 
nets 
to 
the 
poor 
against 
anticipated 
and 
unanticipated 
income 
and 
consumption 
shocks 
through 
targeted 
and 
other 
efforts. 
Finally, 
the 
fifth 
set 
of 
policies 
focuses 
on 
participatory 
governance 
with 
actions 
aimed 
at 
enhancing the 
voice 
of 
the 
poor 
and 
promoting 
security, 
power 
and 
social inclusion. 
5.4 
The 
Government’s  strategy 
is 
to 
launch 
an 
initial 
set 
of 
reforms 
in 
areas where broad 
consensus 
exists, 
with 
the 
expectation that 
these 
will 
begin 
to 
pay-off 
shortly, 
creating 
momentum 
and 
public 
support 
for 
subsequent 
measures. 
The 
central objective 
of 
the 
first 
phase 
of 
the 
Government’s 
reform 
program 
is 
to 
improve 
the 
investment 
climate 
and 
stimulate 
faster 
economic 
growth 
in 
order 
to 
accelerate 
the 
pace 
of 
poverty 
reduction. 
In 
this 
context, Government 
intends 
to 
focus 
its 
efforts 
on 
improving 
three 
broad 
and 
interrelated 
areas, 
i.e. 
the macroeconomic 
environment, 
the 
country’s govemance 
and 
regulatory 
framework, 
and 
the 
quality 
of 
physical 
and 
financial 
infrastructure. 
In 
improving 
the 
macroeconomic environment, 
Government 
envisages 
prudent 
fiscal 
management, 
which 
in 
tum 
will 
necessitate 
higher 
revenue 
mobilization, 
more 
efficient 
public 
administration 
and 
reform 
of 
state-owned 
enterprises. 
Both 
monetary 
and 
exchange 
rate 
policy 
will 
complement 
this 
fiscal 
stance. 
Efforts 
to 
improve governance 
will 
focus 
on 
implementing 
much 
needed 
institutional 
reforms, 
including 
putting 
in 
place 
a 
proactive 
regulatory  environment 
and 
effective 
incentives 
linked 
to 
real 
accountability. 
In 
improving 
physical and 
financial 
infrastructure 
particular 
attention 
will 
be 
paid 
to 
the 
power,  transport 
and telecommunications 
sectors 
and 
reforming 
the banking 
system. 
5.5 
Accelerating pro-poor 
economic 
growth 
will 
be 
the 
key 
element 
in 
achieving 
success 
of 
the 
Strategy. 
In 
order 
to 
halve 
the 
poverty 
rate 
by 
2015, 
a 
goal 
set 
in 
our 
I-PRSP, 
growth 
over 
the 
medium 
to 
long-term 
must 
be 
raised 
to 
at 
least 
7 
percent 
in 
conjunction 
with 
pro-poor policies. 
The 
sources 
of 
increased 
growth 
would 
involve 
several 
areas: 
(i) 
higher 
private 
investment 
and 
increased 
inflow 
of 
foreign direct 
investments 
(FDIs) 
by 
ensuring 
good 
govemance, 
promoting 
an 
Page  57
Annex 
A 
Page 
13 
of 
22 
investment-friendly 
environment  and 
taking 
measures 
to 
reduce transaction 
costs; 
(ii) 
increased 
efficiency 
and 
technological 
progress 
across 
the 
economy 
including promotion 
of 
information 
and 
communication 
technologies  (ICTs) 
and 
biotechnology; 
(iii) 
expanded 
growth 
of 
industry 
(particularly  small 
and

and 
service

(iv)

(e.g.

and 
(v) 
expansion 
and 
diversification 
of 
the 
export 
sector. 
5.6 
By 
the 
end 
of 
the 
PRSP 
period, 
we 
aim 
to 
raise 
GDP growth to 
6.5 percent, 
up 
from 
5 
percent 
on 
average 
during 
the 
second 
half 
of 
the 
199Os, 
and 
to contain 
inflation 
under 
4 
percent. 
For 
ensuring 
such growth, 
prioritization, 
sequencing 
and 
interrelationships 
of 
macro 
and 
sector 
policies 
are 
being 
emphasized. 
Within 
the medium term framework, 
priorities 
would 
be 
given 
to 
ensure the following: 
(i) 
stable 
macroeconomic 
balances; 
(ii) 
strong 
institutions 
and 
improved 
govemance; 
(iii) 
private 
sector-led 
and 
outward 
oriented 
growth; 
(iv) 
Government-private 
sector 
(including 
NGOs) 
partnership; 
and 
(v) 
gender 
sensitive 
macro 
and 
policy 
framework 
embedded 
in 
the 
national 
budget. 
The 
priorities 
of 
sectoral 
and 
sub-sectoral 
activities 
will 
be 
worked 
out 
such 
that the 
growth 
elasticity 
of 
poverty reduction 
can 
be 
increased. 
5.7 
A 
detailed

It

by 
an 
indicative 
costing 
of 
poverty

programs. 
The 
target rate 
of 
growth 
has 
been

to 
ensure 
a 
real 
breakthrough 
in

reduction 
in

medium

Program

will 
be 
tracked 
by

Efforts 
will

to

to 
ensure 
quality 
and 
consistency 
of

used 
in

with

hlly

Policies 
6.3 
Monetary 
and  exchange rate policies 
would 
complement  the 
goal 
of 
macroeconomic 
stability 
with 
recourse 
to 
market 
based 
instruments 
to 
achieve 
inflation 
and 
reserve objectives. 
The 
Government 
is 
committed 
to 
move  towards 
more 
flexible 
exchange  rate  management. 
Several 
structural 
measures 
e.g. 
improved 
performance 
of

(NCBs) 
and development

(DFIs),

demand 
on 
budgetary 
and

resources 
would 
be 
implemented 
to 
support 
the 
stabilization policies 
in 
the 
short term. 
Several

along

lines 
have already

in

of 
FY03. 
Recognizing

of

under

).

and

to

on

along

fiom

Private 
Sector

Energy 
Sector 
Reforms 
6.5 
A 
major 
challenge 
for 
our 
Government 
is 
to 
restore 
business 
and 
investor confidence 
that 
was eroded 
due 
to 
past 
lack 
of 
reforms 
in 
key 
areas, 
such 
as 
ports, 
energy, 
telecommunications, 
and 
financial 
sector. 
Our 
goal 
is 
to 
provide 
a 
stable 
policy 
environment, 
which 
reduces 
the 
incentives 
for 
lobbying 
and 
rent 
seeking, 
discourages corruption, 
and 
minimizes 
the 
extra-legal 
costs 
of 
doing 
business. 
For 
proper 
functioning 
of 
the 
private 
sector, 
physical 
improvements 
and 
management 
reforms 
in 
the 
basic 
infrastructure 
including 
power, water 
supply, 
port 
and 
telecommunications 
will 
be 
given 
priority 
along 
with 
private 
sector 
participation. 
The 
Government 
has 
stressed 
the 
urgency 
for 
ensuring 
the 
following 
reforms 
in 
the energy sector 
over 
the 
program 
period 
(3 
Year 
Road 
Map): 
Setting 
up 
the 
Energy Regulatory Commission 
Strengthening 
and 
improvement 
of 
commercial performance 
Designing 
and 
implementing 
competitive 
and equitable 
pricing policy 
Ensure 
good 
governance 
through 
providing 
autonomy 
of 
gas 
marketing 
companies 
Enactment 
of 
laws 
to 
strengthen 
the 
legal framework 
and 
Strengthening 
the 
capability 
of 
existing institutions 
Reforming 
the 
energy 
sector 
through 
appropriate 
pricing 
and 
transparent 
regulatory 
framework 
will 
be 
given 
due 
importance. 
Decisive 
reform 
of 
the energy 
sector 
is 
critical to 
containing 
SOE 
losses, 
since such 
losses 
are 
heavily

in 
this 
sector. 
Recent

to

and

In

of

Energy 
Regulatory

would

and

Human 
Development 
and Poverty Reduction 
6.6 
We 
recognize 
that 
development 
of 
human 
capital 
has 
strong 
poverty  reducing 
effects 
in 
Bangladesh. 
Three main 
elements 
of 
human 
development 
will 
be 
targeted: 
education, 
health and 
nutrition. 
Within 
the 
health 
sector, 
although 
some 
success 
has 
been 
achieved 
in 
preventive health 
care, 
a 
small 
proportion 
of 
poor 
people have 
access 
to 
public 
health 
care services. 
In 
the 
health 
sector, 
Health 
and 
Population 
Sector 
Programme 
(HPSP) 
achieved 
remarkable 
success 
in 
certain 
areas, 
while 
it 
also  faced  operational 
problems 
in 
some 
others. 
In 
order 
to 
make 
this 
programme 
effective, 
the 
Government 
has 
commissioned 
a 
full 
review 
of 
the 
operational 
strengths 
and 
weaknesses 
of 
this 
new 
approach. 
The 
government remains 
committed 
to 
the 
sector-wide approach 
and 
recognizes 
the 
obvious 
advantages 
of 
this 
approach. 
The 
Ministry 
of 
Education 
has 
taken 
the 
lead to 
improve 
governance 
in 
the 
education 
sector. 
The 
government 
will 
further 
improve 
governance 
in 
the education 
sector 
through 
widening 
the 
coverage 
and improving quality 
of 
education emphasizing education 
for 
all 
concept. 
Moreover, 
finding 
more 
effective 
ways 
to 
provide 
vocational 
and 
technical education 
will 
be 
given 
priority 
in 
the 
education 
strategy. 
The 
strategy 
will 
focus 
mainly 
on 
teachers 
training, 
performance-based government  subventions, 
curricula 
up-gradation,  academic supervision, 
decentralized  management 
and 
expenditure 
tracking 
studies. 
The 
newly 
introduced 
cash 
stipend 
program 
is 
planned 
to 
be 
a 
targeted program. 
The 
Government 
is 
determined 
to 
continue supporting 
the 
secondary 
education 
of 
girls. 
The 
program 
will 
be 
targeted 
to 
those 
girls 
that 
secure 
45 
percent 
or 
more 
marks 
in 
the 
Secondary 
School 
Certificate 
(SSC) 
examination. 
Over 
the 
medium 
term, 
Government 
intends 
to 
assess 
the 
results 
of 
the 
targeting 
pilots 
that 
are‘ 
being 
implemented 
and 
introduce 
a 
nationwide-targeted 
stipend 
and 
tuition 
program 
for 
secondary female 
students 
in 
the 
light 
of 
strategies 
outlined 
above. 
The 
burden 
of 
poverty 
continues 
to 
fall 
disproportionately 
on 
women. 
A 
related institutional 
issue 
is 
to 
increase 
the 
political 
voice 
of 
women, 
especially 
poor 
women, 
which 
will 
further 
enhance 
their 
agency 
role 
and 
hence, 
contribute 
to 
faster 
progress 
in 
the 
well

The  Government 
would 
actively  promote policies 
for 
increasing 
women’s 
participation 
in 
all 
spheres 
of 
development 
under 
the 
poverty 
reduction 
strategy. 
By 
addressing the 
critical 
needs, 
well-executed 
and 
well-targeted 
direct 
interventions 
would be 
used 
to 
significantly 
improve 
the 
welfare

the 
poor. 
A 
set

for

through 
works

The

will 
put 
emphasis 
on 
the

of 
social 
solidarity. 
The

towards 
ensuring 
good 
public 
health 
services 
to 
reduce 
health 
hazard
