Bangladesh: 
Medium-Term 
Macroeconomic 
Framework 
The 
Context 
1. 
When 
the 
present 
government 
took office 
in 
October 
2001, 
Bangladesh’s 
fiscal situation 
was 
weak. 
Declining 
exports, 
reduced 
FDI 
inflows 
and increased 
monetary 
accommodation 
of 
fiscal deficits 
threatened Bangladesh’s 
ability 
to 
even 
maintain, 
let 
alone 
accelerate, 
its 
growth 
and 
poverty reduction 
efforts. 
Global 
recession, 
across-the-board 
decline 
in 
export 
prices, 
rising 
oil 
prices, 
extremely 
slow 
progress 
in 
structural 
policy 
reforms 
and 
continued 
fragility 
in 
political 
and  economic  governance 
underpinned 
the 
vulnerable 
state 
of 
the 
economy. 
2. 
Several macroeconomic  fundamentals  weakened 
in 
the  late 
1990s. 
The 
central  govemment 
budget 
deficit 
increased 
to 
more 
than 5 
percent 
of 
GDP 
and 
domestic 
financing 
increased sharply 
to 
above 
3 
percent 
of 
GDP. 
As 
a 
result, 
central government 
domestic debt increased 
from 
13 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
FYOO 
to 
15 
percent 
in 
FYOl 
and 
the 
share 
of 
interest 
on 
domestic 
debt 
increased 
fkom 
13 
percent 
of 
current 
spending 
to 
16 
percent 
during 
the 
same 
period. 
The 
continued operation 
of 
the 
loss-malung 
state 
owned 
enterprises 
became 
a 
massive 
drain 
on 
public 
resources 
in 
the 
late 
1990s. 
Total 
SOE 
losses 
increased 
from 
Tk 
14 
billion 
in 
FY97 
to 
more 
than 
Tk 
33 
billion 
(1.3 
percent 
of 
GDP) 
in 
FYOl 
and 
total 
borrowing 
of 
these 
enterprises 
from 
the 
banking 
system 
reached 
nearly 
Tk 
74 
billion 
at the close 
of 
FYO 
1, 
equivalent 
to 
3 
percent 
of 
GDP. 
Foreign 
exchange reserves 
declined 
to 
around 
$1 
billion, 
equivalent 
to 
less 
than 
one 
month 
of 
imports 
of 
goods 
and 
services. 
3. 
Thanks 
to 
the 
resilience 
of 
Bangladesh’s economy, 
low 
initial 
levels 
of 
public 
debt 
and interest

fiscal

CPI

despite

Foreign

have 
been 
rebuilt 
to 
around 
$1.7 
billion, 
reflecting 
both 
the 
impact 
of 
a 
tighter 
fiscal

as 
well 
as 
recourse 
to

to 
contain

Central 
Government 
deficit 
was 
reduced 
from 
5.1 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
FYOl 
to 
4.7

in 
FY02 
through 
both 
additional 
revenue 
effort 
and expenditure 
discipline. 
The 
FY03

aims 
to 
reduce the 
deficit 
further 
to 
4.2 
percent 
of 
GDP, 
and 
its 
domestic 
financing 
to 
about 
2 
percent 
of 
GDP. 
4. 
Monetary 
policy 
has 
also 
been 
kept 
tight 
and, 
along 
with 
fiscal 
restraint, 
this

the

net 
international

to 
the

of

following

in

The

and curb

rates 
exhibited 
some

in 
November 
2002, 
reflecting 
depreciation 
expectations 
and 
a temporary 
increase 
in 
the Government’s 
domestic

requirements. 
The 
pressures 
on 
the

rate 
have 
eased 
and 
the 
rate 
has 
been

at  appropriate, 
broadly 
stable

over 
the 
last 
six 
months, 
with 
the 
curb 
market 
premium 
remaining 
well 
below 
2 
percent. 
An 
improved legal 
and regulatory framework 
in 
the 
banlung 
sector 
accompanied sound management 
of 
the exchange rate. 
5. 
Budgetary 
financing 
of

the

of

and

and capital restructuring, 
has

costly 
in 
the past. 
These 
contributed considerably 
to 
the

in

during

The

in

improve

by

Equally

Public

of

NCB

would 
thus

a

The recent

in

The

6. 
The 
improvement 
in 
the 
investment 
climate 
resulting 
from 
the 
structural 
reform 
measures 
already 
implemented 
should significantly 
grease 
the 
economic 
recovery 
that 
appears 
to 
be 
underway. 
Over 
the 
last 
twelve 
months 
ending 
November 
2002, 
industrial 
production 
and 
export 
growth 
have recovered 
to 
6.6 
percent and 
-0.2 
percent respectively,  compared 
with 
1.6 
percent 
industrial production 
growth 
and 
-2.9 
percent 
export 
growth 
over 
the 
12 
months 
ending November 
2001. 
kven 
by 
agncultural production 
and 
export growth, 
real 
GDP 
growth 
in 
FY03 
is 
thus 
expected 
to 
rise 
to 
an estimated 
5.2 
percent. 
However, 
inflation 
in 
FY03 
could 
rise 
to 
a 
still 
modest 
5 
plus 
percent, 
reflecting 
the 
lagged effects 
of 
energy 
price 
adjustments. 
To 
the 
extent 
this 
is 
the 
case, 
the 
rise 
in 
inflation 
need 
not be 
a 
major 
concern 
since 
it 
is 
a 
manifestation 
of 
relative 
price 
corrections, 
which 
became essential 
due 
to 
changes 
in 
intemational 
prices. 
The 
real 
choice 
was 
between 
keeping 
inflation 
repressed 
by 
not 
adjusting 
energy 
prices, 
and 
thus 
worsening 
price 
distortions, 
or 
bringing 
inflation 
out 
in 
the open 
to 
correct 
price 
distortions and 
send 
the 
consumers 
the 
right 
market 
signals. 
To 
the 
extent 
the 
rise 
in 
inflation 
was 
driven 
by 
utility 
price 
increases, 
it 
can 
be 
expected 
to 
last 
only 
as 
long 
as 
it 
takes 
for 
utility 
price 
increases 
to 
feed 
into 
prices 
of 
commodities and 
services 
where 
the 
intensity 
of 
utility 
use 
is 
high. 
The 
Medium-Term 
Macroeconomic 
Framework 
7. 
The  Govemment 
has 
put 
in 
place 
a 
prudent 
and 
sustainable 
medium-term 
macro-economic 
framework 
for 
increasing 
growth 
and 
poverty 
reduction 
(Table 
Cl). 
The 
framework 
is 
highly 
dependent 
on 
the 
assumption that 
the 
impact 
of 
a possible 
war 
in 
Iraq 
would 
be 
transitory and 
that Bangladesh 
would 
take 
enough actions 
to 
avert 
losing 
export market 
share 
following 
the 
MFA 
phase-out 
in 
2005. 
While not 
too 
ambitious, the 
growth 
rates envisaged 
for 
the 
next 
five 
years 
represent 
a 
significant 
increase 
from 
recent 
performance. 
The 
framework 
envisages 
a 
realistic 
recovery 
of 
GDP growth 
rates 
from 
the 
expected 
5.2 
percent 
in 
FY03 
to 
5.5 
percent 
in 
FY04, 
increasing 
further 
to 
6 
percent 
in 
FY05 
and 
plateauing at 
6.5 
percent 
in 
FY06 
and 
onwards. 
The 
Govemment’s I-PRSP 
envisages 
raising growth 
to 
7 
percent over 
the 
medium 
to 
long 
term, 
which 
in 
conjunction 
with 
pro-poor 
policies 
would 
help 
achieve 
the 
I-PRSP 
goal 
of 
halving 
the 
number 
of 
people 
living 
below 
the 
poverty 
line 
by 
2015. 
Inflation 
is 
projected 
to 
decline 
to 
4 
percent 
from 
FY04 
and 
onwards. 
Page  77
Annex 
C 
Page 
3 
of 
7 
8. 
GDP 
growth would 
be 
anchored 
in 
larger investment 
by 
a 
more 
vibrant 
private 
sector 
(including 
FDI), 
a 
strong increase 
in 
the 
efficiency 
of 
investment and a 
further 
building 
up 
of 
human capital and 
physical 
infrastructure. 
Growth 
in 
Bangladesh 
has been 
well 
below 
its 
potential 
during 
the 
last 
decade 
as 
a 
result 
of 
the 
poor 
investment 
climate, 
political 
and 
economic 
instability, 
inefficient public 
spending, 
and 
poor 
governance. 
The 
achievement 
of 
the 
growth 
targets 
hinge 
critically 
on 
the 
timely 
implementation 
of 
reforms, 
particularly 
in 
the 
areas 
of 
macroeconomic 
management, 
tax 
reform 
and 
composition 
of 
public 
expenditures, trade 
and 
exchange 
rate 
regimes, 
reforms 
of 
the 
SOEs 
and 
NCBs, 
energy 
pricing 
and 
regulation,  and 
general 
economic 
governance. 
Priority 
is 
being 
given 
to 
these 
areas 
under 
the 
Bank’s 
dialogue 
with 
the 
Government 
on 
the 
Development 
Support 
Credit. 
These 
reforms 
will 
create 
a 
positive 
environment 
for 
private 
investment 
leading 
to 
more 
efficient 
resource 
allocation 
and 
utilization. 
Higher 
economic 
growth 
needs 
to  be 
complemented 
by 
pro-poor 
measures 
in 
order 
to 
achieve 
the 
I-PRSP 
poverty 
reduction 
target, 
This 
would 
require 
shifting 
public 
resources 
toward 
improving 
the 
access 
of 
the 
poor 
to 
essential 
public 
services such 
as 
basic education, health, 
infrastructure 
services 
and 
addressing 
governance 
issues 
in 
these 
sectors 
to 
make 
sure 
that 
the 
poor 
indeed 
benefit 
from 
the 
additional 
public 
resources. 
Also, improvements 
in 
these 
areas 
are 
among the 
prerequisites 
for 
buoyant 
private 
investment. 
9. 
Fiscal 
Policy. 
The 
path 
of 
fiscal 
policy 
in 
the 
medium-term framework 
is 
consistent 
with 
Bangladesh’s 
reform 
needs 
and 
public 
debt 
sustainability. 
Revenue 
mobilization 
is 
the 
central 
element 
of 
the 
budget 
strategy 
for 
the 
next 
three  years. 
This 
would 
help 
balance 
the 
need 
to 
support 
poverty 
reduction 
with 
the 
need 
to 
contain 
domestic 
financing. 
Also, 
over 
the 
next 
several 
years, 
the 
central 
Government 
budget 
will 
have 
to 
shoulder 
the 
burden 
of 
paying 
the  adjustment  cost 
of 
reforms 
and 
increases 
in 
development  expenditures 
on 
productive 
investment 
in 
education, 
health 
and 
poverty 
reduction. 
These 
are 
expected 
to 
result 
in 
an 
increase 
in 
the 
size 
of 
the 
fiscal 
deficit 
from 
its 
projected 
4.2 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
FY03 
to 
4.8 
percent 
in 
FY04 
and 
4.7 
percent 
in 
FY05. 
The 
wider 
fiscal deficits 
are 
expected 
to 
be 
financed 
by 
increased 
external 
financing 
on 
concessional terms based 
on 
a 
comprehensive 
poverty reduction 
plan. 
A 
more 
detailed description 
of 
the 
expected 
FY03 
budget 
outcomes and the 
indicative 
FY04 
budget 
is 
presented 
in 
Table 
C2. 
Page  78
Annex 
C 
Page 
4 
of 
7 
10. 
Total 
revenue 
in 
FY04 
is 
targeted 
to 
rise 
by 
14 
percent over 
its 
expected 
level 
in 
FY03 
while 
expenditure 
is 
planned 
to 
increase 
by 
17 
percent. 
Increased 
public 
investment 
will 
be 
needed 
to 
complement the 
growth 
of 
private 
investment 
in 
response 
to 
reforms. 
To 
contain 
the cost 
of 
public 
debt 
servicing, 
the Government 
will 
limit 
domestic 
financing 
to 
1.9 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
FY04, 
and 
will 
rely 
more 
on 
highly 
concessional 
external financing. 
If 
the latter 
turns 
out 
to 
be higher than 
the 
level 
currently 
projected, 
the 
overall 
budget 
deficit 
could be 
raised 
to 
5 
percent 
of 
GDP, 
to 
accommodate 
additional 
targeted 
poverty 
reduction 
programs 
and structural 
reform 
costs. 
In 
order 
to 
adjust 
to 
a 
possible 
shortfall 
in 
concessional external 
financing 
relative 
to 
the 
FY04 
target, 
the budget 
will 
be 
executed 
prudently 
by 
back-loading 
expenditures, 
particularly 
with 
respect 
to 
capital 
spending. 
The 
actions 
to 
be 
taken 
in 
the 
event 
of 
financing 
shortfalls 
would 
include rationalizing  and 
trimming 
expenditures 
and  additional 
revenue 
measures. 
1 
1. 
As 
mentioned 
above, 
efforts 
to 
boost 
revenues 
on 
a sustainable 
basis 
will 
be 
a 
core element 
of 
the 
Government’s 
medium-term fiscal 
strategy. 
Emphasis 
will 
be 
given 
to 
measures 
to 
broaden the 
tax 
net 
and 
improving 
tax 
administration. 
Revenue 
measures 
underpinning 
the 
FY04 
budget 
will 
include 
extending the 
income 
tax net 
and 
VAT 
nets; 
strengthening customs administration, 
including 
through 
revamping  the  bonded 
warehouse 
system; 
expanding 
the  audit 
program;  and 
setting 
up 
a 
central 
intelligence 
unit 
for 
the 
main 
taxes. 
The 
Government 
will 
take 
into 
account 
the recommendations 
of 
the 
Revenue 
Reforms 
Commission 
in 
its 
efforts 
to 
significantly 
enhance 
the 
revenue 
base. 
Furthermore, 
utility 
prices 
and 
administrative 
fees 
and 
charges 
will 
be 
adjusted 
on 
a 
timely 
basis 
to 
protect 
non-tax 
revenues.

The 
quality 
of

will

It

that,

no

projects 
will 
be 
added 
to 
the 
program 
as 
this 
undermines 
fiscal

and 
the

It

Adequate 
funding 
will 
be

to

13. 
The 
Govemment 
has 
committed 
to 
attach 
particular 
importance 
to 
improving 
fiscal 
transparency 
and control, especially 
fiscal reporting 
and watchdog institutions. 
Recent gains 
in 
fiscal reporting 
will 
be 
consolidated 
through implementing 
the 
action 
plan 
recommended 
by 
the 
Fiscal 
Transparency 
Module 
of 
the

on 
Observance 
of

and 
Codes 
(ROSC), 
as 
well 
as 
meeting 
key 
benchmarks 
under 
the

Reform 
Program. 
In 
particular, 
steps 
will 
be 
taken 
to 
strengthen

by 
allowing 
the 
office 
of 
the 
Auditor 
General 
to 
carry 
out audit 
functions independently. 
14. 
The 
External 
Sector 
and 
Balance 
of 
Payment 
Outlook. 
In 
order 
to 
put 
the 
economy 
on 
a 
path 
of 
accelerated and sustainable 
growth, Bangladeshi 
manufacturers and service 
providers 
need 
to 
find 
new 
products 
and export 
markets 
for 
the 
absorption 
of 
their 
supplies and 
services. 
It 
is 
reasonable 
to 
assume 
that, 
with 
a 
revival 
in 
the 
global 
economic environment, 
a 
flexible 
exchange 
rate 
regime and reduction 
in 
the domestic cost 
of 
doing 
business, 
Bangladesh 
would 
be 
able 
to 
raise 
merchandize 
export 
growth 
to 
about 
8-9 
percent 
per 
annum 
by 
FY05 
(Table 
Cl). 
Manufacturing 
and 
service 
oriented 
growth 
also 
means 
increased 
dependence 
on 
import 
growth, 
as 
is 
the 
case 
in 
any 
economy 
with 
growing 
openness. 
Import 
growth 
is 
therefore 
projected 
to 
rise 
back 
to 
an 
average 
annual 
rate 
of 
7 percent 
by 
FY05. 
Remittance 
from 
Bangladeshi workers  abroad 
can also 
be 
expected 
to 
grow 
at 
a 
healthy 
4-5 
percent 
annually, 
as 
demand 
for 
labor 
and 
wages 
in 
international labor 
markets 
return 
to normal 
levels. 
The 
required 
export, 
remittance and 
import growth 
rate 
for 
sustaining 
productivity 
growth 
imply 
that 
the 
current 
account 
deficit 
would 
rise 
to 
over 
1 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
FY04, 
primarily 
on 
account 
of 
capital 
imports. 
This 
is 
not 
unsustainable, 
given 
improved 
access 
to 
concessional 
financing 
due 
to 
structural 
reforms 
and 
macroeconomic 
stability. 
The 
balance 
of 
payments 
outlook 
is 
strengthened 
as 
exports 
are 
diversified, 
more 
workers’ 
remittances  are  channeled 
through 
the 
formal 
financial 
sector 
while 
a 
favorable 
business 
climate 
leads 
to 
higher 
FDI. 
15. 
The 
projections 
above 
are 
subject 
to 
significant 
downside 
risks, 
particularly 
higher 
oil 
prices, 
potential 
losses 
in 
workers’ 
remittances, 
and 
disruptions 
in 
exports 
in 
the 
event 
of 
a 
prolonged 
Iraqi 
conflict. 
Also, 
Bangladesh has 
lost 
valuable 
time 
in 
preparing 
to 
face 
the 
anticipated 
changes 
in 
its 
export 
markets 
following 
the 
MFA 
phase-out 
in 
2005.  Persistence 
with 
substantial 
structural 
reforms 
will 
help 
weather the 
shocks. 
Concessional 
foreign financing 
- 
both 
capital 
transfers 
and 
medium 
and 
long-term 
loans 
- 
will 
be 
critical for 
Bangladesh’s 
medium-term 
growth 
sustainability and 
reducing 
vulnerability 
to 
shocks. 
The 
present 
foreign 
exchange reserve 
level 
of 
just 
two 
months 
import 
equivalent 
is 
low 
and 
needs 
to 
be 
raised to 
a 
sustained 
3 months’ 
of 
imports 
by 
the 
end 
of 
FY04. 
A 
more 
accommodative 
fiscal 
stance 
and  emergency 
external 
support 
may 
also 
be 
needed 
to 
help 
avoid 
an 
excessive  economic 
slowdown 
due 
to 
exogenous shocks. 
16. 
Monetary 
and 
Exchange 
Rate 
Policies. 
Maintaining 
low 
inflation, 
increasing 
GDP 
growth 
and 
rebuilding 
international 
reserves 
require 
prudent 
monetary and 
exchange 
rate policies. 
Consistency 
is 
needed 
between 
the 
growth 
of 
broad money and 
the 
BB 
net 
domestic 
assets. 
Broad 
money 
growth 
in 
turn 
has 
to 
be 
in 
line 
with 
the 
growth 
of 
nominal GDP 
and 
the 
economy’s 
monetization 
needs. 
Given 
a 
prudent 
fiscal 
stance, 
tighter 
monetary 
policy 
relative 
to 
the 
experience 
in 
the 
recent past should 
have 
no 
adverse 
implications 
for 
the 
growth 
of 
credit 
to 
the 
private 
sector. 
Page  80
Annex 
C 
Page 
6 
of 
7 
17. 
In 
the 
indicative program 
agreed 
with 
the 
IMF 
(Table 
C3), 
the Government 
has 
committed 
to 
reserve 
money 
growth 
targets 
of 
4.5 
percent and 
9.6 
percent 
for 
FY03 
and 
FY04 
respectively, 
while 
broad 
money 
growth 
is 
expected 
to 
slow to 
12.1 percent 
in 
FY04. 
This 
would 
allow 
Bangladesh 
Bank to 
rebuild 
net 
international 
reserves, 
while 
accommodating 
required 
private 
sector 
credit  growth. 
The 
Government 
is 
also 
in 
the 
process 
of 
linking 
the 
yields 
on 
new 
issues 
of 
national 
savings 
certificates 
to 
the rates 
for 
treasury 
bills 
of 
comparable 
maturity 
and 
enhance 
coordination 
between 
foreign 
exchange 
and 
liquidity 
management operations. 
These 
will 
help 
reduce 
rigidities 
in 
the 
structure 
of 
interest 
rates 
and  augment 
the 
effectiveness 
of 
monetary 
policy. 
Further, 
reforms 
in 
financial 
markets  and  the 
development 
of 
a 
secondary 
market 
for 
treasury 
bills 
would 
allow 
the 
use 
of 
indirect 
instruments 
for 
monetary 
policy 
to 
enhance 
monetary 
management. 
The 
reforms 
in 
the 
monetary 
sector are 
expected 
to 
increase 
control 
of 
BB 
over 
NCBs 
and 
DFIs 
and 
lead 
to 
an 
efficient 
allocation 
of 
credit, 
largely 
to 
the 
private 
sector. 
This 
is 
made 
possible 
by 
reduced 
central  Government  reliance 
on 
banks 
for  deficit 
financing 
and 
with 
market determined 
lending 
rates. 
18. 
In 
the 
area 
of 
exchange 
rate 
policy, 
it 
is 
essential 
that 
the 
exchange 
rate 
is 
not 
over-valued and 
that 
exchange 
restrictions 
introduced 
in 
late 
2000 
and 
2001 
are 
completely 
dismantled.  Government 
is 
discussing 
with 
the 
IMF 
the 
possibility 
of 
early 
adoption 
of 
a 
market-based exchange 
rate 
system 
to 
boost 
the 
effectiveness 
of 
monetary 
policy, 
strengthen 
the 
Government’s 
capacity 
to 
address 
shocks 
on 
a 
timely 
basis 
and 
enhance 
export 
competitiveness 
in 
a challenging 
global 
environment. 
It 
will 
also 
help 
prevent 
exchange 
rate misalignments. 
A 
timetable 
has 
been 
adopted 
to 
phase 
out 
exchange 
restrictions 
under 
the 
Fund’s 
Article 
VIII. 
19. 
Public 
Debt 
Sustainability. 
Despite 
increases 
in 
total 
public 
debt 
from 
44.4 percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
FY98 
to 
about 
50 
percent 
in 
FYO1, 
Bangladesh 
remains 
among 
the 
“less 
indebted low-income’’ 
countries 
with 
the 
ratio 
of 
net 
present 
value 
of 
external 
debt 
to 
exports 
at about 105 percent 
in 
2001. 
Nearly 
two- 
third 
of 
the 
total public 
debt 
comprise 
of 
highly 
concessional 
external 
debt. 
Current 
public 
debt 
would 
be 
sustainable 
under 
the 
fiscal 
and  external 
policy 
path 
envisaged 
in 
the  macroeconomic  framework. 
Downside deviation 
from 
this 
path 
could 
subject 
Bangladesh 
to 
unsustainable 
debt 
dynamics. 
A 
recent 
IMF 
exercise examined 
what 
would 
happen 
to 
the 
public 
sector 
debt-GDP 
ratio 
if 
all 
key 
variables 
remained 
at 
their 
average 
value 
for 
the 
past 
seven 
years. 
This 
analysis 
suggests 
that 
the 
baseline, 
which 
assumes 
a 
slight 
increase 
in 
public 
debt 
from 
about 
51 
percent 
of 
GDP 
to 
53  percent, 
compares 
unfavorably 
against 
the 
historical 
scenario. 
This 
is 
because 
the 
baseline 
assumes 
a 
lower 
3.8 
percent 
GDP 
growth 
rate 
during 
FY03-08 
than 
the 
recent 
historical 
average 
of 
5 
percent, 
a 
higher 
average 
nominal 
interest 
rate 
on 
public 
debt 
(5 
percent 
compared 
with 
2.8 
percent), 
and 
a 
slow 
but 
steady 
decline 
in 
the 
primary deficit 
from 
2.1 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
FY03 to 
1.4 
percent 
of 
GDP 
over 
the 
projection 
period, 
compared 
with 
a 
historical 
average 
of 
1.8 
percent 
of 
GDP. 
20. 
Applying 
individual 
and combined 
adverse shocks 
to 
key 
economic 
variables 
in 
FY03-04, 
IMF 
stress tests 
on 
the 
baseline 
projections 
suggest 
that, 
even 
under 
significant 
adverse 
shocks, 
the 
public 
debt 
is 
likely 
to 
remain 
manageable at 
under 
70 
percent 
of 
GDP. 
The 
greatest 
risk 
to 
the 
baseline scenario 
apparently 
comes 
from 
a 
significant 
real 
depreciation 
of 
the 
exchange 
rate and 
a 
sudden 
increase 
in 
other 
debt-creating 
flows, 
the 
latter arising 
for 
example 
from 
a 
recapitalization 
of 
NCBs or 
an assumption 
by 
the 
Government 
of 
SOE 
debt whose 
costs 
are 
not 
incorporated 
into 
the baseline 
scenario.  Shocks 
to 
the 
real 
interest 
rate, 
GDP 
growth, 
or 
to 
the 
primary 
balance have 
relatively 
modest impacts 
on 
the 
debt 
dynamics. 
While 
this 
analysis 
does 
not 
take 
into 
account the 
contingent 
liabilities 
of 
the 
Government, 
which 
could 
be 
considerably 
large, 
it 
does 
reinforce 
the 
importance 
of 
adhering 
to 
the 
macroeconomic 
policy 
path 
envisaged 
in 
the Government’s 
medium-term 
framework.

1. 
A 
more

examination 
of

of 
the  deteriorating 
fiscal 
stance 
in 
the 
late 
90s, 
at the 
level 
of 
both 
the 
central 
Government and 
the 
consolidated 
public 
sector, 
derived 
a 
level 
of 
the 
primary deficit 
to

consistent 
with 
constant 
money-GDP and 
public 
debt 
to 
GDP 
ratios.32 
The 
results 
showed that 
the 
sustainable 
level 
of 
the Government’s 
primary 
deficit 
was 
2.7 
percent 
of 
GDP; 
well 
below 
the 
actual 
4.4 
percent 
in 
FYOO 
and 
the 
4.7 
percent 
in 
FYO1, 
but 
close 
to 
the 
2.9 
percent 
primary deficit 
achieved 
in 
FY02 
and 
above 
the 
2.2 
percent 
projected 
for 
FY03. 
Thus, 
while 
the 
increases 
in 
primary 
deficit, 
financed 
by 
costly 
domestic 
debt, 
in 
the late 
90s 
was 
not 
sustainable, 
current 
fiscal 
policy 
and 
the 
medium-term  fiscal 
framework 
is 
consistent 
with 
sustainability. 
The 
calculations 
of 
sustainable 
primary deficit 
levels 
for 
the 
Government 
and 
the 
consolidated 
public 
sector, 
using 
FYOl 
actual 
level 
as 
the benchmark, showed 
that a 
fiscal correction 
equivalent 
to 
1.8-2 
percentage 
points 
of 
GDP 
was 
required 
for 
the 
primary 
deficit 
in 
order 
to 
attain 
stationary 
liability 
ratios 
to 
GDP. 
Nearly two-third 
of 
the 
required reduction 
will 
be 
achieved 
by 
FY03. 
As 
Table 
C1 
shows, 
the 
medium- 
term 
fiscal 
deficit path 
adopted 
by 
the 
Government 
implies 
a 
stable 
total public 
debt-GDP 
ratio 
at around 
50-5 
1 
percent.

Coverage 
and 
access 
to

have 
exceeded 
expectations, 
especially 
when 
seen 
against the 
backdrop 
of 
the 
performance 
of 
other 
countries 
in 
the 
region 
(Table 
F.l). 
The 
main 
successes 
include 
(i)

of

72

in

in

(ii) 
a

of

enrollments; 
and

among

poor. 
In

in

and

expanded at 
an

during

and

are 
comparable 
to 
Sri 
Lanka.

eventually

Three

These

(i)

 resource^;^'

8. 
Bangladesh’s 
public 
policy 
and 
expenditures have 
consistently 
emphasized education, 
and 
the 
sharp increases 
in

have

Education 
is 
by

largest

of

in

ADP

Total

on

is

about 
2.2

of

and 
15-16

(Table

Most

Le.,

and

While

9. 
Expansion 
of

took

There

in

in

and

37,7 
10 
government

(GPS) 
and 
19,683 
registered non-govemment

(RNGPS)

In

and

Compared

thus

costs.

Key 
Sector 
Issues 
14. 
GOB 
has 
implemented 
several 
policies 
in 
the 
education 
sector, 
which 
have 
yelded 
laudable 
results. 
The 
outcomes 
certainly 
would 
have 
been 
much 
better 
if 
the 
handicaps, 
mostly 
of 
the 
institutional 
nature, 
did 
not hold 
back 
the 
system. 
A 
consensus 
defines 
the 
measures 
needed 
in 
the 
field: 
(a) 
raise 
the 
amount 
of 
public 
resources 
devoted 
to 
basic education; 
(b) 
continue 
to 
improve 
access 
and 
equity; 
(c) 
establish  better 
partnerships 
with 
relevant 
stakeholders 
(parents, 
communities, 
non-government 
institutions, 
etc.) 
to 
improve 
quality 
of 
education 
services; 
(d) 
provide 
adequate 
teacher 
training 
and 
other 
needed 
pedagogical 
inputs, 
as 
well 
as 
(e) 
undertake 
better 
assessments 
of 
learning and 
outcomes.  There 
is 
also 
a 
need 
to 
improve 
management 
and accountability, 
reduce 
corruption 
and 
waste, 
and 
de-politicize 
the education 
system. 
15. 
Governance 
is 
a 
key 
constraint 
on

the 
quality 
of 
education. 
A 
recent

on

problems 
in 
Bangladesh 
found 
growing 
dissatisfaction 
among 
the 
parents 
of 
school 
children 
with 
the 
quality 
of 
education 
in 
general 
and, 
most 
acutely 
with 
schools

24. 
Bangladesh 
has 
an 
impressive 
record 
of 
health 
outcomes over 
the 
years. 
Infant 
and 
under 
five 
mortality 
has 
been 
reduced 
by 
50 
percent, 
total 
fertility 
rate 
halved through voluntary

of 
contraceptive,

expectancy  increased 
by 
14 
years,

have

of

and malaria, 
as 
well

in

of

The

is 
an 
MDG 
of 
the 
Government 
that already has 
been 
achieved. 
These advances are 
due 
to

with

especially

of

Sound 
interventions 
in 
other 
sectors, 
such 
as 
wider

of 
food, expansion 
of 
education and 
access 
to 
safe 
water, 
leading 
to 
an increase 
in 
peoples' demand 
for 
better 
health 
services have also 
been 
important 
contributing 
factors. 
Taking into 
account 
the 
government's 
low 
level 
of 
resources, 
the 
national health 
policy 
rightly 
focused 
on 
the 
most 
pressing 
needs 
of 
the 
population 
- 
primary

care 
in 
rural 
areas, 
population 
control,

25.

26. 
Besides, 
child 
malnutrition 
short-term, acute 
food 
deficits 
(as

as

is

600

The

The

has made

15

The

For

The

rate 
fell 
even

Recent

Comparing distributions

Bangladesh has

than

and Vietnam,

to 
the

as

The

for

subsidies-only

of

A

phase

HPSP), 
and 
about  delays 
in 
scaling 
up 
nutrition 
programs, 
restructuring 
the 
HIV/AIDS 
prevention 
project, 
as 
well 
as 
the 
lack 
of 
accommodation 
of 
arsenic 
related activities 
within 
the HPSP. 
Despite 
that, 
in 
its 
Conceptual 
Framework 
for 
the 
next 
phase 
of 
the 
program, 
the 
Govemment 
has 
clearly 
stated 
its 
intention 
to 
continue 
the 
sector-wide 
programmatic 
approach 
in 
the 
sector, 
to 
include 
both 
nutrition 
and urban health 
services under 
the 
framework, 
commission 
a 
full 
review 
of 
operational 
strengths 
and 
weaknesses 
of 
PHSP, 
and 
implement 
the 
recommendations 
of 
the 
review. 
Key 
sector 
issues 
38. 
Despite 
this 
relatively 
strong 
record, 
there 
are 
still 
numerous 
gaps 
in 
health 
outcomes. 
Bangladesh 
is 
not 
likely 
to 
achieve 
some 
MDGs: 
(i) 
at 
392 
per 
100,000 
maternal 
mortality 
rate 
is 
among 
the 
highest 
outside 
of 
Sub-Saharan 
Africa 
- 
this 
is 
due 
mainly 
to 
low 
level 
of 
clinic 
delivery 
(95 
percent 
of 
deliveries 
take 
place 
at 
home), 
and 
insufficient 
number 
of 
trained 
mid-wives; 
and 
(ii) 
despite 
increasing 
use 
of 
contraceptives, 
the 
total 
fertility 
rate 
(TFR) 
has 
been 
stagnating 
around 
3.2 
percent 
for 
the 
last 
5-6 
years-this 
is 
due, 
in 
part, 
to 
insufficient diversification 
of 
available 
modem 
contraceptives 
and 
their 
irregular 
use. 
Another 
MDG-child 
malnutrition-remains 
a 
challenge. 
A 
first 
generation 
of 
strategic goals 
in 
reducing 
malnutrition 
has 
been 
achieved 
in 
Bangladesh. 
This 
includes 
the 
generation 
of 
high 
level 
of 
policy 
support, 
development 
of 
a programmatic 
approach 
and 
evolution 
of 
conducive 
structures 
in 
the 
health 
and 
food policy 
arena. 
While 
future economic 
growth 
will 
provide 
strong impetus 
for 
continued reductions 
in 
malnutrition, 
it 
is 
most 
likely 
that 
achievement 
of 
this 
MDG 
goal 
will 
require 
additional 
interventions, 
which, 
inter 
alia 
should 
include 
improvements 
in 
access 
to 
clean 
water, 
currently 
threatened 
by 
such contaminants 
as 
arsenic 
and 
pathogens. 
Future 
interventions  should 
also 
build 
on 
the 
success 
of 
BINP 
to 
expand coverage 
of 
nutrition 
programs. 
There 
are 
other 
lingering 
problems 
such 
as: 
low 
birth 
weight, 
and persistence 
of 
infectious 
diseases. 
In 
addition 
to 
the 
emerging 
additional 
burden 
of 
arsenic 
poisoning, 
there 
are serious 
challenges associated 
with 
the 
changing 
disease 
pattems. 
Besides 
the 
shortfalls 
in 
health 
outcomes, 
substantial 
inequalities between 
poor 
and 
nonpoor 
persist 
in 
many health 
indicators, especially 
regarding 
child 
health. 
39. 
There 
are 
three 
institutional 
problems 
in 
the 
health 
care 
system, 
which 
if 
not 
addressed, 
will 
seriously 
inhibit 
GOB’S ability 
to 
address 
the 
increasing 
health 
needs 
of 
the 
pop~lation.~~ 
These 
problems 
47 
There 
is 
also 
growing 
perception 
of 
increasing 
corruption 
in 
health 
service 
delivery.  Doctors 
as 
well 
as 
the 
public 
are 
very 
vocal 
about 
systemic 
corruption 
regarding 
procurement, 
registration 
of 
clinics, 
appointment, posting, 
and 
promotion 
of 
medical 
professionals, 
the 
provision 
of 
medicine 
and supplies. 
Even after 
admission, 
extra 
payments 
are 
routine 
aspects 
of 
treatment 
whether 
in 
Govemment 
or 
private 
facilities 
Another 
set 
of 
problems 
pertains to 
the 
rigidities 
in 
budget management and 
duality 
of  health 
budget 
leading 
to 
sub-optimal 
geographic 
allocation 
rules 
and 
imbalances between 
recurrent 
and 
development 
expenditures.

Annex 
F 
Page 
13 
of 
17 
pertain 
to 
(i) 
slow 
progress 
of 
HPSP implementation; 
(ii) 
the 
lack 
of 
efficiency 
in 
the 
public 
sector; 
and 
(iii) 
the 
ill-defined 
role 
of 
the 
non-public 
sector. 
40. 
Implementation 
of 
HPSP 
has 
been 
slow 
and 
public 
expenditure 
on 
health 
fell 
in 
real 
per 
capita 
terms 
and 
as 
a 
share 
of 
total 
government expenditure 
during 
the 
first 
three 
years, 
only 
rising 
in 
the 
fourth 
year 
to 
pre-HPSP 
levels. 
The consumption 
of 
ESP 
services 
falls 
well 
behind 
the 
HPSP 
target 
of 
80 
per 
cent 
coverage. 
With 
the 
exception 
of 
maternal 
care, 
there 
is 
no 
evidence that 
HPSP 
has 
generated 
increases 
in 
health 
service 
volume 
or 
improvements 
in 
quality 
or 
efficiency 
at the 
provider level.  The 
following 
aspects 
of 
HPSP need 
strengthening: 
. 
. 
Poverty 
and 
gender 
related 
actions 
have 
yet 
to 
appear 
in 
Annual 
Operational 
Plans 
although 
important analytical 
work 
is 
being 
done 
by 
HEU 
and 
this 
issue 
is 
emphasized 
in 
the 
H"SP. 
Although 
important 
preparatory 
work 
has 
been 
done, 
none 
of 
the 
reforms planned 
under 
HPSP 
have 
been 
implemented 
to 
the 
full 
extent. 
This 
places the 
durability 
of 
preparatory 
work 
in 
jeopardy and vigorous 
implementation 
of 
HPSP 
reforms 
is 
needed. 
The 
development 
of 
procurement 
functions 
in 
MoHFW 
should 
proceed 
with 
utmost urgency. 
Strategies  and  mechanisms 
for 
engaging 
non-government 
providers 
especially 
in 
such 
important 
areas 
as 
BCC 
and 
HIV-AIDS 
should 
be 
further 
developed 
and 
implemented. 
Flow 
of 
financial 
information 
has 
been 
significantly 
improved, 
but 
management 
information 
system 
in 
the 
sector 
need 
further 
strengthening. 
. 
. 
41. 
Productivity 
of 
Thana 
and 
union 
facilities 
is 
very 
low 
by 
international 
standards. 
Poor 
facility 
management, 
sub-optimal 
location 
and 
staffing 
of 
facilities, 
as 
well 
as 
doctor 
absenteeism 
and 
inadequate 
provision 
of 
medical 
supplies, 
lead 
to 
low 
quality 
and 
hence 
low 
attendance 
rates 
and 
low 
productivity 
and 
high 
unit 
costs 
of 
these 
facilities 
in 
comparison 
to 
other large 
low-income 
countries. 
This 
situation 
is 
particularly 
worrisome 
since 
these 
facilities 
attract 
the 
bulk 
of 
resources, 
and 
represent 
the 
backbone 
of 
health 
service 
delivery. 
One 
of 
the 
reasons 
for 
low 
productivity 
is 
that government 
clinicians 
have weak 
incentives 
to 
increase 
their 
productivity 
in 
government work, 
since 
they 
can 
sell 
their 
services 
to 
private 
clients. 
42. 
Little 
progress 
in 
improving 
efficient mobilization 
of 
private 
providers 
has 
been 
achieved. 
Given 
the 
deep 
penetration 
of 
private health providers 
in 
the 
health 
market 
and 
large 
volume 
of 
private 
health 
spending, 
the issue 
of 
regulation, 
and 
enforcement 
of 
quality and 
standards 
becomes essential 
to 
ensure 
the 
effectiveness 
of 
private 
spending. 
PUBLIC 
SOCIAL 
SAFETY 
NET 
PROGRAMS 
Progress 
in 
reducing vulnerability 
43. 
Bangladesh 
has 
moved 
from 
being 
chronically 
in 
food 
deficit 
to 
self-sufficiency 
in 
rice 
production. 
Early 
investments 
in 
agricultural 
research, 
reforms 
in 
inputs 
markets 
(particularly 
for 
fertilizer, 
irrigation 
equipment, 
and 
seeds), 
and 
improvement 
in 
infrastructure 
have 
helped 
spur 
apcultural 
production. The 
government 
has 
privatized 
foodgrain 
distribution, 
lifted 
restrictions 
on 
rice 
and 
wheat 
imports 
by 
private 
traders, 
and 
reduced 
its 
presence 
in 
foodgrain 
markets, 
all 
actions 
that have 
contributed to 
improving 
availability 
and 
diminishing 
deficits 
in 
domestic 
production 
(Ahmed 
et. 
al., 
2000). 
Since 
liberalization 
of 
trade 
in 
the 
early 
nineties, 
private 
sector 
imports 
of 
rice 
have 
on 
several 
occasions 
contributed 
to 
filling 
the 
food availability 
gap 
after 
poor 
rice 
harvests 
(in 
1994-95 
and 
1995- 
96) 
and 
natural 
disasters 
(most 
notably, 
following 
the 
1998 
floods).  Seasonality 
in 
rice 
prices 
is 
also 
much 
reduced, 
a 
consequence 
of 
rapid growth 
in 
dry-season 
boro  rice 
production  and 
improved 
integration 
of 
markets 
in 
different 
regions. 
More 
importantly, 
a recent 
survey (2000 
HIES) 
data 
show that 
Page  102
Annex 
F 
Page14of17 
households 
in 
Bangladesh 
are 
by 
and 
large 
able 
to 
shield 
consumption 
of 
this 
staple  cereal 
from 
fluctuations 
in 
price. 
For 
most 
households, 
no 
significant 
intra-year 
variation 
in 
rice 
consumption 
is 
evident. 
44. 
Over 
the 
years, 
Bangladesh 
has 
also made 
notable 
progress 
in 
its 
capacity 
to 
deal 
with 
natural 
disasters. 
Large 
quantities 
of 
public 
resources 
are 
now 
directed 
to 
water  management, 
holding food 
stocks, 
emergency 
public 
works, 
and 
flood 
protection 
and 
cyclone 
shelters. 
Weather 
monitoring 
and 
early 
waming 
systems 
have 
been 
strengthened, 
and 
improved 
wireless 
operations have 
been 
set 
up 
in 
cyclone 
prone 
areas. 
The 
decline 
in 
numbers 
of 
deaths 
during 
crises 
reflects 
an 
improved 
ability to 
evacuate 
people 
from 
disaster 
zones 
and 
to 
provide 
shelter. 
At 
the 
same 
time, 
the 
spread 
of 
defensive 
infrastructure 
such 
as 
coastal 
and 
river 
embankments has 
helped 
to 
mitigate 
the 
impact 
of 
natural 
disasters. 
This 
enhanced 
capability, 
as 
well 
as 
the 
resilience 
of 
its 
people, was 
in 
ample evidence 
during 
the 
country’s 
resolute and 
effective 
response 
in 
1998 
to 
one 
of 
the 
century’s worst floods. 
45. 
Notwithstanding 
these 
successes, 
high 
levels 
of 
poverty 
magnify 
the 
adverse 
effects 
of 
natural 
disasters: 
poor 
people 
and 
poor 
communities 
are 
frequently 
the 
primary 
victims, 
in 
part 
because 
they 
are 
priced 
out 
of 
the 
more  disaster-proof 
areas 
and 
live 
in 
makeshift 
houses 
in 
areas 
with 
low 
quality 
infrastructure. 
In 
addition,  various 
types 
of 
economic 
and non-economic  shocks 
unrelated 
to 
natural 
disasters 
affect 
the 
poor 
disproportionately. 
These 
conditions 
justify 
the 
commitment 
of 
successive 
govemments 
and 
the 
donor 
community 
to 
ensure 
some 
social 
protection 
for 
the 
poor. 
In 
2001-02, 
total 
govemment 
spending 
on 
social 
safety 
net 
programs 
was 
approximately 
1 
percent 
of 
GDP 
and 
5.6 
percent 
of 
total 
govemment 
expenditures. 
Public 
safety 
net 
and 
its 
targeting 
outcomes 
46. 
Bangladesh 
has 
one 
of 
the 
largest 
systems 
of 
targeted transfer 
programs 
in 
the 
world. 
The 
long 
history 
of 
extensive 
social 
safety 
net 
provision, 
testament 
to 
the 
commitment 
of 
successive 
Governments 
to 
a 
minimum 
level 
of 
protection 
in 
the face 
of 
frequent 
natural 
calamities. 
Budgetary 
expenditures 
on 
safety 
net 
programs 
are 
equivalent 
to 
almost 
1 
percent 
of 
GDP, 
95 
percent 
of 
which 
are 
directed towards 
targeted 
food 
transfer programs 
(Table 
F.6). 
Four 
food 
assistance-based 
programs 
(FFW, 
FFE, 
TR, 
VGD) 
aim 
at 
promoting 
human 
capital 
development and creating 
productive 
physical 
assets, 
while 
two 
other 
programs 
(VGF, 
GR) 
aim 
at responding 
to 
the 
calamities-related 
emergency 
needs. 
The 
remaining 
5 
percent 
of 
GOB 
expenditures 
on 
safety 
net 
is 
directed 
towards 
smaller 
programs 
supporting 
the 
elderly, 
orphans, 
and 
widows. 
47. 
A 
number 
of 
evaluation 
studies 
have 
provided 
evidence that 
the 
four 
large 
safety 
net 
program 
(FFE, 
VGD, 
VGF, 
GR) 
are 
reasonably 
well-targeted 
towards 
the 
poor 
and 
those 
affected 
by 
disasters. 
They 
also succeed 
in 
achieving 
their 
developmental objectives. 
Estimates based 
on 
the 2000 
HIES 
data 
show 
that households 
from 
the 
lowest 20 
percent 
of 
the 
income 
distribution 
are 
nearly 
5 
times 
as 
likely 
to 
participate 
in 
the 
FFE 
program relative 
to 
the 
richest 
20 
percent 
of 
the 
population 
(Table 
F.7). 
These 
targeting 
outcomes 
are 
comparable 
to 
performance 
of 
targeted 
programs 
in 
the 
other 
countries. 
In 
addition,  there 
is 
strong evidence 
from 
the 
mid-1990s 
that 
the 
FFE 
program 
achieves 
its 
developmental 
objective 
by 
attracting 
poor 
children 
to 
school 
(Ravallion 
and 
Wodon 
2000). 
Estimates show 
that, 
on 
average, 
participation 
in 
the 
FFE 
program 
increases 
the 
probability 
of 
attending school 
by 
20 
percent. 
Preliminary 
evidence 
from 
the 
2000 
HIES 
shows 
that 
these 
gains 
have been 
sustained.

Two

targeted resources 
to

of

whde

The 
development

of

is

Recent

of

is

because

Recognizing

the 
Government 
has, 
with

from 
July

2002, 
discontinued

and

by 
an expanded

which 
is

for

Under

the

40

Tk.lOO 
per

if

sending 
one

to 
primary 
schools 
and 
Tk.125 
per 
month 
if 
they 
are 
sending 
more 
than 
one 
child. 
Total 
number 
of 
likely 
beneficiaries 
is 
estimated 
at 
7 
million 
children 
and the 
program 
is 
likely 
to 
cost about 
$150 
million 
annually. 
The 
net 
additional burden 
on 
the 
budget 
as 
a 
result 
of 
this 
program 
is 
estimated at about 
$35-40 
million 
annually.

sector issues 
52. 
Improved 
administration 
of 
the safety 
net 
programs rather than 
their 
expansion 
is 
the 
main policy 
imperative 
and 
would 
yield 
considerable gains 
in 
enhancing 
effectiveness 
of 
government-provided social 
protection. 
Actions to 
reduce 
leakage 
are 
(i) 
strengthening 
the 
administrative 
systems 
for 
the 
monitoring 
of 
expenditures 
(e.g., 
internal 
and  independent 
external 
audits,  strengthening 
participation 
of 
local 
bodies), 
and 
(ii)

public 
access 
to 
information 
(e.g., 
Client 
Satisfaction 
Surveys, 
Participatory 
Monitoring 
and Evaluation, 
Public 
Expenditure 
Trackmg 
Surveys).

53. 
There 
are 
other 
innovative 
program 
design features that 
may 
be 
considered 
in 
order 
to 
improve 
accountability 
while

costs. 
For 
instance, 
Smart Cards 
have been 
used 
in 
many 
countries 
to 
reduce 
administrative 
costs 
and 
the 
possibility 
of 
error 
and 
fraud 
in 
the 
provision 
of 
various 
 service^.^' 
Leakage 
in 
the 
Rural 
Maintenance Program 
(RMP) 
- 
a 
cash-for-work program 
employing 
rural 
women 
for 
road 
maintenance 
- 
is 
also 
believed 
to 
be 
very 
low 
because 
of 
the 
unique 
wage 
payment 
system. 
Salaries 
for 
each 
road 
maintenance association 
are 
paid 
by 
a 
bank 
transfer 
every 
two 
weeks 
directly 
into 
a 
maintenance 
crew bank 
account  that 
is 
operated 
by 
the  elected representative 
of 
the 
workers. 
Potential 
for 
leakage 
is 
minimized 
since 
certification 
by 
officials 
is 
not 
required 
to 
withdraw 
payments, 
in 
addition to 
which 
involvement 
of 
local 
govemment 
officials 
and 
NGOs 
like CARE 
helps 
introduce 
checks and balances 
into 
the 
system and 
thus 
improve 
accountability.

Other

AGRICULTURE 
AND 
RURAL DEVELOPMENT4’ 
Overview 
1. 
Despite 
rapid 
urbanization 
in 
the 
last 
decade, 
the 
rural 
sector 
still 
harbors 
80 
percent 
of 
the 
population. 
Agriculture 
remains 
the 
mainstay 
of 
Bangladesh economy 
contributing 
about 
25 
percent 
of 
the 
country’s 
GDP 
and 
employing 
about 
63 
percent 
of 
its 
labor 
force. 
Agricultural 
production 
is 
dominated 
by 
crops 
with 
rice 
covering 
about 75 percent 
of 
cultivated 
land 
and 
contributing 
14 percent 
to 
total 
GDP. 
Fisheries 
currently 
contributes 
6 
percent 
of 
GDP 
with 
marine 
capture 
accounting 
for 
more 
than 
half 
of 
fish 
production 
in 
the 
country, 
but 
inland 
culture 
fisheries 
(shrimp 
and 
pond 
fisheries) 
providing 
an 
important 
source 
of 
export 
earning. 
After 
several 
decades 
of 
slow 
growth 
(1.6-2.8 
percent 
per 
year), 
agricultural 
value 
added 
grew 
at 
4.7 
percent 
per 
year 
in 
the 
second 
half 
of 
the 
1990s 
largely 
due 
to 
the 
acceleration 
in 
food 
grain 
production 
(6.6 
percent 
per 
year 
in 
1995-00) 
and consistently 
high 
growth 
in 
the 
fisheries 
sub-sector (8-9 percent 
in 
the 
1990-00). 
The 
rural 
non-farm 
(RNF) 
sector, 
which 
produced 
about 28 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
1995-96, has 
grown 
at 
a 
compound 
rate 
of 
7 
percent 
a 
year 
during 
1991-96 
allowing 
for 
employment 
growth 
of 
2.6 
percent 
a 
year (compared 
to 
1 
percent 
employment 
growth 
in 
agriculture). 
This 
reflected 
sustained expansion 
in 
services 
and 
small 
scale 
manufacturing. 
2. 
Productivity-led 
acceleration 
of 
agricultural 
production, 
diversification 
within 
and 
outside 
of 
the 
agricultural 
sector, 
development 
of 
rural 
infrastructure  and 
import 
liberalization policies 
have 
greatly 
transformed 
the 
rural 
economy. Three 
major 
outcomes 
of 
this 
transformation 
include: 
(i) 
elimination 
of 
famines 
and 
attaining 
food 
grain 
self-sufficiency 
(ii) 
decline 
in 
income 
poverty 
and 
(iii) 
containment 
of 
the 
impact 
of 
the 
natural 
disasters. 
Public 
Spending 
and 
Policies 
3. 
GOB 
policies 
have 
traditionally 
emphasized the 
development 
of 
the 
rural 
economy 
as 
a 
means 
to 
alleviate 
poverty 
and 
contain 
the 
impact 
of 
natural 
calamities. 
In 
the 
early 
1970s 
and 
1980s, 
broad 
agriculture 
sector 
claimed 
over 
30 
percent 
of 
all 
public 
 investment^.^' 
Since the 
late 
1980s, 
there have 
been noticeable improvements 
in 
the 
policy 
framework 
for rural 
and 
agricultural 
development, 
namely 
the 
previous 
emphasis 
on 
direct market 
interventions 
and 
large 
capital 
spending 
on 
flood 
control, 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
projects 
has 
been 
replaced 
by 
a 
strategy based 
on 
human 
resource development and 
market- 
oriented 
agricultural 
growth 
to 
develop  the 
rural 
economy. 
Consequently 
while 
most 
of 
public 
expenditures focused 
on 
broad 
agricultural 
development 
in 
the 
1980s 
(with 
relatively 
low 
emphasis 
on 
rural 
infrastructure), 
the 
Government today 
allocates 
a 
larger 
share 
of 
expenditures 
on 
education 
and 
rural 
infrastructure-roads 
and electricity-to 
achieve 
the 
above 
objective 
(Table 
Gl). 
49 
This 
Annex 
draws 
heavily 
on 
Bangladesh 
Public 
Expenditure Review, 
joint 
report 
by 
the 
World 
Bank 
and 
Asian 
Development 
Bank 
2002. 
Public 
expenditures 
in 
agriculture 
are 
classified 
in 
narrow 
(traditional) 
and 
broad 
definitions. 
Narrow 
definition 
of 
agriculture 
includes 
crop, 
forestry, 
food 
marketing, 
fisheries 
and 
livestock. 
Broad definition 
in 
addition 
includes 
rural 
infrastructure, 
institutions, 
flood 
control 
and 
water 
resources.

4. 
Because 
of 
the 
highly 
disaster-prone 
environment 
in 
Bangladesh, 
the 
government 
continuously 
maintained 
high 
level 
of 
disaster-mitigation 
expenditures 
as 
a 
means 
to 
protect 
the 
population. 
Due 
to 
Bangladesh’s 
topology 
- 
the 
flow 
of 
the 
Ganges, 
the 
Brahmaputra and 
the Meghna, 
in 
a 
flat 
delta 
plain-- 
the 
biggest 
problem 
in 
disaster management 
is 
flood 
management 
and 
drainage 
in 
the 
wet 
season. 
Consequently, large 
amounts 
of 
public 
resources 
(0.6 
percent 
of 
GDP) 
are 
directed 
to 
(i) 
water management 
control, especially 
drainage; 
(ii) 
holding 
food 
stocks; 
(iii) 
emergency 
relief; 
and 
(iv) 
flood 
protection 
and 
cyclone 
shelters. 
Expenditures 
on 
disaster, 
and 
flood 
management 
in 
particular, 
are 
implemented 
through 
various 
line 
ministries. 
The 
largest 
allocation 
is 
made 
to 
water 
management 
and 
flood 
control, 
drainage 
and 
erosion control, 
whch 
constitutes 
25 
percent 
of 
broad 
agnculture expenditure (Table 
G. 
1). 
Also 
a 
significant 
part 
of 
the 
government’s one 
million 
tons 
food 
stock 
is 
intended 
for 
emergency 
relief. 
The 
long-term impact 
of 
sustained 
levels 
of 
expenditures 
in 
ths 
area, 
in 
addition 
to 
the 
improved 
environment 
of 
cooperation 
with 
NGOs, 
is 
that today 
the 
sensitivity 
of 
both 
agricultural 
and 
non-agricultural 
sector 
components 
of 
GDP 
to 
natural 
disasters 
has 
declined dramatically. 
5. 
Weather 
monitoring 
and early 
warning 
systems 
have been 
strengthened. 
The 
ability 
to 
evacuate 
people 
from 
disaster 
zones 
and 
to 
provide 
shelter 
has 
improved. The 
1998 
flood 
was unprecedented 
in 
its 
scale 
and 
duration, 
especially 
in 
the 
central 
part 
of 
Bangladesh. 
Despite 
the 
scale 
of 
damage, 
no 
major 
shortages 
of 
food 
items 
were reported 
at 
any 
time 
throughout 
the 
flood 
period, and 
households were 
by 
and large 
able 
to 
maintain 
consumption levels. Also, 
the  1998 
flood 
inflicted 
a 
far 
lower 
death 
toll 
(1,100 
fatalities) 
compared 
to 
an 
estimated 
staggering 
138,000 
deaths 
during 
the  1991 cyclone, 
an 
estimated 
2,055 
deaths 
during 
the 
1987 
flood, 
and 
an estimated 
2,000-6,500 
fatalities 
during 
the 
1988 
floods (Pantelic et 
al, 
2000).” 
Disaster-coping 
mechanisms 
at  the 
micro  level 
are 
also 
much 
stronger  today. 
6. 
A 
number 
of 
other strategic 
policies 
accompanied 
and 
facilitated 
public 
and private 
investments 
in 
rural 
development 
and 
allowed for 
shifts 
in 
public 
expenditures. 
Input 
market 
reform,

and 
tax 
policies 
and 
strategic

with 
NGOs, 
played 
a  substantial 
supporting

in 
Bangladesh’s

transition. 
Liberalization 
of 
input markets 
7.

is 
among 
the 
few

countries  that  have  considerably 
liberalized 
seed 
policy. 
In 
1984-85, the

was 
allowed 
to 
import 
seed. 
Liberalization 
of

policy 
proceeded 
further 
in 
1992 
with 
liberalization 
in 
procurement 
of 
imports (including 
seeds 
of 
notified 
crops) 
and

et. al. 
2000.

domestic

The

was

in

8. 
The fertilizer market

of

placed 
in

Production 
of

of

continued 
to

Unfortunately,

and

Economic

with

to 
about 
Tk7

The

bome 
by

and 
only

Import 
and 
tax 
policies 
9. 
The withdrawal 
of 
restrictions 
on 
import 
of 
irrigation 
equipment 
and 
pumping 
installation 
in 
the 
late 
1980s 
induced 
fast expansion 
of 
irrigated 
area. 
It 
also 
allowed 
a 
shift 
in 
public 
investments 
away 
from 
large 
irrigation 
investments 
into 
development 
of 
rural 
infrastructure. 
As 
a result, 
today 
less 
than 
10 
percent 
of 
irrigated 
land 
in 
Bangladesh 
is 
serviced 
from 
public 
irrigation 
facilities, 
although 
distribution 
of 
irrigation 
development 
across 
the 
districts 
of 
Bangladesh 
is 
very 
uneven.  Because coastal 
and 
north- 
east 
low-lying 
areas 
do 
not 
have 
easy 
access 
to 
tubewell 
irrigation, 
Bangladesh Water 
Development 
Board’s 
(BWDB) 
flood 
control 
and 
drainage 
projects 
concentrated 
in 
these 
areas. 
10. 
The 
exemption 
of 
duty 
on 
power 
tillers 
and 
engines 
in 
1989 
was 
instrumental 
in 
increasing 
their 
use, 
while 
the 
duty 
reduction 
on 
equipment 
and 
raw 
materials 
for 
food 
processing 
helped 
develop 
the 
non-apcultural 
rural 
sector. 
Partnership 
with 
NGOs 
in 
providing 
social 
services 
and 
micro-credit 
1 
1. 
Recognizing 
the 
enomous 
task 
ahead 
GOB 
encouraged 
NGGled 
development 
of 
micro-credit 
institutions. 
Following 
the 
success 
of 
the Grameen 
Bank 
in 
1976, 
micro-credit 
initiatives 
flourished 
in 
rural 
areas 
over 
the 
last 
two 
decades. 
A 
ground-breaking 
feature 
of 
these 
initiatives 
was 
that 
they 
targeted 
not 
only 
those 
lacking 
the 
collateral, 
but 
also 
women 
in 
particular. 
In 
2000, 
micro-credit 
programs 
delivered 
by 
the 
Government and 
NGOs 
covered 
over 10 
million 
individuals 
(Grameen 
Bank 
2.4 
million, 
BRAC 
3.3 
million, 
Proshika 
1.7 
million, 
Association 
for 
Social Advancement 
1.2 
million). 
A 
number 
of 
studies 
have 
shown 
that 
NGO 
activities 
are 
well 
targeted 
to 
the 
poor 
and 
women and 
have 
positive impact 
on food 
consumption,  savings behavior, 
and 
health 
and education 
outcomes 
(Zeller 
et 
al. 
2001, 
and 
citations 
therein.) 
Over 
time, 
large 
volume 
of 
public 
and donor 
resources 
have become available 
through 
these 
credit 
programs. 
In 
particular, 
the 
Palli 
Karma 
Sahayak 
Foundation 
(PKSF)  was established 
as 
an 
apex 
organization 
to 
provide 
credit through 
various partner organizations 
that 
on-lend 
these 
funds 
to 
their 
clients. 
These 
initiatives contributed 
to 
raising 
incomes 
and 
human 
development 
outcomes 
in 
the 
rural 
areas 
of 
Bangladesh. 
Sustained 
emphasis 
on 
crop research 
12. 
About 
30 
percent 
of 
ADP 
expenditures 
in 
the 
two 
successful 
agriculture 
sub-sectors-rice 
and 
fisheries-are 
allocated 
to 
research 
activities. 
Even 
though 
public 
investments 
in 
research 
were 
relatively 
low 
in 
absolute 
terms 
(3-4 
percent 
of 
broad 
agriculture 
expenditure, 
or 
0.4 percent 
of 
agncultural 
GDP 
during 
1980-2000), 
it 
paid 
off 
very 
well. 
The 
Bangladesh 
Rice 
Research 
Institute 
(BFUU) 
has 
released 
about 
45 
high 
yielding 
rice 
varieties 
(HYVs) 
that 
are 
used 
in 
60 
to 
65 
percent 
of 
rice 
area. 
Empirical

Annex 
G 
Page 
4 
of 
6 
evidence shows 
that 
rice 
research 
in 
Bangladesh 
raised 
TFP 
in 
rice 
production 
by 
around 
1 
percent 
per 
annum 
during 
1975-1997. 
The 
rapid 
development 
of 
the 
fisheries 
sector 
can 
also 
be 
attributed 
in 
part 
to 
success 
in 
adopting 
new 
fish 
varieties and technologies. 
Key 
Issues 
and 
Policy 
Priorities 
13. 
An 
important 
achievement 
of 
past 
rural 
development 
policies 
in 
Bangladesh 
is 
the 
more 
broadly 
shared 
pattem 
of 
growth 
in 
rural 
areas 
as 
compared 
to 
urban 
areas. 
Even 
though 
growth 
in 
average 
per- 
capita 
expenditure 
over 
the past 
decade 
was 
lower 
in 
rural 
as 
compared 
to 
urban 
areas 
(1.7 
vs. 
2.8 
percent 
per 
annum), 
rural 
growth 
was 
much 
more 
evenly 
distributed 
across 
income 
levels. 
Consequently, 
the 
poverty rate 
has 
declined 
at the 
same 
rate 
across 
urban 
and 
rural 
areas, 
and 
severe 
poverty 
has 
declined 
faster 
in 
rural 
areas 
as 
compared 
to 
urban 
areas. 
Growth 
of 
per-capita consumption 
in 
rural 
areas 
in 
the 
past 
decade 
seems 
to 
be 
associated 
with 
greater 
diversification. 
Available 
data 
show that 
there 
have 
been 
large 
shifts 
in 
sectors 
of activity 
such 
as 
services, 
factory 
workers 
and 
artisans, 
relative 
to 
agncultural 
laborers. 
Small 
cottage 
industries 
developed 
in 
rural 
areas, 
albeit 
mostly 
in 
villages 
with 
connection 
to 
electricity. The 
land 
rental 
market 
developed 
with 
tenancy 
rising 
from 
16 
percent 
of 
operated 
area 
in 
1983-84 
to 
22 
percent 
in 
1996. 
Fixed 
rent 
tenancy also 
rose 
in 
importance 
relative 
to 
sharecropping 
from 
26 percent 
of 
total 
area 
under 
tenancy 
to 
38 
percent 
in 
1996. 
14. 
Bangladesh 
has 
potential to 
accelerate 
growth 
in 
rural 
farm 
and 
non-farm 
sectors 
by 
realigning 
govemment 
policies 
to 
strongly 
encourage 
the 
rural 
non-farm 
sector, 
grooming 
institutions 
for 
provision 
of 
public, 
rather than 
private 
goods 
(including 
technology 
push), 
developing 
supporting infrastructure, 
and 
removing 
barriers 
for 
efficient functioning 
of 
markets 
(including 
protection 
to 
the 
sugar 
industry). 
In 
the 
environment 
of 
accelerated growth, 
equipping 
the 
poor 
with 
the 
private 
and 
public 
assets, 
as 
well 
as 
continued 
measures 
to 
reduce 
their 
vulnerability 
through 
targeted 
interventions, 
is 
the 
best 
way 
to 
raise 
their 
incomes and 
enable 
them 
to 
participate 
in 
the 
growth 
process. 
Improving 
the environment for 
rural 
non-farm 
growth 
15. 
The 
rural 
non-farm 
sector 
has 
the 
potential 
to 
become a 
leading 
sector 
of 
Bangladesh’s economy. 
However, 
its 
growth 
and 
employment-creating  opportunities  need 
to 
be 
nurtured 
carefully through 
a 
strong 
rural 
focus 
in 
the 
provision 
of 
public 
goods  (infrastructure, 
market  information) 
and 
credit 
delivery. 
Any 
“urban bias” 
caused 
by 
more 
favorable 
treatment accorded 
to 
urban 
industry 
needs 
to 
be 
removed. 
For 
example, 
tax 
holidays, 
enhanced depreciation, 
lower 
rates 
of 
capital 
gains 
tax, 
etc. 
are 
offered 
to 
manufacturing 
export-oriented 
industries 
located 
in 
the 
cities 
(e.g., 
garments 
and 
leather), 
but 
are 
not 
available 
to 
ago-processing 
industries. 
To 
boost 
the  agro-processing 
industry, 
tariffs 
on raw 
materials 
and 
inputs 
used 
by 
rural 
industry 
must 
be 
reduced 
further 
and 
supplementary 
duties 
withdrawn. 
Supporting the 
diversification 
in 
the 
farm 
sector 
16. 
The 
capacity 
of 
the 
rural 
non-farm 
sector 
to 
reduce 
poverty 
depends 
heavily 
on 
consumption 
and 
production 
linkages 
with 
agnculture, 
and 
hence 
upon 
strong 
agncultural 
growth.  The 
govemment 
needs 
to 
focus 
its 
policies 
on 
sustaining 
the  recent 
agricultural 
growth 
momentum 
through 
promotion 
of 
agncultural diversification. 
Reforming outdated 
institutions 
for 
rural 
development 
and 
building 
new 
ones 
17. 
Moving 
to 
commercialized 
and 
diversified 
agnculture 
requires 
a 
major 
shift 
in 
the 
role 
and 
functions 
of 
govemment 
while 
strengthening 
the 
base 
for 
deeper 
involvement 
of 
private 
entrepreneurs 
in 
rural 
areas. 
In 
this 
context, 
the 
govemment 
should 
abandon 
subsidizing 
fertilizer 
and 
sugar 
and 
intervening 
with 
agricultural 
prices, 
and 
undertake 
more 
functions 
as 
dissemination 
of 
information; 
Page  110
Annex 
G 
Page 
5 
of 
6 
overseeing 
the 
process 
of 
quality 
control 
(e.g., 
standardization and 
grades); 
and 
monitoring 
of 
farm 
input 
quality, 
(e.g., 
inspection, sampling, 
and 
testing 
of 
fertilizer 
and 
seeds). 
The 
govemment should 
also foster 
development 
of 
institutions 
to 
take 
up 
such 
roles 
as 
livestock 
disease 
surveillance  and 
control 
and 
development 
of 
veterinary 
services. 
18. 
Changing 
patterns 
of 
demand 
for 
food, 
degrading 
resources, 
the 
emergence 
of 
new 
areas 
of 
integrative 
research  (biotechnology, 
agro-forestry), 
and 
stagnant 
yields 
for  many 
crops  pose 
new 
challenges 
for 
the 
agricultural 
research 
and 
extension 
system. 
Recent 
growth 
in 
rice 
yield 
is 
largely 
due 
to 
reallocation 
of 
land 
from 
local to 
high 
yielding 
varieties 
rather 
than improvement 
in 
variety-specific 
yields. 
Sustaining 
recent 
agricultural 
growth would 
require 
addressing 
these 
new 
challenges, 
but 
the 
budgetary 
impact 
for 
the 
govemment 
will 
depend 
on 
the 
strategy adopted. 
Given 
the 
fledgling 
state 
of 
private 
sector research today, 
in 
the 
short and 
medium 
term 
the 
bulk 
of 
the 
needed research 
work 
will 
have 
to 
come 
from 
the 
public 
sector, 
and 
this 
will 
need 
stable, 
significant 
public 
financial 
support. 
Over 
the 
longer 
term, 
the 
budgetary 
impact 
could 
be 
reduced 
if 
the government 
encourages 
private 
sector 
entry 
and 
builds 
a  strong  partnership 
with 
it. 
Establishing  and 
enforcing  intellectual  property 
rights 
is 
important 
in 
this 
context 
to 
ensure 
inflow 
of 
private 
investments 
in 
such 
areas 
as 
seeds 
research, 
livestock 
and horticulture. 
19. 
It 
is 
also 
necessary 
that 
the 
Govemment-run 
Bangladesh 
Agricultural 
Development Corporation 
(BADC) 
gradually 
withdraw 
from 
supplying 
seeds 
at 
a 
highly 
subsidized 
price, a 
practice 
that constrains 
the  emergence 
of 
private 
sector 
initiatives. 
Phasing 
out 
of 
BADC 
and termination 
of 
a 
number 
of 
development projects 
will 
entail additional 
budgetary 
savings. 
As 
discussed 
above, 
upwards 
of 
2,800 
BADC 
employees have already been retrenched. 
20. 
Government allocations 
on 
research 
should 
increase, 
while 
the 
National Agricultural 
Research 
System 
should 
redirect 
its 
emphasis 
and 
intensify 
research 
efforts 
in 
non-rice 
crops 
and 
non-crop 
agrlcultural 
products. 
Inland 
culture 
fisheries development 
offers 
opportunities 
for 
income, 
employment, 
and 
foreign 
exchange 
generation. 
At 
the 
same 
time, 
the 
Govemment-led 
system 
of 
extension 
services 
would 
benefit 
from 
closer 
collaboration 
with 
NGOs 
and 
should 
be 
redirected 
toward 
provision, 
upon 
demand, 
of 
"know-how" 
on 
these 
higher 
value 
varieties, 
improved 
soil 
and 
farm 
water 
management 
practices, 
port-harvest 
handling 
and 
processing, 
fertilizer 
use 
and 
product 
standardization 
for 
export 
markets. 
2 
1. 
Importantly, 
rural 
institutions 
are 
critical 
in 
ensuring 
the inclusiveness 
of 
growth 
which 
requires 
not 
only 
that 
the 
poor 
build 
up 
their 
assets 
(especially 
health 
and 
education), 
but 
also 
that the 
return 
to 
these 
assets 
are enhanced. 
A 
critical 
factor 
in 
raising 
returns 
is 
better 
defined property 
rights 
and clearer 
regulations 
on 
common 
resource management. 
If 
poverty 
is 
to 
decline, 
it 
is 
also 
imperative 
that 
stronger 
enforcement  mechanisms are 
in 
place 
to 
guarantee 
those 
rights, 
which 
in 
turn 
calls 
for 
governance 
improvements. 
Developing and upgrading 
physical, 
financial 
and 
social infrastructure 
22. 
Sustained  development 
in 
rural 
areas 
is 
not 
possible 
without 
adequate 
infrastructure. 
Notwithstanding 
the 
improvements 
in 
access 
to 
electricity 
in 
recent 
years, 
rural 
electrification 
is 
abysmally 
low, 
with 
only 
one 
out 
of 
five 
rural 
households 
having 
access 
to 
electricity. 
Studies 
have 
shown 
that 
lack 
of 
access 
to 
electricity 
is 
a 
major 
obstacle 
to 
rural 
development, 
especially 
for 
agrlcultural 
diversification 
and 
RNF 
growth. 
Upgrading 
rural 
roads 
offer 
another avenue 
for rural 
development 
in 
Bangladesh. 
Rural 
roads 
are 
plentiful, 
but 
their 
integration 
into 
a 
coherent 
network 
as 
well 
as 
their 
maintenance 
require 
immediate 
attention. 
Improvements 
in 
the 
rural 
financial 
infrastructure 
will 
not 
be 
possible 
without 
removal 
of 
the 
handicaps 
in 
the 
entire 
banking 
system. 
Access 
to 
credit 
remains 
limited, 
while 
the 
two 
agricultural 
banks 
are 
saddled 
with 
the 
same 
kind 
of 
problems 
as 
other 
financial 
Page  111
Annex 
G 
Page 
6 
of 
6 
institutions, 
namely 
under-capitalization 
and 
non-performing 
loans. 
Large 
landowners 
are 
the 
principal 
beneficiaries 
of 
agncultural 
credit, 
which 
precludes 
access 
by 
small 
and 
medium 
size farmers, traders and 
organized 
processors. 
Continued 
support 
and 
encouragement 
of 
grass-root 
organizations and 
NGOs 
as 
providers 
of 
rural 
services, 
as 
well 
as 
introduction 
of 
new 
modes 
of 
social 
engagement 
(e.g. 
contract 
farming, 
access 
to 
local 
and 
international 
markets) 
will 
yield 
high 
dividends 
in 
terms 
of 
spreading 
the 
benefits 
of 
innovation 
and 
growth to 
wider 
segments 
of 
the 
rural 
population. 
Removing barriers 
to 
efjcient 
functioning 
of 
agricultural 
markets 
23. 
Given 
Bangladesh’s 
success 
following 
the 
liberalization 
of 
fertilizer  and 
irrigation 
equipment 
markets, 
removing 
the 
remaining 
market 
barriers 
could 
bring 
high 
benefits. 
The 
priorities 
in 
pursuit 
of 
this 
objective include: 
0 
Complete 
liberalization 
of 
the 
urea 
market, 
which 
is 
a 
more effective 
way 
of 
guaranteeing 
price 
stability. 
GOB’S 
policies, 
currently guided 
by 
fears 
of 
supply 
instability, 
are 
misplaced 
as 
the 
urea 
subsidy 
does 
not 
guarantee 
a 
stable 
supply 
if 
the 
world 
urea 
prices 
fluctuate; 
in 
addition 
the 
subsidy 
benefits 
disproportionately 
the 
non-poor 
(mostly 
the 
public 
sector 
industrial 
producers), 
and 
deteriorates 
soil 
fertility 
balance. 
Maintaining 
the 
gas 
subsidy 
to public 
sector 
urea factories 
prevents 
private 
sector 
entry 
in 
urea 
production. 
This 
entry, 
as 
well 
as 
private 
sector 
imports, 
is 
further 
blocked 
on 
account 
of 
the 
government’s 
maintaining an 
administered 
price 
lower 
than 
the 
factory 
production 
cost 
or 
import 
price. 
Phasing 
out remaining 
public 
interventions 
such 
as 
open-market 
sales 
and 
domestic 
procurement 
in 
the 
food 
grain 
market, 
notwithstanding 
the 
already 
low 
level 
of 
support 
for 
domestic 
food 
grain. 
Private imports 
are 
highly 
responsive 
to 
wholesale 
prices 
and 
thus 
to 
shortfalls 
in 
production, 
while 
price 
fluctuations 
are 
now 
dramatically 
reduced 
through 
use 
of 
new 
technologies  that 
allow 
production 
of 
food 
grains throughout 
the 
year. Government’s 
role 
in 
holding food 
grain 
stocks 
should 
be 
limited 
to 
the management 
of 
an 
emergency 
stock 
to 
insure 
against 
natural 
disasters 
(as 
opposed 
to 
procurement 
for 
price-stabilization 
purposes). 
Phasing 
out 
of 
current 
protection 
to 
the 
sugar 
industry 
by 
freeing 
up 
administrative 
procurement 
prices 
of 
sugar 
and  divestiture 
of 
sugar 
mills. 
Current  policies 
including 
the 
restrictive 
input 
procurement 
system 
result 
in 
high 
level 
of 
taxation 
of 
sugarcane 
farmers 
and 
indirect 
taxation 
raises 
consumer prices 
of 
sugar 
significantly.
