BANGLADESH: 
REFORMING 
FINANCIAL 
MANAGEMENT 
1. 
The 
findings 
of 
various 
diagnostic 
studiess2 
on 
Bangladesh 
reveal 
that 
fiduciary 
risk 
in 
public 
spending 
is 
high 
and 
significant 
improvement 
in 
public 
financial 
management 
is 
required. 
The 
Country Financial 
Accountability 
Assessment 
Report 
(CFAA), 
endorsed 
by 
the 
Government 
of 
Bangladesh 
(GOB), 
indicates 
that an inadequate 
control 
environment, 
lack 
of 
strategic 
planning, 
rigid 
and process 
oriented financial 
management 
systems, 
and 
lack 
of 
transparency 
and 
accountability 
in 
the management 
of 
the 
public 
purse 
are 
major 
impediments 
to 
sound 
public 
financial 
management. 
2. 
The 
following 
are some 
of 
the 
weaknesses 
identified 
by 
the 
analytical 
work 
that 
has 
been 
done: 
The 
internal 
control 
climate 
is 
poor, 
as 
pointed 
out 
repeatedly 
by 
the 
C&AG 
in 
his 
reports. 
Internal 
audit 
in 
Bangladesh 
is 
still 
at 
an 
early 
stage. 
The 
Secretary, 
as 
Pnncipal 
Accounting 
Officer, 
needs 
to 
ensure, 
with 
the 
support 
of 
the 
Chef 
Accounting 
Officer 
(CAO) 
and 
Internal 
Audit 
Officer, 
that 
procedures 
are 
clearly 
laid 
down, 
officers 
are 
adequately trained, records 
are 
properly 
managed 
and 
that 
officers 
are 
supervised 
and 
mentored 
as 
necessary. 
The 
CAO 
pre- 
audits 
all 
payments 
before they 
are 
made, 
except 
for 
some 
“departmentalized” 
agencies, 
such 
as 
Public 
Works, 
Roads 
and Highways, Telegraph 
and 
Telephone 
Board, 
which 
have 
check-issuing 
powers. 
There 
is 
no 
central 
direction 
or 
oversight 
of 
internal audit 
standards 
and management 
gets 
little 
technical 
advice 
on 
risk 
management 
and 
control. 
The upgrading 
of 
internal audit 
needs 
to 
be planned 
in 
conjunction 
with 
the 
strengthening 
of 
internal 
control. 
The 
government 
has 
indicated that 
it 
is 
committed 
to 
develop 
internal audit functions 
in 
the 
ministries 
and 
line 
agencies. 
Internal 
audit 
is 
being 
piloted 
in 
some 
ministries 
and 
will 
be 
expanded 
to 
10 
ministries 
by 
2005. 
Parliamentary 
Oversight 
is 
yet 
to 
be 
effective 
due 
to 
the 
absence 
of 
the 
Public 
Accounts 
Committee 
and 
other finance 
and 
standing 
committees. 
Though 
parliamentary 
elections 
were 
held 
in 
October 
200 
1 
, 
parliamentary 
standing committees 
have 
not 
yet 
been 
established, 
largely 
on 
account 
of 
differences 
with 
the 
opposition 
party 
on 
chairmanship 
of 
the committees. 
The 
Public 
Accounts Committee (PAC) 
is 
the 
most important parliamentary 
committee, 
as 
it 
oversees 
public 
expenditures  based 
on 
reports  submitted 
by 
the 
C&AG. 
However, 
although 
some 
improvements  were 
made 
in 
its 
operations 
during 
the 
late 
1990s, 
PAC 
has 
not 
been 
fully 
effective. 
It 
has 
no 
secretariat 
and 
its 
budget 
is 
inadequate 
to 
perform 
its 
mandate. 
While 
PAC 
can make 
recommendations, 
it 
does 
not 
have 
any 
mechanism 
for 
follow-up. 
If 
the 
concerned 
ministry 
chooses 
not 
to 
take 
corrective 
action, there 
is 
little 
that 
PAC 
can do 
about 
this. 
As 
part 
of 
the 
Financial 
Management 
Refom 
Program, 
Supported 
by 
UK-DFID 
and 
the 
Netherlands, 
the 
government 
has agreed 
to 
set-up 
Audit 
Committees 
in 
every 
ministry. 
Once 
established, 
the 
Audit 
Committees should 
review 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
recommendations 
of 
PAC 
and 
report 
back 
to 
PAC. 
The 
independence 
of 
the 
Comptroller 
and 
Auditor  General 
is 
limited. 
Although 
the 
constitution 
guarantees 
the independence 
of 
the 
Office 
of 
the 
Comptroller 
and 
Auditor 
General 
(C&AG), 
past 
C&AG’s 
have 
indicated 
that 
they 
face 
impediments 
in 
performing 
their 
constitutional 
mandate. 
The C&AG’s  report 
is 
first 
submitted 
to 
the 
Prime 
Minister’s 
Office, an 
auditee. 
This 
is 
inconsistent 
with 
the 
notion 
of 
an 
independent 
supreme 
audit 
and 
in 
contrast 
to 
the 
practice 
in 
countries 
with 
effective 
public 
audits, 
which 
submit 
their 
reports 
directly 
to 
parliament. 
The 
C&AG’s 
office 
is 
subject 
to 
the 
Rules 
of 
Business 
of 
the 
Government, 
which 
52 
Country 
Financial Accountability 
Assessment 
(CFAA), 
Public 
Expenditure Review 
(PER), 
Country 
Procurement 
Assessment 
Report (CPAR), 
Institutional 
and 
Govemance 
Review (IGR) 
and 
IMF’s 
Fiscal 
ROSC 
and 
Safeguard 
Assessment. 
Page  113
Annex 
H 
Page 
2 
of 
4 
means 
that 
it 
gets 
treated 
like 
a Department 
in 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance. 
The 
Ministry 
of 
Finance 
and 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Establishment  decide the 
budget 
and 
personnel 
policies 
of 
the 
C&AG’s 
office. 
Ideally 
it 
should 
be 
the 
Public 
Accounts 
Committee, 
which 
is 
the 
principal 
user 
of 
the 
C&AG’s 
services, 
that should 
determine 
the 
budget 
of 
the 
C&AG, 
although 
the 
budget 
allocation 
would 
be 
made 
by 
the 
Finance 
Division. 
To 
ensure 
accountability, 
the 
accounts 
of 
the 
C&AG 
office 
should 
be 
audited 
by 
an 
auditor  appointed 
by 
PAC. 
In 
addition, 
to 
improve 
the 
performance 
and 
quality 
of 
audit 
reports, there 
is 
need 
for 
peer 
review 
of 
the 
performance 
of 
the 
C&AG’s 
Office. 
. 
Cash 
management 
is 
not 
very 
effective 
and 
results 
is 
excessive 
short-term 
borrowing 
from 
Bangladesh 
Bank 
and 
commercial banks. 
One 
reason 
for 
this 
is 
that 
budget 
revenue 
projections 
in 
the 
past 
have 
been 
high. 
To 
address 
this, 
a suitably staffed 
Resource 
Management 
Unit 
is 
being 
set 
up 
in 
the 
Finance 
Division 
of 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance. 
The 
financial 
statements 
of 
GOB 
are 
not 
fully 
accurate 
and transparent. 
The 
accounts 
of 
GOB 
are 
maintained 
on 
a 
cash basis and 
are 
not 
always 
complete. 
Assets 
and 
liabilities 
are 
not 
appropriately recorded 
in 
the 
books 
of 
accounts.  There 
is 
no 
system 
of 
commitment 
accounting. 
Payments 
required 
on 
an emergency basis 
are 
often 
recorded 
in 
suspense 
accounts. 
It 
is 
not 
uncommon 
to 
find 
lack 
of 
reconciliation 
and 
misclassification 
of 
expenditures 
that 
lead 
to 
inaccurate 
financial 
data. 
The 
accounting 
records, 
in 
many 
cases, 
do 
not 
include  direct 
disbursements 
for 
goods 
and 
services 
for 
the 
development projects. 
There 
are 
major 
unexplained 
differences 
between 
the 
bank 
balance 
shown 
in 
the 
Government’s  balance 
sheet 
and 
the 
balance 
shown 
in 
the 
scrolls 
(bank  statements) 
of 
the 
Bangladesh 
Bank. 
Only 
regular 
monthly 
reconciliations 
can remove 
this 
risk. 
The 
government 
is 
aware 
of 
these 
problems  and 
this 
is 
another  component 
that 
is 
being 
tackled 
under 
the 
Financial 
Management 
Reform 
Program. 
Government 
has 
also 
agreed 
to 
implement 
the 
International 
Public 
Sector 
Accounting 
Standards, 
initially 
on 
a 
cash basis. 
. 
The 
financial 
accountability 
of 
local 
government 
institutions 
is 
very 
weak. 
Most 
local 
government budgets 
are 
not 
in 
the 
public 
domain. 
The 
accounts 
of 
local 
governments 
are 
not 
prepared 
on 
a 
timely 
basis 
and 
their 
audited 
financial 
statements are 
not 
made 
public. 
None 
of 
the 
local 
governments  prepare 
any  performance  reports 
on 
the 
use 
of 
public 
funds. 
As 
Government 
embarks 
upon 
a 
program 
of 
decentralization, 
it 
is 
very 
important 
to 
improve 
the 
financial 
accountability 
environment 
of 
local 
govemment. 
Such a 
phased 
program 
could 
begin 
with 
improving financial 
accountability 
in 
four 
city 
corporations 
(Dhaha, 
Chittagong, 
Khulna 
and 
Rajshahi) 
on 
a 
pilot 
basis. 
GOVERNMENT’S 
FINANCIAL 
MANAGEMENT 
REFORM 
STRATEGY 
3. 
In 
recent 
years, 
and 
particularly 
since  the 
election 
of 
the  present 
Government 
in 
October 
2001, 
Bangladesh 
has 
made considerable progress 
in 
fiscal and 
economic 
reforms. 
A 
major 
pillar 
of 
these 
reforms 
has 
been 
improvements 
in 
the 
management 
of 
the 
public 
purse. 
Achievements 
to date 
0 
The 
Financial 
Management 
Reform 
Program 
(FMRF’) 
adopted 
by 
Government 
is 
designed 
to 
deliver 
five 
components: 
(i) 
improved 
audit  and 
other 
financial 
management 
reports 
for 
parliamentary scrutiny; 
(ii) 
a 
sustainable 
regulatory 
environment 
to 
enhance 
fiscal 
management; 
(iii) 
enhanced 
resource 
allocation, 
utilization 
and  performance 
management; 
(iv) 
enhanced 
Page  114
Annex 
H 
Page 
3 
of 
4 
financial 
management 
systems; 
and 
(v) 
greater 
capacity 
for 
the 
Financial 
Management 
Academy 
to 
support 
financial 
management 
training. 
A 
new 
budgetary classification 
system 
was 
introduced 
in 
1999 
under a 
Reform 
Project 
on 
Budget 
and 
Expenditure 
(RIBEC) 
that 
helped 
in 
making 
budgets 
and accounts 
more 
useful, 
particularly 
for 
economic 
analysis. 
In 
order 
to 
restore 
fiscal 
and 
budgetary  discipline, 
the 
Government 
imposed 
an 
embargo 
on 
irregular 
transfers 
of 
funds 
to 
the 
Public Account 
of 
the 
Republic 
in 
FY02. 
Public 
accounts and 
audit reports 
are 
now 
presented 
to 
Parliament 
annually 
and 
delays have 
been 
substantially 
reduced. 
From 
July 
1, 
2002, offices 
of 
CAOs 
(Chief 
Accounts Officers) 
in 
each 
ministry 
were 
created 
to 
report 
directly 
to 
their 
respective 
Secretaries 
and 
the 
Controller 
General 
of 
Accounts (CGA) 
who 
ultimately 
reports 
to 
the 
Finance 
Secretary. 
In 
addition, 
internal audit 
teams 
have 
been 
piloted 
in 
a 
limited 
number 
of 
ministries. 
To 
assure 
quality 
of 
accounting 
data, 
a Central 
Reconciliation 
Unit 
(CRU) has been 
established 
in 
the 
CGA’s office.  The 
unit 
is 
now 
carrying out reconciliation 
of 
cheques 
electronically 
issued 
by 
the 
CAOs 
based 
in 
Dhaka 
and 
paid 
by 
the 
Bangladesh 
Bank. 
Field 
accounting 
offices 
have 
been 
reorganized 
through 
the 
abolition 
of 
20 
Regional 
Accounts 
Offices  and 
the 
creation 
of 
6 
upgraded 
Divisional 
Offices. 
Information 
generated 
at 
Thana 
and 
District 
levels 
is 
now 
monitored 
and  consolidated 
by 
these 
6 
Divisional 
Controllers 
and 
electronically 
transferred 
to 
the Central 
Data 
Processing 
Unit 
of 
the 
CGA, 
and 
from 
there 
to 
the 
Finance 
Division. 
On-line 
electronic 
data 
transmission 
now 
allows 
the 
central 
government 
accounts 
to 
be 
compiled 
and 
reported 
within 
five 
weeks 
of 
each 
month-end. 
The auditing 
and 
accounting 
function 
are 
in 
the process 
of 
being 
separated. 
The 
CGA 
no 
longer 
reports 
to 
the 
Auditor 
General (C&AG).

Major 
Reforms 
Planned 
During 
2004-06 
Government 
has 
agreed 
to 
establish 
an 
Independent 
Anti 
Corruption Commission 
and 
is 
in 
the 
process 
of 
preparing 
the necessary 
legislation. 
The 
Government 
has agreed 
that 
it 
will 
move 
to 
adopt 
International 
Accounting 
Standards. 
It 
will 
be 
necessary 
to 
prepare 
a 
plan 
for 
transition 
to 
full 
adoption 
of 
the 
Public 
Sector 
International 
Accounting 
Standards 
(cash 
basis 
as 
a 
first 
step). 
All 
ministries 
will 
be 
required 
to 
publish 
annual performance reports 
and 
submit 
these 
to 
their 
respective 
Parliamentary Committees 
for 
review. 
Government’s 
Financial 
Statements 
will 
be 
made 
more 
comprehensive 
and 
will 
include foreign 
aid, 
public 
debt, 
all 
bank 
account 
balances, 
and 
all 
liabilities. 
Internal 
audit 
teams, 
reporting 
to 
the 
immediate 
business manager, 
will 
be 
established 
in 
at least 
10 
additional 
ministries. 
A 
comprehensive 
manual and 
training 
plan 
will 
be 
developed 
in 
conjunction 
with 
the 
C&AG. 
Audit 
Committees 
will 
be 
established 
in 
all 
ministries. 
Full 
legal 
separation 
of 
audit 
from 
accounts 
will 
be 
affected, 
including 
the 
C&AG 
reporting 
to 
the 
Parliament 
with 
the 
PAC 
determining 
the 
budget, 
personnel 
and 
selection 
of 
the 
C&AG. 
A 
strategic 
plan 
for 
improvements 
to 
the 
C&AG’s 
office 
will 
be 
prepared.
